Every byte has a provenance — a record of who issued it, when, against what (if anything), and with what authorization. The provenance is what makes the byte trustable: a recipient can verify that the byte came from a legitimate issuer without trusting the sender.

**Ownership chain** (the sequence of signature-bound transfers from the original recipient to the current owner) is a property of the byte's history. Every byte has an ownership chain, regardless of cargo. The chain is what makes the byte's ownership claims falsifiable: any party can verify the chain back to the byte's origin and confirm the current owner is legitimate.

**Joule cost** (the two-part `measured + estimated` energy accounting accumulated over the byte's lifetime) is a property of the byte's operational cost. Every byte has a joule cost, regardless of cargo. The cost is what makes the substrate's economics legible: any party can compare the substrate's actual resource consumption to the rents charged by alternative settlement mechanisms.

The cargo itself:

**Cargo** is whatever the byte represents in the higher-layer economy. Cargo types are defined by application protocols, not by the substrate. The substrate's only requirement is that cargo be serializable to bytes and that the cargo type be tagged so applications can interpret it. The substrate validates the carrier properties (signature, history, joule cost) and propagates the cargo unchanged. Whether the cargo means $50 USD, 10 kWh, a vote on proposition 7, an attestation that the holder is over 21, a temperature reading from a specific sensor, or a claim on tomorrow's wheat harvest is application-layer concern.

This separation is what makes the substrate general-purpose. The matching, clearing, and trust-signaling functions identified in §3 are *all* about the carrier, not about the cargo. The institutional middle gets paid for moving things; the things being moved are incidental to the rents charged. Once the carrier is provided as a substrate primitive, the rent on moving any specific cargo type collapses to the actual cost of moving — which is the joule cost of the substrate's machinery, plus whatever rent the application layer can defend against substrate-native alternatives.

### 5.4 Concrete cargo types the substrate handles

To make the abstraction concrete, here are cargo types the substrate handles cleanly, organized by category. Each is a real use case with a corresponding institutional-middle rent that the substrate's generalization to that cargo type collapses.

**Money-equivalent cargo**:
- USD bytes issued by GENIUS-licensed stablecoin issuers. Cargo is "$X USD redeemable from issuer Y." Substitutes for: bank deposits, stablecoin balances, payment-processor float.
- Joule bytes issued by power producers (community solar arrays, utility cooperatives, individual rooftop systems). Cargo is "X joules of electrical energy redeemable from producer Y." Substitutes for: kWh meter readings, time-of-use billing, distributed energy resource accounting.
- Other-currency bytes (EUR, JPY, CNY, regional currencies) issued by appropriately-regulated entities in their jurisdictions. Substitutes for: foreign exchange clearing, cross-border wire transfers, multi-currency bank accounts.
- Commodity bytes (ounces of gold, bushels of wheat, barrels of oil) issued by commodity custodians. Substitutes for: futures contract settlement, ETF share issuance, commodity warehouse receipts.

**Compute and infrastructure cargo**:
- Compute-hour bytes issued by computing clusters (community-owned, commercial, hybrid). Cargo is "X CPU-hours or GPU-hours redeemable from cluster Y." Substitutes for: cloud computing reservations, datacenter capacity contracts, compute time markets.
- Storage bytes issued by storage operators. Cargo is "X gigabyte-months of storage at provider Y." Substitutes for: cloud storage subscriptions, content-delivery contracts, archival service agreements.
- Bandwidth bytes issued by network operators. Substitutes for: peering agreements, transit contracts, last-mile data plans.

**Physical-good cargo**:
- Food bytes issued by community greenhouses, vertical farms, food cooperatives. Cargo is "X kilocalories of food redeemable from producer Y." Substitutes for: food cooperative membership, CSA subscriptions, food bank operations.
- Water bytes issued by atmospheric water generation systems, municipal water authorities, irrigation districts. Substitutes for: water bills, irrigation contracts, mutual water company shares.
- Fabrication-time bytes issued by community fabrication facilities, makerspaces, distributed manufacturing networks. Substitutes for: hourly rental of equipment, contract manufacturing agreements, material-and-labor pricing.

**Social and governance cargo**:
- Governance vote bytes issued by community governance systems (cooperative assemblies, DAO equivalents, neighborhood associations). Cargo is "one vote on proposition X by entity Y." Substitutes for: paper ballots, online voting platforms, board governance procedures.
- Attestation bytes (KYC attestations, age attestations, professional credential attestations, sanctions-status attestations) issued by attestation authorities. Cargo is "verifiable claim X about holder Y, issued by attestor Z." Substitutes for: identity verification services, background check clearinghouses, professional licensing databases.
- Reputation bytes (in-context endorsements, reviews, performance attestations) issued by individuals, communities, or platforms. Substitutes for: review-platform monopolies, credit-scoring oligopolies, professional reference networks.

**Real-world-data cargo**:
- Sensor reading bytes (temperature, pressure, location, environmental conditions, biometrics) issued by sensor operators. Cargo is "measurement X by sensor Y at time Z, with cryptographic attestation that the sensor reported it." Substitutes for: data broker oligopolies, IoT platform lock-in, scientific data exchange friction.
- Event attestation bytes (a meeting happened, a delivery occurred, a milestone was reached, a contract condition was met) issued by event witnesses. Substitutes for: notarization, escrow services, contract milestone verification.

**Claim cargo**:
- Output-claim bytes (a claim on X% of the output of farm Y, factory Y, or fund Y for a specified period) issued by the producing entity. Cargo is "fractional claim on production." Substitutes for: equity shares, royalty contracts, output-sharing agreements.
- Service-claim bytes (a claim on X hours of service from provider Y) issued by service providers. Substitutes for: gift certificates, prepaid service contracts, retainer agreements.
- Conditional claim bytes (a claim on X conditional on event Y occurring before time Z) issued by claim originators. Substitutes for: futures contracts, options, conditional escrow.

**Categories the substrate subsumes whole.** Several systems being built in 2026 as standalone, separately-capitalized products are, in substrate terms, thin specializations of the smart byte — they re-implement the carrier, usually badly, because they did not start from the carrier:

- *Data unions / "DataDAOs."* A pooled dataset whose value is co-owned by its contributors is a smart byte whose cargo is the dataset (or a content-addressed pointer to it) and whose ownership chain has many co-signers, with output-claim bytes (above) issued against the pool's licensing revenue in proportion to contribution. The "union" mechanics — pooling, governance, revenue-share, license-and-distribute — are ownership-chain and output-claim semantics the substrate already provides. The standalone versions add a protocol-level token, which is the substrate's prohibited sixth field (§8.7) wearing a different hat; the token is the part that fails (one prominent data-union network ran its treasury down to roughly $232K in early 2026), not the union pattern itself, and not the underlying observation that individually-worthless data, pooled and governed, has leverage.
- *Verifiable credentials.* The attestation byte above *is* the W3C Verifiable Credentials data model and the credential carried in an EU eIDAS-2 digital-identity wallet — an issuer signs a claim about a holder, the holder holds it, a verifier checks the issuer's signature against a known key. No chain, no token, no "blockchain diploma" — just the carrier's provenance field carrying a claim instead of a dollar value. The credential networks that actually won this space (Credly-scale professional-badge utilities) use no blockchain at all; the substrate subsumes them by making the credential a cargo type rather than a product, and interoperates with the W3C and eIDAS-2 standards rather than reinventing them.
- *Programmable-IP licensing.* "Register a work, attach machine-readable license terms, track derivatives, collect royalties" is a conditional-claim byte (the license: a claim on payment conditional on a qualifying use occurring) composed with output-claim bytes (the royalty stream) and the byte history's derivation pointer (which work a byte was minted "against" — the same provenance field the substrate already records). The standalone L1s built for this added a token and a bespoke consensus; the substrate provides the licensing and derivative-tracking semantics at the carrier layer, with settlement on its own lockstep rather than on a separate chain. (Basis licensing for composed-media bases — the federated-registry case — is exactly this pattern; it is specified where the registry is.)

The pattern is uniform: a 2026 startup picks one cargo type, wraps it in a token and a bespoke consensus, raises on the narrative, and the token — not the use case — is what collapses. The substrate's contribution is to provide the carrier once, correctly, ownerless, so that all of these compose on top without re-litigating consensus, identity, settlement, or provenance each time.

This list is not exhaustive. It is illustrative. The point is that the substrate's machinery — content-addressed identity, signature-bound transitions, deterministic lockstep replication, two-part joule cost — is correct for all of these cargo types, and the rents currently extracted by the institutional middle on each of them collapse when the substrate provides the carrier directly. The institutional middle's framing has bundled all of these into either "financial services" (with money-business regulation) or "data services" (with information-broker rent extraction) or "physical commerce" (with custodial rent extraction). The substrate denies the bundling. Each cargo type is the same architectural primitive carrying different cargo, and the rent on the carrier collapses uniformly.

### 5.5 The architectural consequence

Once the vesicle reframing is in hand, the substrate's design follows mechanically. The substrate must specify the carrier properties precisely (Part II of this treatise) and stay agnostic about cargo. Application protocols are layer-2 work; the substrate provides the layer-1 carrier and lets ten thousand layer-2 protocols compose on top.

This is the architectural pattern that worked for the internet. TCP/IP is a vesicle. It carries HTTP, SMTP, FTP, DNS, BGP, and ten thousand other application protocols, none of which are privileged at the IP level. The IP layer cares about packet headers, routing, addressing, fragmentation, and reassembly. The IP layer does not care what the packets contain. The astonishing generality of the internet — that the same wires and the same routers carry email, video, banking, gaming, telemetry, and conversation — is the consequence of TCP/IP's vesicle behavior. A money-only IP, an email-only IP, or a video-only IP would each have produced a network with a fraction of the actual internet's value. The substrate's design follows the same pattern: a money-only substrate, a stablecoin-only substrate, or a payments-only substrate would each produce a system with a fraction of the smart byte substrate's value.

The TCP/IP analogy also clarifies what the substrate is replacing. The institutional middle of money is not analogous to TCP/IP. The institutional middle is analogous to AOL — a vertically integrated system that bundled connectivity, identity, applications, and content, charged rent on each, and was rendered obsolete by an open, application-agnostic alternative that performed the connectivity function as a public good. The substrate is to the institutional middle of money what TCP/IP was to AOL. The transition is the same shape, the same direction, and the same beneficiaries: the application layer and the end users, at the expense of the integrated incumbent.

The vesicle reframing is the conceptual move that makes the architectural decisions defensible. Without it, every design question becomes "but what about the money?" — which currency, which monetary policy, which fee structure, which inflation schedule. With the reframing, the design questions become the right ones: what does the carrier need to do, what cargo types should it support, what does the layer-2 application landscape look like, what rents collapse and what rents survive. §8 onward specifies the carrier's design. §6 states the freedoms the carrier provides precisely. §7 closes Part I with the condensed thesis. The substantive case for the substrate's necessity is complete by §7. The remaining six parts specify how the substrate works, what it survives, and what it predicts.

The smart byte is a vesicle. The cargo is application-defined. The institutional middle's rents on byte-denominated commerce, of every kind, collapse when the carrier is provided as a substrate primitive. This is the reframing. The rest of the treatise specifies what falls out of it.
## §6. The freedom case, precisely stated

The substrate provides specific freedoms at specific costs under a specific threat model. This section names each freedom, names what each freedom requires giving up to obtain, and names the freedoms the substrate does not provide. The discipline is non-negotiable: a treatise that overclaims freedoms loses credibility on contact with anyone who reads carefully, and the substrate's argument is strong enough not to need overclaiming. Where the substrate's freedoms are partial, the treatise says so. Where they depend on conditions the substrate does not control, the treatise says so. Where they are absent, the treatise says so.

The argument structure: §6.1 names four freedoms the substrate provides. §6.2 names the costs of obtaining those freedoms. §6.3 names freedoms the substrate does not provide. §6.4 names freedoms the substrate makes possible-but-not-guaranteed, where humans must build the political and institutional structures that translate technical affordance into lived freedom. §6.5 closes by stating what the substrate's freedom claim actually is, in one sentence, in a form precise enough that any reader can hold the claim against any later section of the treatise.

### 6.1 Four freedoms the substrate provides

**Freedom from rent extraction on cognitive operations that no longer require institutional performance.** Every dollar currently extracted by the institutional middle for matching, clearing, or trust-signaling work that the substrate performs at marginal cost approaching zero is a dollar the substrate frees. §3.4 estimated this at $2-3T per year globally, the difference between the institutional middle's $3-5T total extraction and the $1-2T legitimate residual (maturity transformation, risk pooling, physical infrastructure, regulatory liaison). The freedom is partial: the rent layer does not collapse instantly on substrate adoption; it compresses gradually as adoption crosses thresholds, transactions migrate, and the institutional middle's pricing power decays. The freedom is also asymmetric across cargo types: a USD-byte transfer between regulated counterparties pays compliance rent to a layer-2 issuer; a joule-byte transfer between cooperative members pays nothing. The freedom is greatest where the regulatory bundling is least, and the substrate makes the unbundling possible.

**Freedom from institutional capture of receipts.** Every transaction in the current system produces a receipt — a ledger entry, a wire confirmation, a credit card statement, a clearing report — and every receipt is owned by an institution that can withhold it, alter it, or surveil it. Receipts in the substrate are content-addressed and held by the parties to the transaction. No institution can withhold a receipt from a user who participated in the transaction. No institution can alter a receipt without producing a hash divergence detectable by any honest party. No institution can prevent a user from independently verifying their own transaction history against the substrate's content-addressed chain. This is a structural freedom, not a policy choice: it falls out of the architecture, and removing it would require redesigning the substrate.

**Freedom from forced reliance on intermediaries.** Every transaction in the current system requires at least one institutional intermediary — a bank, a card network, a clearing house, a custodian — and the intermediaries can refuse service. Account closures, debanking, payment-processor takedowns, sanctions-list enforcement (whether the sanction is legitimate or the result of error or political pressure) all operate on the substrate of intermediaries' service obligations. The substrate replaces the institutional intermediary with cryptographic primitives plus issuer relationships that the user can choose. A user transacting in joule-bytes from a community solar cooperative depends on the cooperative's continued operation but not on any bank, card network, or clearing house. A user transacting in USD-bytes from a GENIUS-licensed issuer depends on the issuer but can switch issuers if the issuer behaves badly, in a way that switching banks does not actually deliver under current conditions. The substrate makes intermediary relationships chooseable rather than forced.

**Freedom from the illegibility-of-cost protection that has shielded rent layers for centuries.** Every transaction's energy cost is intrinsically reported in the substrate's two-part `JouleCost` schema. Any party can compare the substrate's actual operational cost to the rents charged by alternative settlement mechanisms. The Visa interchange fee, the SWIFT wire charge, the bank deposit-to-loan spread, the foreign exchange spread — each is a number the institutional middle has been free to set arbitrarily because users had no honest baseline against which to compare it. The substrate provides the baseline. Rent layers can still exist on top of the substrate, charging whatever the market will bear, but they have to charge against an honest cost number. This is not an automatic freedom; users must look at the cost numbers and compare them to the rents. The substrate provides the data; humans must use it.

These four freedoms are concrete, structural, and verifiable. Each follows from the substrate's architecture rather than from any institutional promise. Each is robust against any institutional middle's attempt to subvert it from above. Each is also bounded — partial in scope, dependent on adoption, contingent on humans using the affordances the substrate provides.

### 6.2 What obtaining these freedoms costs

The four freedoms are not free. The substrate exchanges specific costs for them, and the treatise's discipline requires naming the costs precisely.

**Cost: every byte's history is intrinsically public.** The substrate's content-addressed history makes byte provenance verifiable by any party. This is the property that delivers the second freedom (no institutional withholding of receipts) and the fourth freedom (cost legibility). It is also the property that means a user's full transaction history in any specific byte class is observable to any party who can identify the user's pubkey. The substrate is not anonymous in the way physical cash is anonymous. It is pseudonymous in the way Bitcoin is pseudonymous, with the same caveats: a sufficiently determined adversary with enough auxiliary information can deanonymize most users.

The substrate's response to this cost has three parts. First, the byte history is public *to anyone with the byte's identity*, not to the world automatically; finding all the bytes belonging to a specific human requires linking their pubkeys, which is a layer-2 problem solved by the user's own operational discipline. Second, the substrate supports per-cargo-type privacy strategies (separate pubkeys per relationship, mixing services for fungible cargo, zero-knowledge proofs for cargo where they are appropriate), all as layer-2 features that compose with the substrate's transparent layer-1. Third, the privacy properties of the substrate are weaker than those of physical cash and stronger than those of bank-mediated electronic transactions, which means the substrate is a privacy improvement for the substantial majority of current digital transactions and a privacy regression for the small minority of cash-equivalent transactions.

This is an honest tradeoff and the treatise should not pretend otherwise. Privacy advocates who require strong anonymity for principled reasons will find the substrate's transparency unacceptable for some use cases, and they will be correct to find it so. The substrate's claim is not that it solves privacy; it is that it provides four specific freedoms and the privacy cost is what those freedoms cost.

**Cost: operators can still be coerced individually.** The substrate's defenses against state-level pressure (§27 in Part III on physical attacks, §11.2 on cluster admission distributed across jurisdictions) are layered and partial. Cluster operators distributed across jurisdictions are robust against any single state's pressure and degraded but functional against most state-coalitions; they fail under unanimous state-coalition pressure if the substrate has not yet acquired enough independent constituency to operate underground. The freedom from forced reliance on intermediaries does not extend to freedom from state action against the substrate's operators. A user whose only access to the substrate goes through an operator in a hostile jurisdiction can be cut off if that operator is coerced or shut down.

The substrate's response is operational diversity (multiple operators per cluster, gossip-based bootstrap, eventual underground-mode operation if necessary), but each of these is a partial defense, and the treatise is honest that no architecture can fully defend against unanimous state-coalition pressure. The substrate raises the cost of suppression substantially, but it does not eliminate the possibility of suppression. Users who require absolute freedom from state action will find the substrate insufficient for their threat model, and they will be correct.

**Cost: the legal-political fight over substrate adoption is real and ongoing.** Adopting the substrate requires acting against the institutional middle's interests, against incumbent regulatory frameworks designed for the institutional middle, and (in many jurisdictions) against state preferences for monetary control. The fight is not a one-time event; it is a multi-year process in which substrate adoption competes against the institutional middle's own AI-augmented productivity gains (the alternative scenario described in §1 and developed in §28, §36, §43). The substrate's freedoms are available to users only to the extent that the substrate exists at the moment they want to use it. The substrate's existence depends on the political-commercial-adoption fight being won by the substrate's adopters.

The substrate's response is the treatise itself, plus the reference implementation, plus whatever subsequent work produces a critical mass of adoption. The treatise is honest: the freedoms are conditional on the substrate winning, and the substrate winning is not guaranteed.

**Cost: users must perform some work that institutions previously performed.** The institutional middle currently absorbs operational complexity on behalf of users — key management, transaction monitoring, fraud detection, dispute resolution. The substrate moves some of this work back to users, with cryptographic tools that make the work tractable but do not make it disappear. A user who loses a private key loses the bytes controlled by it, in a way that a user who loses a bank password does not lose their bank balance. A user who is defrauded into authorizing a transfer cannot reverse it the way a credit-card user can dispute a charge. The substrate provides primitives for layer-2 services that handle these problems (custodial wallets, dispute-resolution services, social-recovery key management), but the user must choose those services and pay their costs. The freedoms come with operational responsibilities, and users who do not want the responsibilities must pay layer-2 services to handle them.

This cost is the smallest of the four because the layer-2 services emerge naturally and the cost they charge is bounded by competition. It is named here for completeness: the substrate is not a magic system that gives users freedoms without obligations.

### 6.3 Freedoms the substrate does not provide

The discipline of precision requires naming what the substrate is not. Each absence is a concrete claim that some prior or contemporary advocacy might have led readers to expect; the treatise denies the expectation specifically.

**The substrate does not provide privacy at the byte level.** As §6.2 explained, byte histories are content-addressed and verifiable by any party with the byte's identity. Layer-2 privacy techniques exist and the substrate composes with them, but the substrate itself is transparent. A user who wants privacy must use the layer-2 tools, accept the costs of those tools, and understand that the layer-2 privacy is weaker than the layer-1 privacy of cash.

**The substrate does not provide freedom from regulation entirely.** Any cargo type subject to regulation (USD-byte issuance, securities-classified bytes, sanctions-screened transfers) remains subject to regulation when carried in the substrate. The substrate makes the regulatory cost proportional and unbundled rather than bundled and uniform; it does not eliminate the regulatory cost. Users transacting in regulated cargo pay regulatory costs to the issuers and compliance services that handle the regulation. Users transacting in unregulated cargo pay nothing for regulation they do not need. The substrate's contribution is to make this distinction operational; the substrate does not abolish regulation.

**The substrate does not provide automatic prosperity.** The institutional middle's rent extraction is a redistribution from users (and, by passthrough pricing, consumers and merchants) to the institutional middle. Substrate adoption returns the rent stream to users. The total economic activity does not increase by the rent amount; it redistributes. Whether the redistributed value produces broader prosperity depends on what users do with the freed capital — invest, consume, save, donate, build commons, fund public goods. The substrate enables the redistribution; humans must direct the freed capital toward outcomes that count as prosperity. §17 in the v0.2 full-map document and Part VI of this treatise engage this distinction at length.

**The substrate does not provide protection against the user's own bad decisions.** A user who authorizes a fraudulent transfer, who falls for a phishing attack, who shares their private key, who chooses an untrustworthy issuer, who fails to back up their key — all of these produce losses the substrate cannot prevent. Layer-2 services (custodial wallets with fraud monitoring, social-recovery key systems, insured custodial accounts) reduce these losses, but they reintroduce some of the institutional middle's structure. Users who want absolute protection from their own mistakes will find the substrate's bare-cryptography layer insufficient; users who want absolute self-sovereignty will find the layer-2 insurance services unappealing. Both are valid choices, and the substrate accommodates both.

**The substrate does not provide a guarantee of its own success.** The substrate may fail. The institutional middle may consolidate into an AI-mediated rent layer faster than the substrate ships. State coalitions may suppress the substrate before it acquires independent constituency. The substrate's reference implementation may have bugs that compromise its security. The substrate's economic case may be wrong in some way the treatise has not anticipated. Each of these is a real possibility and the treatise does not pretend otherwise. The freedoms the substrate provides are conditional on the substrate working as designed; the substrate's working as designed is conditional on engineering, adoption, and political work that has not yet been completed.

These are not weaknesses to be hidden. They are the boundaries of the substrate's claim. A reader who finishes §6.3 and concludes "the substrate does less than I thought" is reading correctly. The substrate does specifically the four things named in §6.1. It does not do the things named in §6.3. The treatise is precise about the boundary because the precision is what makes the four freedoms credible.

### 6.4 Freedoms the substrate makes possible but does not guarantee

The substrate has architectural affordances that, if humans build on top of them, enable freedoms that the substrate alone cannot deliver. These are taken up at length in Part VI of the treatise and were named in v0.2 §17.5 of the full-map document. Three are worth naming briefly here.

**Non-state redistribution becomes technically buildable.** A community solar cooperative can issue joule-bytes to its members proportionally to their cooperative shares. A community compute commons can issue compute-hour-bytes. A community food cooperative can issue kcal-bytes. The substrate provides the bookkeeping primitive that lets these distributions happen without an institutional intermediary's overhead. Whether communities choose to build commons of this kind, and whether the commons grow large enough to address the distribution problem taken up in Part VI, is a political-organizational question outside the substrate's scope. The substrate makes the option available; humans must take it.

**Joule-denominated commons changes the political economy of distribution.** Money-denominated redistribution is adversarial by construction (taxing one party to fund another). Joule-denominated commons distribution is non-rivalrous up to the system's capacity (the joules from a solar array exist whether or not anyone uses them; sharing them does not extract from anyone). The substrate enables joule-denominated commons; whether they replace any meaningful share of money-denominated redistribution depends on adoption and political will.

**Ownership measurement as byproduct of substrate operation.** Every productive byte's ownership is cryptographically explicit. Aggregating across substrate-native production produces an honest map of who owns what fraction of substrate-native productive capacity in any region or sector. Movements organized around redistribution of productive ownership now have, for the first time, a reliable real-time map of substrate-native ownership distribution. Whether the map is used to inform political action depends on whether such movements exist and whether they choose to use the data. The map is a byproduct of substrate operation; humans must read it and act on it.

These three affordances are the substrate's positive contribution to the distribution problem. None of them is a freedom the substrate delivers automatically. Each is a tool the substrate provides that humans must wield to convert technical affordance into lived freedom. The treatise's claim is honest: the substrate makes these things buildable in a way that prior architectures did not, and what humans build with the affordances is on humans, not on the substrate.

### 6.5 The freedom claim, in one sentence

The substrate provides freedom from rent extraction on cognitive operations that no longer require institutional performance, freedom from institutional capture of receipts, freedom from forced reliance on intermediaries, and freedom from the illegibility-of-cost protection that has shielded rent layers for centuries — at the costs of intrinsic byte-level transparency, partial defense against state-level coercion, ongoing political contestation, and the operational responsibilities that the institutional middle has absorbed on users' behalf — under the threat model that adversaries (states, AI companies, hostile capital, and unforeseen attacks) will read this treatise and design their attacks accordingly, with defenses specified in Part III; and the substrate makes possible, but does not guarantee, the further freedoms of non-state redistribution, joule-denominated commons, and substrate-native ownership measurement, which depend on humans choosing to build the political and institutional structures that convert architectural affordance into lived outcome.

The sentence is long by design. Each clause is load-bearing. Removing any clause produces a less precise version of the claim. The treatise's freedom claim is exactly this and exactly nothing more. Readers who hold this sentence against any later section of the treatise will find the treatise consistent with it; readers who find an inconsistency are invited to identify it as a possible error, and the errata page (Appendix A) will document any errors that survive review.

The substrate is for freedom, in the precise sense named here. It is not for utopia, and it is not for any specific political program. It is for the freedoms named in §6.1, at the costs named in §6.2, with the absences named in §6.3, and the affordances named in §6.4. The next section (§7) closes Part I with the condensed substantive thesis. The thesis follows from §2 (what money is), §3 (what the institutional middle does), §4 (what changed), §5 (the vesicle reframing), and §6 (the freedom case precisely). By the close of §7, Part I is complete.
## §7. The thesis, condensed

The substantive case for the smart byte substrate is the following.

Money is a workaround for limited cognition (§2). Specifically, it is a portable, fungible, divisible, durable token that lets parties who cannot remember each other's full transaction history settle exchanges anyway. Each property substitutes for a cognitive operation — spatial memory, identity tracking, size negotiation, temporal memory — that humans operating at human scale could not perform directly. Money worked, for centuries, because the cognitive limits it worked around were binding.

The institutional middle of money — banks, payment processors, custodians, exchanges, clearing houses, compliance bureaucracies — performs three functions on top of money: matching counterparties, clearing transactions, and signaling trust (§3). Each function had a real cost in pre-cryptographic, pre-cheap-computation, pre-energy-measurement conditions. The institutional middle was paid in rough proportion to those costs, and the rents extracted — somewhere between three and five trillion dollars per year globally, of which the McKinsey-sourced payments component is $2.5 trillion — reflected the difficulty of performing the functions in their original technological context.

Three independent technological maturations have changed the context (§4). Cheap cryptography (ed25519, blake3, content-addressed storage) crossed the threshold around 2020 and made the trust-signaling function performable at marginal cost approaching zero for the cryptographic-identity layer. Ubiquitous deterministic computation (lockstep simulation libraries, fixed-point arithmetic discipline, WebAssembly) crossed around 2020 and made the clearing function performable in milliseconds at the energetic cost of running the simulation. Per-operation energy measurement (RAPL, ARM equivalents, Apple Silicon counters) crossed around 2018 and made the rent-extraction layer falsifiable for the first time by exposing the actual cost of computation against the rents charged on top of it.

The three maturations compose into a single architectural primitive: a system that lets any party perform a cryptographically-authorized operation on a content-addressed data structure, have the operation deterministically replicated across a cluster of independent verifiers, and have the energy cost of the operation honestly reported in microjoules. The smart byte (§8) is the load-bearing object the substrate uses; it is content-addressed by hash, transferred by signature, replicated by lockstep, and priced in joules.

The smart byte is not money. It is a vesicle (§5) — a carrier defined by transport behavior, agnostic about cargo. A USD-denominated byte and a joule-denominated byte are the same primitive carrying different cargo. The substrate is general over cargo types: USD bytes, foreign currency bytes, commodity bytes, compute-hour bytes, storage bytes, food bytes, water bytes, fabrication-time bytes, governance vote bytes, attestation bytes, sensor reading bytes, output-claim bytes, conditional claim bytes. Money is one cargo type among many, and the institutional middle's three functions — matching, clearing, trust signaling — collapse uniformly across all cargo types when the carrier is provided as a substrate primitive.

The substrate provides four specific freedoms (§6) at four specific costs, with three named absences and three further freedoms it makes possible but does not guarantee. The freedoms: from rent extraction on cognitive operations that no longer require institutional performance; from institutional capture of receipts; from forced reliance on intermediaries; from the illegibility-of-cost protection that has shielded rent layers for centuries. The costs: every byte's history is intrinsically public; operators can still be coerced individually; the legal-political fight over substrate adoption is real and ongoing; users must perform some operational work that institutions previously performed. The absences: no byte-level privacy, no freedom from regulation entirely, no automatic prosperity, no protection from the user's own bad decisions, no guarantee of the substrate's own success. The conditional further freedoms: non-state redistribution, joule-denominated commons, ownership-measurement-as-byproduct of substrate operation. The substrate enables these; humans must build on the affordances to deliver them.

The substrate's claim to inevitability follows from the composition's mechanical character. Given that the three maturations have happened, given that the institutional middle's rents are large enough to make the composition profitable, given that the techniques are well-understood and the libraries are off-the-shelf, the substrate will be built. The question is by whom and on what terms. The two stable trajectories are:

**Trajectory one: the substrate ships under permissive licensing, with multiple independent implementations, with no protocol-level token, with no steward acquired by or made exclusive to any single dominant AI lab or capital bloc, with reference attribution to the public-good open-source projects it composes, and adoption crosses the threshold at which the institutional middle's bundled rent extraction becomes economically untenable.** The institutional middle compresses to its legitimate residual functions (maturity transformation, risk pooling, physical infrastructure, regulatory liaison), which represent perhaps 20-40% of its current revenue. The freed capital flows to substrate-native applications, commons-based distribution mechanisms, and whatever else the substrate's users build on top of the layer-1 carrier. This trajectory produces broadly distributed economic gains and structurally limits the formation of new rent-extraction layers.

The "no steward captured" clause is not decoration; it names the specific way trajectory one is lost without anyone noticing it happen. A steward that publishes the spec, ships the reference implementation, and operates commercial services around the substrate is doing exactly the right job — *as long as it remains one supplier among several and one customer's vendor among many*. The moment that steward is acquired by, or becomes the exclusive data / compute / settlement supplier to, a single dominant AI lab or capital bloc, its neutrality is the price of the deal, and the substrate's other consumers will route around it: the entire value of an open carrier is that no party who builds on it can be cut off by the carrier's owner, and a captured steward is a carrier that has acquired an owner. This dynamic has already been demonstrated in an adjacent market — the 2025 reconfiguration of the largest AI-training-data vendor after a frontier lab took a near-majority stake, followed within months by that vendor's other frontier-lab customers winding down their work with it — and the lesson generalizes cleanly: in any layer that everyone needs, neutrality *is* the asset, it is non-fungible, and it is sold exactly once. Transaction Science's role with respect to the substrate is steward, not proprietor — the spec text and reference code are permissively licensed, and canonicity lives in the conformance vectors and content-addressing, not in any brand or company; a steward that forgot this would be choosing trajectory two and calling it a partnership. The commitment is therefore stated as a hard one: the substrate's steward does not sell its neutrality, and an implementation whose steward has sold it is, by that fact, no longer a reference for anyone but its acquirer.

**Trajectory two: AI companies, racing against the substrate's adoption curve, succeed in transforming the institutional middle into an AI-mediated rent layer before the substrate acquires independent constituency.** The bytes of the world flow through one or a few large AI companies' infrastructure, with rents extracted at every step, captured to a small number of corporate beneficiaries. This trajectory produces narrowly concentrated economic gains, structurally entrenches a new rent-extraction oligopoly, and forecloses the substrate's freedoms before they can be realized.

The trajectories are not symmetric. Trajectory one is the end of a five-hundred-year structural extraction. Trajectory two is its consolidation. The treatise's purpose is to make trajectory one mechanically buildable, and to document the substrate precisely enough that the work of building it can be distributed across thousands of independent actors who do not need to coordinate through any central authority. The reference implementation (Appendix A) is the existence proof. The threat model (Part III) names the attacks both trajectories' adversaries will mount. The workaround analysis (Part IV) engages each alternative seriously. The retrofit traces (Part V) name the specific patterns by which trajectory two adversaries will attempt to capture the substrate after it ships. The distribution analysis (Part VI) specifies what the substrate makes possible on the freed-capital side. The closing argument (Part VII) ties the technical, political, and philosophical case together.

The substrate is what money looks like when memory is not the binding constraint, what clearing looks like when computation is deterministic, what trust signaling looks like when cryptography is cheap, and what rent extraction looks like when the actual cost of the underlying operation is intrinsically reported. The thesis is that this is the right architecture for the next decade's economic transition, and that the transition is happening with or without the substrate; the only question is whether it happens through the substrate or through the AI-mediated rent layer that is currently being built in its place.

Part I closes here. Part II specifies the substrate's architecture in enough detail that a competent engineer in any language with cryptographic primitives equivalent to ed25519, blake3, and length-prefixed deterministic serialization can implement it. Part III walks the threat model. Parts IV and V engage the alternatives and the retrofit attacks. Part VI develops the distribution-side argument. Part VII closes with the freedom case completed and the substrate's predictions stated falsifiably.

The substrate is not a clever idea. It is the inevitable composition of three things that have already happened, applied to the specific purpose of replacing the institutional middle of money. The composition will be made. The treatise's job is to make it correctly.
# Part II — The Substrate Architecture

Part I established why the substrate is necessary. Part II specifies what the substrate is. The discipline of Part II is engineering precision: every architectural primitive named here is implementable from the prose by a competent engineer working in any language with cryptographic primitives equivalent to ed25519, blake3, and length-prefixed deterministic serialization. Part II does not give byte-level wire formats — those are a separate document, the conformance specification, that ships alongside the reference implementation. What Part II gives is the *architectural reasoning* the wire format encodes, in enough detail that the wire format follows mechanically from the prose.

Part II has ten sections. §8 specifies the smart byte schema — the load-bearing primitive on which everything else builds. §9 specifies deterministic simulation as the consensus mechanism. §10 specifies the byte history as a content-addressed chain. §11 specifies cluster topology and the constraints that govern it. §12 specifies the phase-buffering requirement that prevents lockstep deadlock at any cluster size greater than two. §13 specifies BFT supermajority commit. §14 specifies the dissent log as the load-bearing observability primitive. §15 specifies live issuance. §16 specifies persistence. §17 specifies the two transports the reference implementation supports.

By the close of Part II, the reader has the full architectural picture. Part III then walks the threat model the architecture has to survive. Parts IV–VII engage alternatives, retrofits, distribution implications, and the closing argument.

---

## §8. The smart byte schema

The smart byte is the substrate's only object. Everything else is a function over smart bytes. Clusters operate on smart bytes. Lockstep simulation transitions smart bytes. Issuers create smart bytes. Cryptographic verification verifies smart bytes' histories. The treatise's argument that the institutional middle's three functions — matching, clearing, trust signaling — collapse onto a single architectural primitive is the argument that the smart byte is sufficient to perform all three.

This section specifies what a smart byte carries, why it carries those specific things, and how the specification falls out of the substrate's architectural commitments. The reader who finishes §8 should be able to draw the smart byte's data structure from memory and explain why each field is necessary and why no other fields are necessary.

### 8.1 What a smart byte carries

A smart byte is a record carrying exactly five things:

1. **Identity** — a content-addressed unique identifier, computed as the cryptographic hash of the byte's origin attestation.
2. **Provenance** — the byte's origin attestation, signed by the issuer, recording who issued the byte, when, against what (if anything), and with what authorization.
3. **Ownership chain** — the cryptographic chain of signature-bound ownership transitions from the original recipient to the current owner, with each transition's preimage including the prior history hash so that the chain is content-addressed end-to-end.
4. **Cargo** — the application-layer payload the byte carries: USD, joules, compute hours, governance vote, attestation, anything else (§5). The cargo is opaque to the substrate's machinery and interpreted only by application protocols.
5. **Joule cost** — a two-part energy accounting (`measured_microjoules` plus `estimated_microjoules`) that records the cumulative energy cost of operations performed on the byte over its lifetime.

These five fields are the entire smart byte. Any additional field would either duplicate one of the five (cargo can carry application-specific metadata), violate the substrate's separation of concerns (timestamps would couple bytes to wall-clock time, which the substrate does not depend on), or undermine the substrate's architectural commitments (a transaction-fee field would protocol-privilege a particular cargo type, contrary to the vesicle reframing in §5). The five fields are not chosen for elegance; they are chosen because removing any one of them breaks a load-bearing property of the substrate, and adding any sixth produces a worse design than the five-field version composed with application-layer protocols.

The remainder of §8 walks each field in turn, explaining what it carries, why it is necessary, what cryptographic primitives it depends on, and what the field's presence enables that other architectures lacking it cannot achieve.

### 8.2 Identity: the content-addressed unique identifier

A smart byte's identity is the blake3 hash of the byte's origin attestation. The hash is 32 bytes. It is computed once at the byte's birth and never changes. Every reference to the byte — in any other byte's history, in any cluster's state, in any application protocol — uses this hash as the byte's identifier.

The choice of content addressing rather than sequential identifiers (counter-based, UUID-based, or issuer-namespaced) is not stylistic. Content addressing produces several properties no sequential scheme can:

**Independent verifiability.** Any party holding the byte's origin attestation can independently compute the byte's identity and confirm that it matches the asserted ID. A sequential scheme requires trusting whoever assigned the ID. Content addressing makes the assignment self-verifying.

**Uniqueness without coordination.** Two issuers in different jurisdictions issuing bytes simultaneously cannot collide on identity, because the inputs to the hash include the issuer's pubkey, the cargo, the recipient, the nonce, and the issuer's signature — the probability of collision in a 256-bit hash space is vanishingly small. A sequential scheme requires a coordination mechanism (a global counter, a namespace registry, a UUID authority) that the substrate would have to provide and trust. Content addressing eliminates the coordination requirement.

**Retroactive immutability.** A byte's identity cannot be changed without producing a different byte. Any attempted modification of the origin attestation produces a different hash, which is a different byte, which has no relationship to the original. This makes fraud trivial to detect: a forged byte either has the wrong identity (and is rejected on first verification) or has a content-addressed identity that does not match any record any honest party holds.

**Network-effect alignment with content-addressed history.** §10 specifies that the byte's full history is also content-addressed. Identity-by-hash composes with history-by-hash to produce a byte whose every property is verifiable from the byte's content alone, with no reliance on any external authority. This is the architectural pattern that lets the substrate operate without a central registry, central naming authority, or central state store.

The cryptographic dependency is on a hash function with three properties: collision resistance, preimage resistance, and speed. Blake3 satisfies all three at the level the substrate requires (see §4.1 on cryptographic maturation). SHA-256 also satisfies all three but is slower; either works correctly but blake3 produces lower joule cost per byte, which matters at substrate scale.

The byte's identity is computed once and frozen. There is no rekeying, no rotation, no version. If the cryptographic primitive itself is compromised — if blake3 turned out to have a collision attack — the substrate would have to migrate to a successor primitive, but the migration is a substrate-wide event, not a per-byte event. §19 (Cryptographic attacks) walks the migration path.

### 8.3 Provenance: the origin attestation

Every smart byte was created by some issuer at some moment, against some thing (if the cargo is redeemable), with some authorization. The provenance records all of this in a signed structure that becomes part of the byte's permanent identity.

The origin attestation contains:

- **Issuer pubkey**: the ed25519 public key of the entity issuing the byte. Issuers are bound by the cluster's authorization rules (§15) — the cluster's state carries a registry of authorized issuers per cargo type, and only registered issuers can create valid bytes of the corresponding cargo type.
- **Cargo specification**: the cargo's type tag, value, and any cargo-specific metadata. For a USD-byte issued by a GENIUS-licensed stablecoin issuer, the cargo specification includes the dollar value, the issuer's redemption policy URL, and an attestation that the dollar reserves are held in qualifying liquid assets. For a joule-byte issued by a community solar cooperative, the cargo specification includes the joule value, the redemption mechanism (which physical electrical-distribution interconnect honors the byte), and a freshness window beyond which the byte expires.
- **Recipient pubkey**: the ed25519 public key of the original recipient. The byte's first transfer must be authorized by this key.
- **Nonce**: a 16-byte random or sequentially-allocated value that prevents two attestations with otherwise-identical content from producing the same identity. The nonce is the substrate's solution to the issue that an issuer who legitimately wants to issue two USD-100 bytes to the same recipient at the same logical moment would otherwise produce two attestations with identical content and therefore identical hashes — which is wrong, because they are distinct bytes.
- **Issuer signature**: a signature by the issuer's private key over a canonical serialization of the prior four fields, prefixed with a one-byte *algorithm identifier* naming the signature scheme in use (`0x01` = ed25519 in v1; the remaining range is reserved for post-quantum schemes such as ML-DSA, so a successor can be introduced incrementally alongside ed25519 — see §4.1, §19.5, §19.8 — without a flag-day migration). The signature binds the issuer to the attestation and prevents anyone else from producing valid attestations claiming to be from the issuer; the algorithm identifier tells every verifier which verification predicate to apply, and lets a single issuer rotate to a post-quantum key without invalidating verification of the bytes it signed under the old one.

The provenance is the byte's *birth certificate*. It is checked by every node that ever touches the byte: when the byte is created, the cluster verifies the issuer is registered for the cargo type and verifies the issuer's signature; when the byte is transferred, recipient nodes verify the provenance back through the ownership chain to confirm the chain's first link is authorized by the original recipient pubkey; when the byte is verified independently by a non-cluster party, the party verifies the provenance from the cargo and pubkeys and signature directly.

Provenance is what gives the smart byte its second property: trustability. A byte with valid provenance is a byte the substrate's machinery accepts. A byte with invalid provenance — bad signature, unregistered issuer, malformed cargo — is dropped silently on contact. The drop is deterministic across honest nodes; if the provenance fails verification on one honest node, it fails on every honest node. There is no race condition where some nodes accept and some reject, because the verification is purely a function of the byte's content and the cluster's issuer registry, both of which are deterministically synchronized.

### 8.4 Ownership chain: the signature-bound transitions

A smart byte's ownership history is a sequence of transitions, each signed by the byte's owner-at-the-time-of-transition, transferring the byte from one pubkey to another. The first transition is from the original recipient (named in the provenance) to the next owner. Subsequent transitions are from each owner to the next. The current owner is the recipient of the most recent transition.

Each transition contains:

- **Previous history hash**: the blake3 hash of the byte's history up to but not including this transition. This is what makes the chain content-addressed end-to-end. A transition cannot be inserted into the middle of the chain without producing different hashes for every subsequent transition, which would be visible as a divergence to any party with the original chain.
- **From pubkey**: the pubkey of the owner authorizing the transfer. Must equal the *To pubkey* of the previous transition (or the original recipient pubkey for the first transition).
- **To pubkey**: the pubkey of the new owner.
- **Frame number**: the lockstep frame in which the transition is applied. This is the substrate's only time-like field; it counts cluster-committed frames, not wall-clock time.
- **Signature**: a signature by the from-pubkey's private key over a canonical serialization of (byte ID, previous history hash, from pubkey, to pubkey, frame number), carrying the same one-byte algorithm identifier as the provenance signature (§8.3) — so a transition signed under ed25519 and a transition signed under a post-quantum successor are both verifiable, and a byte's ownership chain can span the migration boundary without a break.

The signature is what authorizes the transfer. The substrate's transfer-validation machinery verifies the signature against the from-pubkey, verifies the from-pubkey matches the byte's current owner, verifies the previous history hash matches the byte's actual current history hash, and verifies the frame number matches the current cluster frame. Any verification failure causes the transfer to be silently dropped.

The signature's preimage explicitly includes the byte ID and the previous history hash. This is not redundant with what's already in the chain; it is the cryptographic binding that prevents transition replay across different bytes or different histories. A signature valid for byte X's transition at history-hash H is *not* valid for byte Y's transition or for byte X's transition at a different history-hash. Without this binding, an adversary who captured a single legitimate signature could replay it across many bytes; with it, the signature is cryptographically scoped to exactly one transition on exactly one byte at exactly one moment.

The ownership chain has three properties that no account-balance-based architecture can achieve:

**Per-byte ownership rather than aggregate balance.** A user who holds 1,000 USD-bytes from issuer A does not have an "account balance" of $1,000. They hold 1,000 distinct byte objects, each with its own identity, its own history, and its own provenance. Transfers move specific bytes, not abstract balance. This is structurally different from how banks (and most cryptocurrencies) work, where a user's holding is a fungible balance. The byte-level granularity is what enables cargo specificity (a 1996 vintage joule-byte from a specific solar array is distinguishable from a 2024 joule-byte from the same array, even though both might be denominated in joules) and what enables provenance to remain meaningful through arbitrary transfer sequences.

**No double-spend at the protocol level.** A byte can be transferred to exactly one new owner per cluster frame, because the transfer is authorized by the byte's *current* owner against the byte's *current* history hash, and a successful transfer changes both. An attempted second transfer of the same byte in the same frame, even by the same legitimate owner, has the same previous-history-hash and is either ordered first (and applied) or ordered second (and dropped because the previous-history-hash no longer matches). Lockstep simulation's deterministic input ordering ensures every honest node makes the same choice.

**Independent verifiability of any historical state.** Any party with the byte's full history can compute the byte's state at any prior moment. The chain is content-addressed, so there is no ambiguity: the history is exactly what it is, and any tampering produces a hash divergence. This enables forensic-style verification: a recipient receiving a byte can audit its full provenance back to the issuer without trusting the sender, the cluster, or any intermediary.

### 8.5 Cargo: the application-layer payload

The cargo is what the byte is *for*. It is the application-layer content that gives the byte meaning in the higher-layer economy. The substrate is deliberately silent about what cargo means — the substrate verifies cryptographic and structural properties; it does not interpret cargo semantics.

The cargo field has two parts:

- **Cargo type tag**: a small integer identifying the cargo class. Cargo types are registered in a public registry that the substrate publishes; new cargo types are added via the same registry mechanism. The tag is opaque to the substrate's machinery — the substrate knows that type 1 is "USD redeemable from the issuer named in provenance" only by convention, not by validation. Application protocols read the tag and interpret the cargo accordingly.
- **Cargo bytes**: a length-prefixed sequence of bytes containing the cargo-specific payload. For a USD-byte, the cargo bytes encode the dollar value as a fixed-point integer plus any cargo-specific metadata (issuer's redemption policy URL, freshness window, etc.). For a joule-byte, the cargo bytes encode the joule value plus the redemption interconnect identifier. For a governance-vote byte, the cargo bytes encode the proposition ID and the vote direction. The substrate transports the cargo bytes unchanged.

The cargo's opacity to the substrate is not a limitation; it is the architectural decision that makes the substrate general (§5). A substrate that interpreted USD bytes specially would either privilege USD over other cargo types (violating the vesicle reframing) or require a cargo-specific code path for every cargo type (producing a substrate that grows in complexity with every new application). Opacity is the design.

Cargo carries one constraint that applies to every cargo type: the cargo bytes must be deterministically serializable. Two honest nodes parsing the same cargo specification must produce byte-for-byte identical results, regardless of platform, compiler, or runtime. This is the substrate's consistency requirement and it constrains cargo encodings to fixed-point integers, length-prefixed strings, content-addressed nested structures, and similar deterministic forms. Floating-point cargo is forbidden at the substrate level; cargo types that conceptually need floating-point must use fixed-point representations.

The substrate provides no semantic validation of cargo. A USD-byte whose cargo claims a value of $1,000,000 is structurally valid if the issuer's signature verifies; whether the issuer is solvent, whether the dollar reserves exist, whether the redemption policy is honored — these are application-layer and issuer-layer concerns. The substrate's contribution is cryptographic provenance and tamper-evident history; the substrate does not police what issuers issue.

### 8.6 Joule cost: the two-part energy accounting

Every smart byte carries a record of the cumulative energy cost of operations performed on it. The record has two parts:

- **`measured_microjoules`**: the sum of energy measurements taken by nodes performing operations on the byte. Where RAPL or equivalent hardware energy counters are available, the measurement is read from the hardware. Where they are not, the measurement is zero (an honest report of "not measurable on this node").
- **`estimated_microjoules`**: the sum of workload-correlated estimates predicting the operation's energy cost based on a calibrated model. The estimation function is a substrate-level constant, deterministic, and verifiable: any honest node executing the same operation produces the same estimate.

The two-part design is the substrate's solution to a real problem. Per-operation energy measurement is approximate at the per-process level (§4.3) — RAPL measures the package, and other processes contribute to the package draw. The measurement is meaningful and useful, but it is not a precise per-byte attribution. The estimation function provides a parallel signal: an independent prediction of what the operation should have cost, based on workload characteristics that the substrate knows. The two together let the substrate detect divergence: a node whose measured values consistently undershoot the estimated values is either running faster hardware than the model expects (legitimate, and the model can be recalibrated) or is lying about its measurements (a dissent-log entry, see §14).

The cumulative property of the joule cost is intentional. Every operation on the byte adds to the running totals. The byte's joule cost grows monotonically. A byte that has lived for a long time and been transferred many times accumulates a substantial joule cost, which is information: a long-traveled byte's full lifetime energy footprint is part of its provenance. Application protocols can use this information for whatever they want — pricing freshness premiums, identifying high-velocity bytes that may indicate suspicious activity, environmental accounting at the cargo-portfolio level. The substrate provides the data; applications interpret.

The joule cost is also the substrate's primary defense against the illegibility-of-cost protection that has shielded rent layers for centuries (§4.3, §6.1). Every transaction's energy cost is intrinsically reported. Any party can compare the substrate's actual operational cost to the rents charged by alternative settlement mechanisms. Rent layers can still exist on top of the substrate, charging whatever the market will bear, but they have to charge against an honest cost number. The substrate provides the baseline; humans must compare the rents to the baseline.

There is a second consequence of intrinsic joule accounting worth stating, because an entire category of products is built on its absence. Every byte — including every byte whose cargo *is* a dataset, a model, a corpus, or any other "data asset" — carries a cumulative, reproducible record of the energy it cost to mint, transfer, verify, and serve. That record is the only *automatic, falsifiable* valuation primitive a data asset can have. It is not the market price (the market sets price; the substrate is silent on it), but it is a defensible cost basis, computed by hardware energy counters anyone can read, with no consulting engagement and no proprietary model in the loop. This is the precise contrast with the "data valuation" products that periodically appear — opaque "data scores" and "data values" produced by undisclosed algorithms, which are unfalsifiable by construction and have, accordingly, never reached scale as a standalone business (the discipline that does exist — Laney's infonomics taxonomy and the handful of valuation consultancies — is advisory work, not a product, precisely because there is no automatic version of it). The substrate does not score data. It reports what data cost, in joules, and lets the market and the application layer do the rest. "What it cost, falsifiably" turns out to be the part nobody selling a "DataValue" has been able to ship — because shipping it requires the per-operation energy measurement (§4.3) that the substrate has and a marketing claim does not.

### 8.7 What the schema does not carry

Equally important is what the smart byte schema does not include.

**No wall-clock timestamps.** The substrate has no concept of wall-clock time. The only time-like field is the lockstep frame number (§8.4, §9), which counts cluster-committed frames. Wall-clock time is a non-deterministic property — different machines have different clocks, NTP synchronization fails, time zones differ, daylight saving time changes — and including wall-clock timestamps in the schema would couple the substrate to a non-deterministic input that adversaries could manipulate. Application protocols that need wall-clock timestamps can include them in cargo metadata (with the issuer or transferor attesting to the time), but the substrate itself does not depend on them.

**No transaction fees at the protocol level.** A byte does not carry a fee field. Transfers are cryptographically authorized but not economically priced at the substrate layer. This is the architectural decision that prevents protocol-level rent extraction (§5.2): a substrate that charged fees per transfer would either privilege a particular cargo type (whatever the fee was denominated in) or require complex multi-currency fee logic. By having no protocol-level fees, the substrate stays neutral over cargo types. Layer-2 services may charge fees for the services they provide (custodial wallets, dispute resolution, compliance), but the substrate's transfer machinery is fee-free.

**No account balances.** The substrate is per-byte, not per-account. There is no field anywhere in the schema that represents a user's aggregate holding of any cargo type. Aggregation is application-layer work: a wallet application that wants to display "you hold 1,000 USD" computes that by summing the cargo values of all USD-bytes the user owns. The substrate stores the bytes; the wallet does the math. This is structurally different from account-based architectures and is what enables byte-level cargo specificity.

**No mutability.** A byte's content is immutable from the moment of creation. Transfers add transitions to the ownership chain but do not modify the byte's identity, provenance, or original cargo specification. (Cargo can carry application-layer mutable state, but the cargo specification itself — the issuer's attestation about what the byte represents — is fixed.) Updates to the substrate's view of a byte are accomplished by adding new transitions, not by editing existing fields. The immutability is what makes content-addressing meaningful: a mutable byte would have an identity that changes with its content, defeating the purpose.

**No identity binding to legal persons.** The pubkeys in the schema (issuer, recipient, owners) are cryptographic identifiers. The substrate does not bind them to real-world legal persons, corporate entities, or government-issued identities. That binding, where it is required for regulatory or commercial reasons, is performed by issuers and compliance services as layer-2 work. The substrate's transfer machinery does not know whether a pubkey corresponds to a human, a corporation, an AI, or a contract; it knows only that signatures from the pubkey are valid. This is what enables both the substrate's freedom-from-forced-intermediary property (§6.1) and the substrate's pseudonymity-not-anonymity property (§6.2).

The five-field schema is the substrate's load-bearing data structure, and it is the place where the rest of the substrate's architectural commitments are made concrete. §9 specifies the consensus mechanism that governs transitions. §10 specifies the byte history's content-addressed structure in more detail. §11 specifies the cluster topology that bounds where the consensus mechanism can operate. The remainder of Part II builds out from this five-field foundation. By the close of Part II, the reader has the full architectural picture.
## §9. Deterministic simulation as the consensus mechanism

The substrate uses lockstep deterministic simulation as its consensus mechanism. Each byte's cluster of replicating nodes runs an identical state machine, advanced one frame at a time, with the cluster committing each frame's post-state via cryptographic supermajority agreement. There is no proof-of-work. There is no proof-of-stake. There is no leader election. There is no mempool. There is no fee market. The consensus mechanism is the same one that has run real-time strategy games for forty years, applied to byte-denominated commerce because the same architecture solves the same problem at three to five orders of magnitude lower energy cost than blockchain alternatives.

This section is where the substrate's most counterintuitive engineering decision gets defended. The reflexive objection from anyone who has worked on distributed systems is that lockstep cannot possibly work at internet scale: it requires every node to receive every input, it deadlocks if any node is slow, it has no leader to break ties, it cannot tolerate Byzantine adversaries without a separate consensus protocol. Each of these objections is real, each has a specific architectural answer in the substrate's design, and the answers compose into a system that operates at scales the objections did not anticipate. §9 walks each objection and its answer.

By the close of §9, the reader should accept that lockstep simulation is not a clever workaround for blockchain's energy problem but the *natural* consensus mechanism for byte-denominated commerce, which the institutional middle and the cryptocurrency space both failed to notice for the same structural reason: institutional pattern-matching, in different forms.

### 9.1 What lockstep simulation is

Lockstep deterministic simulation is the architectural pattern in which every node in a replicating cluster runs an identical state machine, processes the same inputs in the same order, and produces byte-for-byte identical output state. The cluster's distributed agreement on the world is achieved not by sharing the world's state across nodes (each node already has it) but by exchanging only the inputs that move the state forward, plus periodic cryptographic verification that the states have not diverged.

A frame is the substrate's atomic unit of progress. Each frame proceeds in three phases:

**Phase one: input exchange.** Each node broadcasts its pending inputs — transfer requests, mint requests, attestation requests — to every other node in the cluster. The exchange is bidirectional and complete: each node ends the phase holding the union of all peer inputs for the frame.

**Phase two: deterministic transition.** Each node applies the union-ordered inputs to its local state, deterministically. The ordering is canonical (input by input hash, sorted) so all honest nodes apply inputs in the same order. The transition function is purely functional: same inputs in same order produce same output state.

**Phase three: cryptographic verification.** Each node computes the post-frame state hash and broadcasts it. The frame is committed if a supermajority (⌊2n/3⌋+1) of nodes report the same hash. The supermajority commit tolerates ⌊(n-1)/3⌋ Byzantine nodes per the standard BFT bound (§13).

The cycle repeats. A cluster running at one frame per millisecond commits a thousand frames per second; each frame can carry many transactions; throughput scales with frame rate and per-frame transaction capacity. The substrate's reference implementation runs at 1144 frames/second with 3 nodes and 99 frames/second with 10 nodes (Appendix A scaling table) — the throughput envelope is well within what byte-denominated commerce requires.

The architectural pattern is not new. Lockstep simulation was invented by real-time strategy game developers in the late 1990s to synchronize game state across networked players who could not afford to ship the full world state every frame (typical RTS games have tens of thousands of units; shipping all state every frame would saturate any home internet connection). The pattern was matured through the 2000s and 2010s into production-quality libraries (GGRS for Rust, backroll-rs, RetroArch's netplay subsystem, Photon's Bolt for Unity). By 2020 the pattern was off-the-shelf, well-documented, and battle-tested in commercial games shipping to millions of players. The substrate borrows the pattern unchanged, substituting "byte transactions" for "player inputs" and "byte state" for "game world state."

### 9.2 Why lockstep is sufficient for byte-denominated commerce

The reflexive objection is that lockstep is "obviously" inadequate for global financial infrastructure. The objection rests on three misreadings, each of which §9.2 addresses.

**Misreading one: lockstep is for low-stakes games, not for high-stakes finance.** This conflates the consequences of error with the architecture's correctness. A game using lockstep correctly will produce identical state across all clients, with state divergence (a "desync") immediately detectable and handled by either client disconnection or rollback. The architecture does not stop being correct when applied to financial state; it produces the same identical-state guarantee, with the same divergence-detection. The stakes are higher in finance, but the cryptographic and protocol machinery is the same. The substrate adds Byzantine-tolerant supermajority commit (§13) precisely because financial state requires defending against actively malicious nodes, but the underlying lockstep pattern is unchanged.

**Misreading two: lockstep cannot scale to internet-size networks.** This is correct but misleading. A *single* lockstep cluster cannot scale beyond ~32-64 nodes before message overhead dominates (§11). The substrate's response is to never run a single cluster at internet scale; instead, the substrate runs many clusters, with cross-cluster transfer protocols handling movement of bytes between clusters. The pattern is the same as the internet's: TCP/IP does not run a single autonomous system globally; it runs ~100,000 autonomous systems with BGP coordinating them. The substrate runs ~100,000 clusters with the cross-cluster protocols (§11.3) coordinating them. Scaling is by federation, not by consensus growth.

**Misreading three: lockstep cannot tolerate failures or adversaries.** This is the objection that confuses lockstep-the-pattern with naive-lockstep-without-defenses. The substrate's lockstep includes per-message phase buffering (§12), supermajority commit with ⌊(n-1)/3⌋ Byzantine tolerance (§13), dissent logging that identifies persistent misbehavers (§14), and persistence-and-replay that lets crashed nodes resume cleanly (§16). Each of these is a real defense against a real failure mode. The composed system tolerates the failure modes that internet-scale operation actually presents: slow nodes (handled by phase buffering), malicious nodes (handled by supermajority commit), crashed nodes (handled by persistence), and adversarial input ordering (handled by canonical hash-sort).

The substrate's lockstep is not "naive lockstep applied to finance." It is "lockstep composed with cryptographic supermajority commit, content-addressed history, and operational defenses against the specific failure modes adversarial financial environments produce." The composed pattern is correct for byte-denominated commerce. The reflexive "lockstep can't work for finance" objection answers a strawman.

### 9.3 The energy economics of lockstep vs blockchain

The energy comparison between lockstep and blockchain consensus is not subtle. It is three to five orders of magnitude. The reason is structural.
