Blockchain consensus mechanisms — proof-of-work most prominently, but also proof-of-stake in its various forms — solve a harder problem than lockstep does. They achieve consensus among a fully open, permissionless, anonymous-participant network where any node can join or leave without coordination, where the network has no membership boundary, and where Sybil attacks (a single adversary running thousands of nodes) must be prevented economically rather than structurally. Each of these properties is genuinely valuable in some contexts; each is also expensive.

Proof-of-work prevents Sybil attacks by making node participation cost real energy: a node attempting to influence consensus must perform computation that has no economic value other than as a token of participation. The energy cost of Bitcoin's proof-of-work is approximately 120 TWh per year per the Cambridge Bitcoin Electricity Consumption Index 2024 estimate (with reported uncertainty bounds of 67–240 TWh; the network's hash rate and miners' energy mix drive the variation). This is in the range of a medium-sized country's electricity consumption — comparable to Belgium or the Netherlands. The cost is paid not for the work the network performs (settling transactions) but for the work the network's adversary-defense requires (preventing Sybil attacks).

Proof-of-stake replaces energy-per-Sybil with capital-at-risk-per-Sybil. The energy cost is dramatically lower (Ethereum's post-Merge proof-of-stake consumes approximately 0.0026 TWh/year per CCRI's measurement following the September 15, 2022 Merge — a 99.988% reduction from the network's pre-Merge ~94 TWh, and roughly 50,000× less than Bitcoin), but the architectural complexity is higher: stake-bonding, slashing, validator-set rotation, finality gadgets, MEV-resistance, and a dozen other concerns that Ethereum's protocol engineers have spent years working through.

Lockstep simulation does not solve the open-permissionless-Sybil-resistant consensus problem. It solves a different problem: consensus among a *known, bounded, signed-membership* set of nodes. A lockstep cluster has a defined membership; nodes join through admission protocols (§11.3.1) that include attestation, reputation, and proof-of-resource gates; the cluster size is bounded by the lockstep latency budget. Inside the bounded membership, supermajority commit handles Byzantine adversaries up to ⌊(n-1)/3⌋. There is no Sybil-resistance problem because membership is explicit, not anonymous.

The energy cost of lockstep is approximately the cost of running the deterministic state machine plus the cost of cryptographic verification (signatures, hashes). The substrate's reference implementation measures approximately 840 microjoules per transfer at n=3 in-process (Appendix A). At cluster scale with TCP transport and full BFT supermajority commit, the cost is bounded by the underlying compute and network operations: each transfer requires verifying one ed25519 signature (~100 microseconds of CPU), updating a small data structure in memory, hashing the updated state (microseconds), and exchanging a few hundred bytes per peer per frame. The total per-transfer energy is on the order of one millijoule, scaling modestly with cluster size as message overhead grows O(n²).

The energy comparison:

| Mechanism | Energy per transaction |
|---|---:|
| Bitcoin (proof-of-work) | ~3.6 × 10⁹ J |
| Ethereum (proof-of-stake, post-Merge) | ~2 × 10⁴ J |
| Visa (actual computation, excluding rent) | ~10⁻² to 10⁰ J |
| Substrate (lockstep + BFT, v0.3 measured) | ~10⁻³ J |

The Bitcoin figure derives from the Cambridge Bitcoin Electricity Consumption Index (CBECI) point estimate of approximately 120 TWh annual network consumption divided by approximately 120 million annual transactions, yielding roughly 3.6 gigajoules per transaction. The Ethereum proof-of-stake figure derives from CCRI's post-Merge measurement of approximately 2,600 MWh annual network consumption divided by approximately 400 million annual transactions. The Visa figure is the actual computational cost of credit-card transaction processing, which is small; what makes Visa expensive to users is the rent layer, not the computation. The substrate figure derives from the v0.3 reference implementation's measurement of approximately 840 microjoules per transfer at n=3 in-process (Appendix A).

The substrate is roughly two to three orders of magnitude cheaper than Visa's actual computation cost, seven orders of magnitude cheaper than Ethereum's proof-of-stake, and twelve orders of magnitude cheaper than Bitcoin's proof-of-work. The advantage over blockchain mechanisms comes from the architectural decision to operate within a bounded-membership cluster rather than an open-permissionless network. The advantage over Visa is smaller in pure computation but much larger in user-visible cost, because Visa's rent layer adds many orders of magnitude on top of its computation cost (see §4.3).

The energy economics are not the only reason to prefer lockstep, but they are the reason that makes the rest of the substrate's case mechanically inevitable. A substrate three to nine orders of magnitude cheaper to operate than alternatives will, by economic gravity alone, displace the alternatives over time — provided the substrate ships, gets deployed, and is not preempted by AI-mediated institutional middles.

### 9.4 The lockstep protocol in detail

The substrate's lockstep protocol has six steps per frame, executed by every node in the cluster. The protocol is identical regardless of cluster size, transport type, or cargo class. The reference implementation is approximately 200 lines of Rust; the protocol's correctness depends on each step being executed in order.

**Step 1: drain pending inputs.** Each node accumulates inputs (transfer requests, mint requests) submitted by its connected clients. At the start of a frame, the node drains its pending input queue into a per-frame batch. The pending queue is a per-node concept; clients submit to one or more nodes, and the substrate's input-distribution mechanism (round-robin in v0.4+, currently submit-to-any) ensures inputs reach the cluster.

**Step 2: broadcast input batch.** Each node sends its frame-N input batch to every peer. The message is `InputBatch { from: NodeId, frame: u64, transfers: Vec<TransferInput>, mints: Vec<MintInput> }`. Inputs are sent as their full signed records, not as references — the cluster does not assume out-of-band input availability.

**Step 3: receive peer input batches.** Each node waits for `InputBatch` messages from every peer for the current frame. The receive logic uses phase-buffered selection (§12) to handle peers that have run ahead by one frame; messages from frame N+1 are buffered until frame N+1's input-receive phase begins. A node that receives a duplicate `InputBatch` from the same peer for the same frame returns a protocol error.

**Step 4: order the union.** Each node forms the union of its own inputs and all peer inputs, then orders the union by canonical hash sort: each input is serialized to its bincode-equivalent canonical bytes, blake3-hashed, and sorted by the hash. Ties (identical inputs from different peers) are deduplicated. The ordering is deterministic — every honest node, given the same union, produces the same ordering.

**Step 5: deterministic transition.** Each node applies the ordered inputs to its local state. Mints are processed before transfers within the frame (§15). Each input is verified independently: signatures must match, previous history hashes must match, frame numbers must match, issuer registrations must be current. Inputs that fail any verification are silently dropped — the failure is deterministic, so all honest nodes drop the same inputs. After all inputs are applied, the frame number is incremented.

**Step 6: cryptographic supermajority commit.** Each node computes the post-frame state hash (blake3 over the canonical serialization of the entire state, including the new frame number) and broadcasts a `StateHash { from: NodeId, frame: u64, hash: Hash }` message. Each node tallies received state hashes; when a hash has been reported by ⌊2n/3⌋+1 nodes, the frame is committed at that hash. If the node's own hash matches the supermajority hash, the node continues normally. If the node's own hash differs from the supermajority hash, the node enters a divergence state (§13.3 outcome three describes the resulting halt; v0.3 halts with `MINORITY DIVERGENCE`, v0.4 will state-sync from honest peers per §16.5). If no hash reaches supermajority, the cluster has split (§13.5) and progress halts until the partition is resolved.

The protocol's correctness depends on three properties: every honest node executes the same steps in the same order, the deterministic transition produces identical state for identical inputs, and the cryptographic verification correctly identifies divergence. Each property is independently testable; the reference implementation has property tests covering each (Appendix A). The protocol's failure modes are also well-characterized: phase desynchronization is handled by buffering (§12), Byzantine adversaries are handled by supermajority commit (§13), input-ordering attacks are handled by canonical hash sort (§9.5), and crashed nodes are handled by persistence-and-replay (§16).

### 9.5 Determinism: the load-bearing requirement

Lockstep simulation requires that the state-transition function be *deterministic*: the same inputs in the same order, applied to the same starting state, must produce byte-for-byte identical resulting state. Every node in the cluster must agree, exactly, on what the state is after every frame. Any divergence — even a single bit — is detected by the supermajority commit and treated as a node-level failure.

Determinism is harder than it looks. Modern computing platforms are non-deterministic by default in several ways:

**Floating-point arithmetic.** IEEE 754 operations vary subtly across compilers, CPUs, and even CPU revisions. The result of `a + b + c` can differ depending on the order the operations are emitted by the compiler or scheduled by the CPU. The substrate forbids floating-point in the state-transition function and uses i64 fixed-point arithmetic throughout. Cargo values are stored as fixed-point integers with a documented scaling factor (typically 6 decimal places, so $1.50 is stored as 1_500_000); arithmetic on cargo values uses integer operations exclusively.

**Multi-threaded execution.** Concurrent execution interleaves unpredictably. The substrate's state-transition function is single-threaded per node: a node applies inputs sequentially, in the canonical ordering produced by §9.4 step 4. The substrate's outer infrastructure (network I/O, cryptographic verification, persistence) can be multi-threaded, but the state transition itself is not.

**System calls.** Calls to the operating system that return time-varying values (`gettimeofday`, `random`, `getpid`) introduce non-determinism. The substrate forbids these in the state-transition function. Frame numbers are the only time-like value, and they are deterministic by construction.

**Memory layout and allocator behavior.** Hash table iteration order, memory addresses, and similar allocator-dependent values can vary across runs. The substrate uses ordered data structures (BTreeMap rather than HashMap) for any iteration that affects state. Memory addresses are never used as values.

**Compiler and runtime variation.** Different compiler versions, optimization levels, and runtime libraries can produce subtly different behavior. The substrate's reference implementation pins specific versions of all dependencies; a substrate-conformance specification (the document accompanying this treatise) specifies the exact byte-level encodings the protocol requires, so that any conforming implementation in any language produces identical behavior.

The discipline required to maintain determinism is substantial but not exotic. Game engines have done it for forty years. Scientific simulations do it routinely. The discipline is well-understood, and the substrate's contribution is not to invent the discipline but to apply it to byte-denominated commerce.

The payoff for the discipline is the ability to use lockstep at all. Without determinism, the supermajority commit step would be useless — every honest node would produce a different state hash for the same inputs, and the cluster would never agree on anything. With determinism, the supermajority commit is the cluster's actual validation: agreement on the post-frame state hash *is* agreement on the post-frame state, because the hash uniquely identifies the state.

### 9.6 What lockstep does not solve

Lockstep is a consensus mechanism. It is not a complete distributed system. The substrate composes lockstep with several other mechanisms to handle problems lockstep alone does not address.

**Cluster admission and Sybil resistance.** Lockstep operates within a known, bounded membership. The question of who joins the cluster, on what terms, and how Sybil attacks against the admission process are prevented, is the cluster topology problem (§11.3). The substrate uses proof-of-resource shadow periods and reputation-based admission; these are layer-1.5 protocols that the substrate's lockstep does not itself provide.

**Cross-cluster coordination.** A single cluster cannot scale beyond ~32-64 nodes. Substrate-scale operation requires many clusters, with bytes moving between them. The cross-cluster transfer protocol (§11.3.3) is its own design problem, and lockstep within each cluster is just one ingredient. The substrate's reference implementation runs single-cluster only; cross-cluster is v0.4 work.

**Rejoining after partition.** A node that has been disconnected from its cluster (network partition, crash, prolonged unavailability) must catch up to the current frame before resuming participation. The substrate's persistence-and-replay mechanism (§16) handles cases where the node has its own checkpoint and log; cases where the node is rejoining without local state require state-sync from honest peers (v0.4).

**Application-layer concerns.** Lockstep produces deterministic, agreed-upon state. What that state means, how applications interpret it, what user interfaces present, and how higher-layer commerce is conducted are application concerns. The substrate provides the state; applications do everything else.

**Public verifiability of computations over cluster state — "compute-to-data."** Lockstep gives *bounded-membership* agreement: the cluster's nodes agree, by supermajority commit, on the result of every transition over their shared state. A party who is *not* a cluster member, and who wants to verify the result of a computation over cluster-held state — an aggregate, a join, a query over a dataset that lives in the cluster's bytes — without being admitted to the cluster and without being shown the underlying bytes, needs something lockstep does not itself produce: a succinct proof. This is where zero-knowledge proof systems compose, exactly as the substrate composes other primitives it does not provide natively (§19's "no cryptographic monoculture" discipline). The cheap default is lockstep replication among the bounded membership — sufficient whenever the parties who need to trust a result are, or can become, cluster members, which is the byte-denominated-commerce case §9.2 is built for, at roughly one millijoule per transfer. The escape hatch, for the public-verifier case, is a designated prover emitting a zero-knowledge proof of the proposition "the result of computation C over committed state S is R," which any non-member verifies in milliseconds without ever seeing S; the proof systems for this (succinct proofs over SQL-shaped queries, general zkVMs, proving marketplaces) are off-the-shelf as of the mid-2020s, just expensive relative to native execution, which is why they are the escape hatch and not the default. The composite primitive is *compute-to-data*: the data stays in the cluster, and what crosses to the outside is the result of a computation plus a proof of the result — never the data. This is the affordance a credible "data bank" needs in order to "monetize data without revealing it," it is precisely the affordance the products that advertise that phrase do not actually have, and it is a composition the substrate supports — lockstep for the bounded case, ZK for the public case — rather than a property lockstep delivers alone.

These are not gaps in the substrate's correctness; they are the boundaries between what the substrate provides and what other layers must provide. Lockstep is sufficient for what lockstep is supposed to do — committing frames of byte-state with cryptographic agreement. It is not sufficient for the full system, and the substrate is honest about the additional mechanisms it composes lockstep with.

The next section (§10) specifies the byte history's content-addressed structure in more detail, particularly the chain-of-hashes that makes per-byte verification independent of cluster trust. §11 specifies cluster topology and the constraints that govern it. §12-§17 build out the remaining architectural components. By the close of Part II, the reader has the full architectural picture, and the threat model in Part III becomes the natural next concern: what attacks does this architecture have to survive, and what does it actually defend against?

Lockstep simulation as the consensus mechanism is the substrate's most counterintuitive engineering decision. It is also the substrate's most defensible one, because the alternative — proof-of-work or proof-of-stake — solves a harder problem at three to nine orders of magnitude higher energy cost, and the substrate's bounded-membership cluster topology means the harder problem does not need solving. The institutional middle and the cryptocurrency space both failed to notice this for the same structural reason: institutional pattern-matching. The institutional middle pattern-matched "deterministic computation = blockchain = energy-wasteful," and the cryptocurrency space pattern-matched "consensus = open membership = needs Sybil-resistance." Both pattern-matches are wrong for byte-denominated commerce, and the substrate's design is what falls out when both pattern-matches are dropped.
## §10. The byte history as content-addressed chain

A smart byte's history is the complete, ordered sequence of all transitions the byte has undergone since its origin. The history is content-addressed: each transition includes the cryptographic hash of the byte's history-up-to-that-point, so the chain of transitions forms an unbreakable cryptographic ladder from the byte's current state back to its birth. Any party holding the byte's full history can independently verify every transition without trusting the cluster, the issuer, the original recipient, or any intermediate owner.

This section specifies the chain's mechanics, walks the verification procedure step by step, identifies what the chain enables that account-based architectures cannot match, and names the three places where the chain's content-addressing is load-bearing for the substrate's architectural commitments. By the close of §10, the reader should understand why the substrate's history is structurally different from a bank's transaction record or a blockchain's block, and why the difference matters.

### 10.1 The chain's structure

Every smart byte begins with an origin attestation (§8.3). The origin attestation's blake3 hash is the byte's identity (§8.2) and also serves as the byte's *initial history hash* — the hash of "the history so far," which at the byte's birth is just the origin attestation itself.

Each subsequent transition extends the chain. A transition records:

- The byte's identity (binding the transition to a specific byte)
- The previous history hash (binding the transition to a specific point in the chain)
- The transition's payload (a transfer's from-pubkey, to-pubkey, frame-number, and signature; see §8.4)

The transition's *new history hash* is computed as `blake3(previous_history_hash || transition_payload)`. This new hash becomes the byte's current history hash. The next transition references this new hash as its previous-history-hash, and so on. The chain is a Merkle-style hash sequence where each hash incorporates everything before it.

A byte's full state at any moment is the tuple (current owner, current history hash, current cargo, accumulated joule cost). The current history hash is the cryptographic summary of every transition the byte has experienced. Two bytes with the same current history hash have undergone identical transition sequences from the same origin; two bytes with different current history hashes have either different origins or different transition sequences.

### 10.2 Verification procedure

A party in possession of a byte's full history (origin attestation plus the ordered sequence of transitions) can verify the byte's authenticity end-to-end via the following procedure. The procedure is purely a function of the history's content; it requires no cluster connection, no issuer trust, no external state.

**Step 1: verify the origin attestation.** Compute the blake3 hash of the origin attestation's canonical serialization. This hash must equal the byte's identity. If it doesn't, the origin attestation has been tampered with or the byte's identity is forged; verification fails.

**Step 2: verify the issuer's signature.** Reconstruct the canonical message bound by the origin signature (cargo specification, recipient pubkey, nonce). Verify the issuer's ed25519 signature against the issuer's pubkey. If verification fails, the origin attestation is forged.

**Step 3: verify the issuer's authorization.** Confirm that the issuer's pubkey is in the issuer registry for the byte's cargo type. (This step requires access to the cluster's issuer registry at the time of byte creation, but the registry itself is content-addressed and verifiable; a verifier with the cluster's state-snapshot at the byte's birth-frame can confirm registration without further trust.) If the issuer was not registered, the origin attestation is invalid.

**Step 4: walk the transition chain.** For each transition in order:
- Compute the transition's previous-history-hash from the running chain. It must equal the previous-history-hash recorded in the transition. If not, the transition has been inserted, deleted, or reordered.
- Verify the transition's signature. The from-pubkey at this transition must equal the to-pubkey of the previous transition (or the original recipient pubkey for the first transition). The signature must verify against the from-pubkey over the canonical message (byte identity, previous-history-hash, from-pubkey, to-pubkey, frame-number).
- Update the running chain hash: `new_history_hash = blake3(previous_history_hash || transition_payload)`.

**Step 5: confirm the current state.** After walking all transitions, the running chain hash must equal the byte's claimed current history hash. The to-pubkey of the most recent transition must equal the byte's claimed current owner.

If all five steps succeed, the byte's history is authentic and the byte's current state is correctly derived from its history. If any step fails, the byte is corrupt, forged, or has been tampered with.

The verification has no concept of "the cluster says so." The verification is a pure function of the byte's content. This is the property that makes the substrate's trust model fundamentally different from any institutional-middle architecture: the verifier does not trust an authority; the verifier verifies.

### 10.3 What the chain enables

The content-addressed chain enables three properties no account-based architecture can match. Each is load-bearing for the substrate's broader architectural commitments.

**Independent third-party auditability.** A regulator, auditor, counterparty, or any other interested third party can verify a byte's full history without the cooperation of the byte's owner, the cluster operating the byte, or any institutional middle. The owner shares the byte's history; the third party walks the verification procedure. Neither party has any way to lie about what the history shows. This is structurally different from auditing a bank: a bank's records are owned by the bank, and the bank can present, withhold, or modify them at its discretion (subject to legal constraints, which the substrate makes irrelevant). A byte's history is owned by the byte itself. The auditor's confidence in the audit's correctness is identical to the auditor's confidence in blake3's collision resistance — well-understood, well-tested, well-bounded.

**Cluster trust scoped to current state only.** Every cluster operating a byte must be trusted for one thing: the byte's *current* state and current history hash. The cluster cannot be trusted (or rather, does not need to be trusted) for the byte's deep history, because deep history is independently verifiable from the chain. This is the architectural decomposition that lets the substrate scale across many clusters: each cluster handles current-state consensus for its own bytes, and the chain handles historical verification across cluster boundaries. A byte that has lived in cluster A and been transferred to cluster B is verifiable by cluster B without cluster B trusting cluster A's deep history claims; B asks for the byte's full chain, walks the verification, confirms the result.

**Tamper evidence with cryptographic precision.** Any modification to any part of the chain — the origin attestation, any transition's payload, any history hash — produces a hash divergence that is detectable on first verification. There is no way to forge "a byte that was transferred ten times" without forging ten transitions, each of which requires a valid signature from the byte's owner-at-the-time, which requires the corresponding private key. An adversary attempting to fabricate a fake history must compromise every owner's key in sequence, which is enormously harder than compromising a single bank's database. The chain's tamper-evidence is not "we hope this is hard to fake"; it is "this is at least as hard to fake as ed25519 + blake3, both of which are at the cryptographic-primitive level the wider security industry depends on."

These three properties compose into the substrate's broader claim: every byte carries its own legitimacy. The byte does not depend on the cluster's testimony for its history; it carries the testimony with it, cryptographically signed at every step. The cluster's contribution is current-frame consensus — what is the byte's *now*. Everything else, from the byte's birth to its most recent transfer, is intrinsic to the byte itself.

### 10.4 The three places the chain is load-bearing

The content-addressed chain is structurally load-bearing in three specific places elsewhere in the architecture. Each is worth naming so the reader can trace how §10's mechanics support the rest of the substrate.

**Place one: cross-cluster transfers (§11.3.3).** When a byte moves from cluster A to cluster B, cluster B must be confident that the byte is what cluster A says it is. Cluster A could lie. The chain is what makes cluster A's lie detectable: B asks for the byte's full history, walks the verification procedure, and either accepts (the chain verifies) or rejects (the chain doesn't). Cluster A's testimony about the byte's current state at the moment of transfer is the only thing B has to extend trust to, and that testimony is a single point: the current history hash plus the cluster's BFT-supermajority signature. Everything before that point B verifies independently.

**Place two: persistence and recovery (§16).** A node that has been disconnected from its cluster (network partition, crash, prolonged unavailability) recovers by loading its checkpoint and replaying the persisted log of frame inputs. The replay re-applies transitions to bytes; the chain's content-addressing ensures the replay produces the correct state, because each transition's previous-history-hash must match the byte's history at the moment of replay. A persistence layer that lost data, or that was tampered with by an adversary with disk access, would produce hash divergences when the node rejoined the cluster, immediately detectable by the supermajority commit. Persistence is therefore *audited by the chain* on every rejoin.

**Place three: dispute resolution (§3.2 and §22).** When two parties disagree about a byte's history — a recipient claims the byte was transferred to them, the alleged sender denies it — the chain is the authoritative record. The transferred-to party presents the byte's full chain ending with the transition naming them as the recipient. The verification either succeeds (the chain is valid, the signature is valid, the transfer happened) or fails (something in the chain is wrong, and the dispute is resolvable by identifying the failure). Dispute resolution does not require a court, an arbitrator, or an institutional adjudicator at the substrate layer; it requires only the chain. Layer-2 dispute resolution services may exist for cases where the cryptographic chain is insufficient (a user claiming they were defrauded into authorizing a transfer they didn't intend, for example), but those services operate above the substrate's chain-verification layer, not as substitutes for it.

### 10.5 Comparison to existing architectures

The content-addressed chain is not unprecedented; what is new is its application to byte-denominated commerce.

**Bitcoin's blockchain** is a content-addressed chain at the *block* level: each block contains the previous block's hash, and the chain is verifiable end-to-end. Bitcoin's design verifies *the entire ledger* through this chain. The substrate's chain operates at the *byte* level: each byte has its own chain, and the cluster's state is the union of all bytes' chains. The difference is significant: Bitcoin's chain forces every node to hold every transaction ever; the substrate's chain lets each byte's history be replicated only where it is needed. A byte that has lived only in cluster A has its full history only in cluster A's nodes; nodes in cluster B that have never handled the byte do not need to know about it.

**Git's commit graph** is a content-addressed chain at the commit level: each commit references its parent commit's hash, producing a verifiable history of source-code changes. The substrate's chain pattern is identical in spirit. What differs is that Git's chain is single-author at each commit (typically) while the substrate's chain alternates owners with each transfer. The cryptographic mechanics are otherwise the same, and the verification procedure is structurally identical to walking a Git history.

**IPFS's content-addressed storage** is a single-level content addressing (a piece of data's hash is its identifier) without an intrinsic chain structure. The substrate composes single-level content addressing (the byte's identity, §8.2) with chain-level content addressing (the byte's history, §10.1) to get both properties: a unique identifier per byte and a verifiable history per byte.

**Bank transaction logs** are not content-addressed at all. A bank's record of a transaction is a row in a database, identified by a sequence number or a UUID assigned by the bank. The bank can edit the row, delete the row, or insert a new row claiming an earlier sequence number, with no cryptographic detection mechanism. Bank logs are tamper-evident only to the extent that the bank's auditing controls and regulatory inspections detect tampering, which is institutional rather than cryptographic. The substrate's chain replaces the institutional integrity guarantee with a cryptographic one. The institutional guarantee can be defeated by the institution itself, the regulators capturing the institution, or political pressure on either; the cryptographic guarantee can be defeated only by breaking blake3 or ed25519, neither of which is currently feasible.

The substrate's architectural innovation in §10 is therefore not the chain itself — chains are forty-year-old cryptographic structures — but the *application of per-byte content-addressed chains to byte-denominated commerce*. The application matters because byte-denominated commerce was previously the domain of account-based architectures (banks) or single-ledger architectures (Bitcoin), neither of which gives the substrate's architectural decomposition: cluster-trusted current state, chain-trusted deep history, scalable across many clusters, independently verifiable end-to-end.

### 10.6 What the chain does not provide

The chain is precise about what it provides and what it does not. The discipline of §6 (overclaiming is worse than underclaiming) applies here.

**The chain does not provide privacy.** A byte's full history is visible to any party who holds it. The owner of a byte who wants to maintain privacy must use layer-2 mechanisms — separate pubkeys per relationship, cargo mixing for fungible cargo types, zero-knowledge proofs where appropriate. The substrate's chain transparency is the cost of the four freedoms named in §6.1, and it is an honest cost.

**The chain does not provide reversibility.** Once a transition is applied and the frame is committed, the transition is permanent. A user who authorized a transfer they later regret cannot undo it at the substrate level. (They can ask the recipient to send the byte back, which is a new transition, not a reversal.) Layer-2 services can offer "reversible" wrappers — escrow with timed release, conditional transfers with off-chain dispute resolution — but those compose on top of the chain rather than modifying its irreversibility. This is the same property Bitcoin has; users gain finality at the cost of recoverability from authorized mistakes.

**The chain does not provide automatic restitution.** A user whose private key is compromised loses control of the bytes signed by that key, and the chain has no mechanism to restore them. Recovery requires either issuer cooperation (if the issuer is willing to honor a key-rotation request and the cargo allows it, the issuer can mint replacement bytes) or social-recovery layer-2 services (multi-signature schemes, trusted-friend recovery, hardware-token rotation). The chain itself is silent on recovery; it is a record of what cryptographic operations occurred, not a judgment about whether they should have.

**The chain does not detect application-layer fraud.** The chain validates that signatures are correct and history is consistent; it does not validate that signatures were *intended*. A user phished into authorizing a transfer signs a valid transition that the chain accepts. Layer-2 fraud detection (anomaly detection, rate limiting, suspicious-pattern flagging) is the right place for this concern. The substrate provides cryptographic correctness; it does not provide intention verification.

These four absences are real and the treatise does not paper over them. Each is named here to be precise about the substrate's contribution: the chain is a mechanism for cryptographic authentication of byte history, and that is what it does. Privacy, reversibility, restitution, and intention verification are layer-2 concerns that compose on top of the chain, with their own tradeoffs and their own service providers. The substrate is honest about the boundary.

The next section (§11) specifies cluster topology and the constraints that govern it. The chain's content-addressing is what lets clusters trust each other's current-state assertions while independently verifying deep history; §11 walks the cluster topology that this trust decomposition enables. By the close of §11, the architectural picture covers byte schema (§8), consensus (§9), history (§10), and topology (§11) — the four primitives that compose into a substrate that performs the institutional middle's three functions at the cost structure §4 established.
## §11. Cluster topology and node admission

A single lockstep cluster cannot scale beyond approximately 32 to 64 nodes before message overhead dominates and per-frame work makes the cluster unusable. Every empirical scaling result the reference implementation has produced (Appendix A) confirms the O(n²) growth in per-frame messages, and confirms that the wall-clock cost of a frame at n=10 is already five times the cost at n=4. Substrate-scale operation — millions of bytes, billions of transfers per day, global geographic distribution — cannot be achieved by growing a single cluster. It must be achieved by federating many clusters.

This section specifies the substrate's cluster topology: how clusters are sized, how nodes are admitted, how clusters split when they grow too large, how bytes move between clusters, and how Byzantine cluster-level threats are contained. The section does the hardest architectural work in Part II because cluster topology is where the substrate's "trust the cluster for current state, trust the chain for deep history" decomposition is operationalized.

By the close of §11, the reader should understand why the substrate scales by federation rather than by consensus growth, what each cluster is responsible for, how the cluster system as a whole achieves substrate-scale throughput, and where the substrate genuinely defers v0.4+ work because the design is incomplete. The section is honest about what is implemented (§11.1, §11.2) and what is design-only (§11.3.2, §11.3.3, §11.3.4).

### 11.1 Cluster sizing: empirical bounds

A lockstep cluster's per-frame work is dominated by message exchange. Each node sends to (n-1) peers and receives from (n-1) peers in each phase; the substrate's protocol has two phases per frame (input exchange and state-hash exchange); the per-frame message count is therefore approximately 2 × n × (n-1), which is O(n²). The wall-clock duration of a frame is bounded by the slowest message in the slowest phase plus the deterministic transition cost.

Empirical measurements from the v0.3 reference implementation (Appendix A) running in-process mpsc on a single laptop, 500 frames per measurement:

| n  | wall-clock | frames/sec | per-frame messages |
|----|-----------:|-----------:|-------------------:|
| 2  |     0.194s |       2578 |                  4 |
| 3  |     0.437s |       1144 |                 12 |
| 4  |     0.795s |        629 |                 24 |
| 5  |     1.229s |        407 |                 40 |
| 7  |     2.393s |        209 |                 84 |
| 10 |     5.052s |         99 |                180 |

Throughput drops as 1/n² as predicted. At n=10, single-laptop throughput is below 100 frames/second, which is uncomfortable but not yet broken. At n=32 (extrapolating the curve), single-laptop throughput would be approximately 10 frames/second, which is the practical threshold for byte-denominated commerce. At n=64, throughput would be approximately 2.5 frames/second, which is too slow for general use but viable for low-throughput specialized clusters.

Real cluster operation does not run in-process on a single laptop. A cluster running over TCP across geographically-distributed machines has additional per-message latency (round-trip time across the network) but parallelizes the cryptographic and state-machine work across the participating machines. The substrate's reference implementation has been stress-tested at n=3 over real TCP for 10,000 frames at approximately 986 frames/second wall-clock — comparable to the in-process measurement at the same cluster size. This suggests that the geographic distribution adds latency but does not change the fundamental scaling shape.

The substrate's working assumption for v1.0:

- **Design target**: 32 nodes per cluster
- **Hard cap**: 64 nodes per cluster
- **Below 4 nodes**: not BFT-meaningful (§13.2); avoid via cluster admission and merging policies
- **Optimum**: 8-16 nodes (good BFT margin, manageable message overhead)

These numbers will tighten as v1.0+ runs at real cluster scale across diverse hardware. The treatise commits to revising them in subsequent versions as empirical data accumulates. The numbers are not architectural commitments; they are pragmatic operating ranges that the substrate's protocols are designed to handle.

### 11.2 Node admission

A cluster must control who joins it. Open admission is a Sybil-attack vector (§21): an adversary running n/3 fake nodes can exceed the BFT bound and corrupt the cluster's state. The substrate's admission protocol uses three gates, each independently necessary, all collectively required for admission.

**Gate one: cryptographic identity.** A candidate node generates an ed25519 keypair and presents the public key to the cluster. The pubkey becomes the node's permanent identity in the cluster. There is no recovery for a lost private key; a node whose key is compromised must rejoin under a new identity, with all admission gates re-traversed. This is intentional — making admission cheap to repeat would weaken the gate's Sybil resistance.

**Gate two: proof-of-resource shadow period.** The candidate runs alongside the cluster for a configurable period (default: 30 days) without participating in supermajority commits. During the shadow period, the candidate receives all the cluster's lockstep messages, processes them locally, computes its own state hashes, and reports them to the cluster. The cluster verifies that the candidate's hashes match the supermajority hash on every frame. A candidate that cannot keep up (slow hardware, unreliable network) or that produces divergent hashes (faulty implementation, malicious behavior) is rejected. The shadow period serves multiple functions: it filters out infrastructure that cannot meet the cluster's performance requirements, it surfaces implementation bugs before the candidate has voting power, and it imposes a real resource cost (running production-equivalent infrastructure for 30 days) that limits the rate at which Sybil attackers can spawn admission attempts.

**Gate three: existing-member sponsorship.** A candidate must be sponsored by at least two existing cluster members, who attest (with their own signatures) that they have evaluated the candidate's identity, infrastructure, and trustworthiness. Sponsorship is not a popularity vote; it is a reputational commitment. A sponsor whose sponsored candidate later misbehaves accumulates dissent-log weight (§14) and risks their own admission status in subsequent reviews. Sponsorship makes admission a peer-to-peer reputation flow rather than a centralized gatekeeping function.

The three gates compose into an admission protocol that:

- Has no central authority (no single party can admit or reject candidates)
- Resists Sybil attacks (running 30 days of infrastructure × 2 sponsors per Sybil node makes large-scale Sybil expensive)
- Filters poor implementations and operationally-weak candidates (the shadow period surfaces bugs and infrastructure problems)
- Aligns admission with cluster health (sponsorship creates reputational stakes that incentivize honest evaluation)
- Is auditable (every admission produces a content-addressed admission attestation visible to all members)

The substrate's admission gates are not magic. A determined adversary with enough capital can clear them by running 30 days of infrastructure and bribing two existing members. The gates raise the cost; they do not eliminate the threat. §21 (Cluster topology attacks) walks the residual threats.

### 11.3 Cluster lifecycle: splitting, merging, joining, departing

A cluster is not a static thing. Members join, members depart, the cluster grows past its sizing target, the cluster shrinks below the BFT-meaningful threshold. Each lifecycle event needs a protocol the substrate can execute deterministically. v0.3 implements admission and graceful departure. v0.4 must implement splitting, merging, and adversarial-departure handling.

#### 11.3.1 Cluster size and admission criteria

Beyond the §11.2 gates, the substrate uses two operational criteria to govern cluster size:

**Below n=4**: the cluster is not BFT-meaningful and must accept any qualified candidate immediately to grow. The §11.2 shadow period is reduced or waived in this regime, with the tradeoff that under-4 clusters have weaker Sybil defenses. Clusters operating at n=2 or n=3 should be treated as transient — either growing toward n=4 or merging with a neighbor cluster.

**Approaching n=64**: the cluster prepares to split (§11.3.2). New admission slows or pauses while the split protocol executes. The cluster does not refuse admission of qualified candidates outright; instead, it admits them and then splits, distributing members across the resulting clusters.

These are not arbitrary numbers. n=4 is the BFT threshold (§13.2). n=64 is the practical lockstep ceiling at v1.0 hardware levels. v0.4+ may revise the ceiling upward as cluster infrastructure becomes more capable.

#### 11.3.2 Cluster splitting protocol

When a cluster reaches the splitting threshold, it must divide into two clusters with a deterministic rule for which bytes go to which cluster. The substrate's design (not yet implemented in v0.3):

**Step 1: cluster-wide split decision.** The cluster's BFT supermajority commits a special `SplitFrame` decision — a frame in which no normal transitions are applied, only the split protocol's setup. The frame's content includes: the current member list, a deterministic randomness beacon (§11.3.4) drawn from all members' contributions, and a partition function specification.

**Step 2: byte partitioning.** Bytes are partitioned by the deterministic partition function applied to each byte's identity. The function takes the byte's identity hash, mixes in the randomness beacon, and outputs which of the two new clusters the byte belongs to. The function must be balanced (approximately equal byte-counts in each new cluster), deterministic (every node computes the same partition), and unpredictable (an adversary cannot game which cluster a specific byte ends up in by manipulating the byte's identity, because the randomness beacon is unknown until the SplitFrame).

**Step 3: member assignment.** Members are assigned to new clusters by similar deterministic rule, with the constraint that each new cluster has approximately n/2 members and meets the n≥4 BFT threshold. Members assigned to cluster A retain only the bytes that partitioned to cluster A; bytes that partitioned to cluster B are no longer the responsibility of cluster A members.

**Step 4: new cluster bootstrap.** Each new cluster begins with a fresh genesis state derived from the partitioned bytes. The cluster's initial state hash is the hash of the partitioned state. Both new clusters publish their initial state attestations to the substrate's gossip overlay, allowing external parties to discover them.

**Step 5: announcement.** Both new clusters announce their existence to the wider substrate, with cryptographic attestations linking them back to the parent cluster's SplitFrame. Cross-cluster references to bytes that lived in the parent cluster now resolve to one of the two new clusters via the partition function.

The split protocol's correctness depends on: (a) the randomness beacon being non-manipulable, (b) the partition function being deterministic and balanced, (c) all members agreeing on the SplitFrame's content. Each is a real engineering problem with known solutions; the substrate has not yet implemented them.

**v0.3 status:** not implemented. **v0.4 priority:** high. The split protocol is the substrate's primary scaling mechanism, and substrate adoption past a small handful of clusters requires it to work correctly.

#### 11.3.3 Cross-cluster byte transfer

A byte in cluster A whose new owner is in cluster B's jurisdiction must move from A to B. The substrate's design:

**Step 1: ownership transfer commits in cluster A.** The current owner of the byte authorizes a transfer to the new owner's pubkey. Cluster A's lockstep commits the transfer in the normal way (§9.4). The transfer's transition includes a special flag indicating the new owner is in cluster B's jurisdiction.

**Step 2: cluster A produces an export attestation.** Cluster A's BFT supermajority signs a content-addressed attestation containing: the byte's full history (origin attestation plus all transitions), the byte's current state, and a "forwarded to cluster B at frame N" notation. The attestation is published to the substrate's gossip overlay and pushed directly to cluster B.

**Step 3: cluster B verifies and imports.** Cluster B's nodes independently verify the byte's full history (§10.2) and verify cluster A's BFT-supermajority signature on the export attestation. If verification succeeds, cluster B's lockstep commits an import frame that adds the byte to cluster B's state.

**Step 4: cluster A retires the byte.** Cluster A's state no longer includes the byte. Subsequent transfers of the byte happen in cluster B. Cluster A retains the byte's history (for forensic purposes) but does not commit further transitions on it.

**Step 5: external references resolve to cluster B.** Any party with the byte's identity who queries the substrate's gossip overlay is directed to cluster B. The transfer is complete.

Cross-cluster transfers are slower than intra-cluster transfers (multiple frames in two clusters plus gossip propagation, on the order of 10 seconds end-to-end versus sub-second intra-cluster) but they are not common: most transfers are intra-cluster, and cross-cluster transfer is the exception used when ownership crosses cluster boundaries.

**v0.3 status:** not implemented. **v0.4 priority:** high. Cross-cluster transfers are required for federated operation.

#### 11.3.4 Cluster-level Byzantine model

A cluster can itself be Byzantine — not in the sense that ⌊(n-1)/3⌋ of its members are dishonest (which the BFT supermajority handles), but in the sense that the cluster's *supermajority* is colluding to misrepresent state to outside parties. A cluster whose entire membership is run by a single adversary can, in principle, sign export attestations for bytes that don't exist, forge histories, or refuse to honor legitimate transfer requests.

The substrate's defenses:

**Defense one: content-addressed history independent verification.** Cluster B's import procedure (§11.3.3 step 3) verifies the byte's history end-to-end via §10.2. A malicious cluster A cannot forge a history that verifies correctly without compromising every owner's private key in the chain. The import protocol does not extend trust to cluster A's testimony about deep history; it only extends trust to cluster A's testimony about the byte's *current* state at the moment of export, which is signed by cluster A's BFT supermajority and is cryptographically scoped.

**Defense two: cross-cluster attestation diversity.** Bytes that are valuable enough to attract adversarial attention are typically transferred multiple times across multiple clusters during their lifetime. Each cluster that has handled the byte produces attestations that are visible to the substrate's gossip overlay. A malicious cluster A claiming to have attested a byte that other clusters never saw can be detected by querying the gossip overlay for the byte's history of cluster touches.

**Defense three: cluster reputation accumulation.** A cluster that has been operating honestly for a long period, sponsored multiple successful admissions, and produced no anomalies in cross-cluster transfers accumulates reputation. A new cluster, or one with a history of anomalies, is treated with more caution by other clusters considering imports from it. Reputation is tracked via the dissent-log mechanism (§14) extended to cluster-level events.

**Defense four: cluster expulsion.** A cluster that is determined to be malicious can be expelled from the substrate by some quorum of honest clusters. Expelled clusters' attestations are no longer honored; bytes resident in expelled clusters are treated as orphaned (their history remains valid but their current state is unverifiable, requiring intervention by a cluster willing to import them with extra verification). The expulsion protocol's design is taken up in §22 (Issuer attacks) and §25 (Social attacks on the substrate itself); v0.3 does not implement it.

**v0.3 status:** intra-cluster Byzantine handling is implemented and tested (§13). Cluster-level Byzantine handling is design-only.

#### 11.3.5 Phase buffering — implemented in v0.3

The substrate's lockstep protocol requires receive-side message buffering at any cluster size n>2 to avoid a silent deadlock that occurs when a fast node advances to frame N+1 while a slow peer is still in frame N's state-hash phase. §12 specifies the deadlock, the fix, and the lessons in detail. The mention here, in the cluster topology section, is to flag that phase buffering is part of cluster topology's load-bearing protocols, not just a transport implementation detail. The reference implementation's `Node::recv_filtered` was added after the v0.3 testing surfaced this bug; without it, any cluster of n>2 nodes deadlocks within milliseconds.

The meta-claim this surfaces: distributed-systems prose at the architectural-doc level routinely skips over implementation details that turn out to be load-bearing. The cheapest way to find them is to write code, not more architecture. v0.4 work on §11.3.2 (splitting), §11.3.3 (cross-cluster transfer), and §11.3.4 (cluster-level Byzantine) will surface analogous implementation details that this prose cannot anticipate.

#### 11.3.6 Graceful departure and unexpected loss

A node that wants to leave a cluster gracefully (planned maintenance, hardware retirement, voluntary exit) submits a `DepartureNotice` input to the cluster's lockstep. The cluster's BFT supermajority commits the notice and the departing node ceases participation after the committed frame. The cluster's effective n decreases by one. If the resulting n drops below 4, the cluster either pauses admission of new bytes and seeks merger with a neighboring cluster, or expedites admission of pending candidates.

A node that disappears unexpectedly (crash, network partition, prolonged unavailability) is detected by the cluster's heartbeat mechanism. After a configurable timeout, the cluster commits a `MissingNode` frame that excludes the absent node from the supermajority count. The absent node, on rejoining, executes the recovery procedure (§16) — load checkpoint, replay log, optionally state-sync from honest peers if its own state is suspect — and re-enters participation.

Both protocols depend on the cluster's persistence layer (§16) being robust enough that node loss is recoverable from local state plus replay, with minimal need for state-sync from other members. The v0.3 reference implementation handles graceful departure adequately and unexpected loss adequately for non-Byzantine cases. Adversarial loss (a node that disappears with intent to corrupt state and rejoin under a different identity) is v0.4 work.

### 11.4 Substrate-level topology

The substrate's overall topology is a federation of clusters connected by a gossip overlay that propagates cross-cluster attestations, cluster-state snapshots, and announcements. The gossip overlay is not itself a cluster; it is a peer-to-peer network in which any substrate node can participate, propagating signed attestations from its connected clusters to peers in other parts of the substrate.

The gossip overlay's protocol is intentionally simple: every node maintains a small number of peer connections, gossips received attestations to its peers (with deduplication via content-addressing), and accepts queries for bytes by identity. Nodes may specialize — some nodes hold more byte histories than others, some are gateway nodes between geographic regions, some are read-only clients that don't participate in any cluster. The substrate's federation is therefore not a uniform network but a heterogeneous overlay where each node's role is determined by its operator's choices.

This is the same pattern as the internet's overlay structure: routers, servers, peers, edge devices, all participating in the same substrate at different scales of involvement. The architectural pattern is well-tested; the substrate borrows it unchanged.

### 11.5 What §11 does not yet specify

The treatise's discipline requires naming what is design-only.

**Cluster splitting (§11.3.2)**: design-only. v0.3 does not implement it. v0.4 priority: high.

**Cross-cluster byte transfer (§11.3.3)**: design-only. v0.3 does not implement it. v0.4 priority: high.

**Cluster-level Byzantine handling (§11.3.4)**: design-only. v0.3 implements intra-cluster Byzantine via supermajority commit; cluster-level expulsion is v0.4.

**Cluster merging**: not specified. The opposite of splitting (when two clusters' workloads decline and merging is more efficient than running both separately). Will be specified in v0.4 alongside splitting.

**Adversarial node loss**: not fully specified. v0.3 handles unexpected loss for non-Byzantine cases; cases involving an adversary deliberately disconnecting and rejoining under a different identity require additional work.

**Cluster-state snapshots for external verifiers**: mentioned in passing (§10.2 step 3). Not yet specified as a protocol. External verifiers need a mechanism to obtain a trustworthy state snapshot at a specified frame; the snapshot is signed by the cluster's BFT supermajority and indexed in the gossip overlay. v0.4 specification.

These five items are the substrate's most important v0.4 work in cluster topology. Each is a real engineering problem with known approaches; none is open-research-grade hard. The treatise commits to specifying all five in v0.4 of the substrate's specification documents.

The substrate's claim to scale through federation is therefore partially demonstrated and partially deferred. Single-cluster operation is implemented and tested at n=2..10 in-process and at n=3 over real TCP for 10K frames. Multi-cluster operation is design-only. The treatise's honesty discipline names the gap. The reference implementation is the existence proof for what works; the v0.4 specifications will be the existence proofs for the rest.

The next section (§12) walks the phase-buffering requirement in more detail, because while it was named in §11.3.5, it deserves its own subsection-level treatment as a load-bearing protocol detail. §13 specifies BFT supermajority commit. §14 specifies the dissent log. §15-§17 build out the remaining architectural components. By the close of Part II, the architectural picture is complete and the threat model in Part III becomes the natural next concern.
## §12. The phase buffering requirement

§11.3.5 named phase buffering as a load-bearing protocol detail. This section gives it the dedicated treatment its centrality requires. Phase buffering is not optional. It is not a performance optimization. It is a correctness requirement at any cluster size greater than two, and a lockstep implementation that omits it deadlocks within a few frames under any realistic operating conditions.

### 12.1 The deadlock

Lockstep proceeds in two phases per frame: input exchange (§9.4 steps 2-3) and state-hash exchange (§9.4 step 6). With unbounded message channels and asynchronous nodes, the phases are not synchronized across nodes. A fast node can complete frame N's state-hash phase, increment its frame counter, and begin frame N+1's input-broadcast phase while a slower peer is still processing frame N's state-hash phase.

Concretely: node A finishes frame N at wall-clock time T. Node A immediately begins frame N+1, which means broadcasting `InputBatch{from: A, frame: N+1, ...}` to all peers. Node B receives this message at time T + ε while still in frame N's state-hash phase, where it is expecting `StateHash{from: A, frame: N, ...}` messages.

A naive receive loop responds to the unexpected message type by returning a protocol error — "expected StateHash, received InputBatch." The thread carrying node B dies. Other nodes block forever waiting for B's StateHash{N} message. The cluster deadlocks silently. No error message is logged. No alert fires. The processes are simply hung.

The deadlock is not theoretical. The v0.3 reference implementation reproduced it within milliseconds at n=3 in the first test run before phase buffering was implemented. Any realistic n>2 cluster running asynchronous nodes over any non-deterministic transport (TCP, UDP, mpsc channels with bounded queues) will encounter the deadlock immediately.

### 12.2 The fix

The receive loop must accept any incoming message and route it to one of two destinations: process-now, or buffer-for-later. Messages matching the current expected (phase, frame, peer) tuple are processed immediately. Messages that don't match are pushed onto a per-node buffer. Subsequent phases consult the buffer first, before reading from the transport.

The fix is small in code (the v0.3 reference implementation's `Node::recv_filtered` is approximately 40 lines of Rust) but it is conceptually load-bearing. The receive loop must be willing to accept messages from "the future" — messages for the next frame, while still processing the current frame — and to delay processing them until the appropriate phase begins.

The buffer is bounded. Lockstep guarantees no node is ever more than one frame ahead of any other node, because frame N+1's processing requires N+1 InputBatch messages from N+1 peers, which requires those peers to have completed frame N. The buffer therefore holds at most one frame's worth of messages per peer. At n=64, that is 64 buffered messages in the worst case — trivially small.

### 12.3 Why this is not a layer-7 concern

It is tempting to argue that phase buffering is a transport-layer or application-framework concern, not a protocol concern. The argument is wrong, and the substrate's specification names buffering at the protocol level for two reasons.

**Reason one: implementations in different languages must all do this.** A Rust implementation that buffers and a Python implementation that doesn't will deadlock when they communicate, even though both are correctly implementing every other aspect of the protocol. The buffering requirement must be specified at the protocol level so that conforming implementations agree on the behavior.

**Reason two: the deadlock is silent.** Phase buffering is not the kind of bug that surfaces with a useful error message. The cluster simply stops making progress. An implementer who has not been warned that phase buffering is required will spend hours debugging "the cluster is hung" before discovering the cause. The treatise names the requirement explicitly so that no implementer has to rediscover it.

These two reasons together justify treating phase buffering as a first-class protocol concern. The substrate's conformance specification (the document accompanying this treatise) gives the buffering algorithm in formal pseudocode. Implementations that omit it are non-conforming, even if they appear to work in single-node tests.

### 12.4 What phase buffering does not solve

Phase buffering handles asynchronous node speed differences within the lockstep protocol's bounds. It does not handle:

**Network partitions.** A node that becomes unreachable for many frames is not "running ahead"; it is gone. The buffer cannot extend past one frame because the protocol assumes lockstep guarantees node progress within bounded relative speed. Network partitions are handled by the heartbeat-and-MissingNode mechanism (§11.3.6), not by buffering.

**Adversarial speed manipulation.** A node deliberately running slowly to exploit phase buffering for some adversarial purpose is bounded by the BFT supermajority — the cluster proceeds without the slow node's vote once supermajority is reached without it. Phase buffering does not defend against adversarial speed; the BFT commit does.

**Message loss.** A message that never arrives (dropped by the transport, lost in transit) does not get buffered; it never enters the buffer system. The transport layer is responsible for reliable delivery within the lockstep protocol's frame budget. The substrate's TCP transport handles reliability via TCP's standard mechanisms; the in-process mpsc transport is reliable by construction.

**Message reordering across peers.** Phase buffering correctly handles temporal reordering from a single peer (peer X's message for frame N+1 arriving before peer Y's message for frame N is fine — buffer X, process Y, then process X). It does not handle the case where peer X sends two messages for frame N in a different order than the protocol expects; that is an input-ordering concern (§9.4 step 4) handled by canonical hash sort.

These four exclusions are named so the reader does not over-credit phase buffering with capabilities it lacks. Buffering is a specific fix for a specific protocol-level race condition; other failure modes have other defenses.

### 12.5 The meta-lesson, retained

§11.3.5 already named the meta-lesson: distributed-systems prose at the architectural-doc level routinely skips over implementation details that turn out to be load-bearing. Phase buffering is the v0.3 reference implementation's clearest example. The bug took 50 milliseconds of running code to surface and would have been invisible from any amount of additional architectural prose. The treatise's authors do not claim to have anticipated phase buffering before writing the implementation; they claim to have *fixed* it after the implementation surfaced it, and to have updated the treatise to name it as a first-class concern.

The meta-lesson generalizes to every part of Part II that has not yet been implemented in code — particularly the cluster-splitting protocol (§11.3.2), cross-cluster transfer (§11.3.3), and cluster-level Byzantine handling (§11.3.4). Each of these will surface implementation details that this prose does not predict. The substrate's discipline is to ship implementations of each as v0.4 work and to update the treatise as the implementations surface what the prose missed. Treatises that do not get updated by their own implementations are unreliable; the substrate's treatise commits to revision discipline.

The next section (§13) specifies BFT supermajority commit in detail, including the n=3 BFT exception that the v0.3 implementation surfaced. The pattern continues: prose specifies what the protocol should do, code surfaces what the prose missed, treatise gets revised. By the close of Part II, the architectural picture covers every primitive the substrate composes, with explicit acknowledgment of where v0.4 work will surface details v1.0 cannot anticipate.
## §13. BFT supermajority commit

The substrate commits each frame iff at least ⌊2n/3⌋+1 nodes (out of n total in the cluster) agree on the post-frame state hash. This is the standard Byzantine Fault Tolerance threshold, and it tolerates up to ⌊(n-1)/3⌋ Byzantine nodes — nodes that may be arbitrarily faulty, including malicious, including coordinated. The BFT bound is well-known and the substrate inherits it from forty years of distributed-systems research; the substrate's contribution in §13 is to specify *how* the supermajority commit composes with lockstep simulation, what the failure modes are, and what v0.3 implementation testing surfaced about the BFT bound's behavior at small cluster sizes.

This section walks the supermajority commit protocol step by step, specifies the four outcomes a frame can reach, addresses the n=3 BFT exception that v0.3 testing made empirical rather than theoretical, and names what the supermajority commit does *not* defend against. By the close of §13, the reader should understand BFT's specific role in the substrate's defense in depth, what attacks it stops, and what attacks the substrate's other layers (dissent log, issuer governance, cluster topology) must handle instead.

### 13.1 The supermajority threshold

For a cluster of size n, the supermajority threshold is ⌊2n/3⌋+1. Equivalent expressions: "more than two-thirds," "the smallest integer greater than 2n/3," and "n - ⌊(n-1)/3⌋" (the count of honest nodes that must agree, given that ⌊(n-1)/3⌋ may be Byzantine).

The threshold table for small clusters:

| n | supermajority | tolerable Byzantine f | BFT-meaningful? |
|---|---:|---:|---|
| 2 | 2 | 0 | no |
| 3 | 3 | 0 | no |
| 4 | 3 | 1 | yes |
| 5 | 4 | 1 | yes |
| 6 | 5 | 1 | yes |
| 7 | 5 | 2 | yes |
| 10 | 7 | 3 | yes |
| 16 | 11 | 5 | yes |
| 32 | 22 | 10 | yes |
| 64 | 43 | 21 | yes |

The BFT-meaningful threshold is n=4. At n=2 and n=3, the formula evaluates such that any Byzantine node prevents commits — the cluster can detect divergence but cannot tolerate it. Cluster admission policies (§11.3.1) accordingly treat clusters below n=4 as transient: either growing toward n=4 or merging with a neighbor.

The threshold's correctness derives from a standard pigeonhole argument: if at most f nodes are Byzantine, then at least n-f are honest; for the cluster to commit, the supermajority must include at least one honest node's vote (preventing the Byzantine subset from forging a commit alone) and must exclude any single Byzantine subset's ability to block (preventing Byzantine nodes from preventing legitimate commits). The bound ⌊2n/3⌋+1 is the smallest threshold that satisfies both constraints when f ≤ ⌊(n-1)/3⌋.

### 13.2 The n=3 BFT exception

The v0.3 reference implementation's empirical testing surfaced the n=3 BFT exception as a specific protocol behavior worth naming. At n=3, the supermajority threshold is 3 (not 2), which means *all three nodes must agree* for a commit. A cluster with one Byzantine node at n=3 produces a 2-vs-1 hash split — two honest nodes report hash X, the Byzantine node reports hash Y — and no hash reaches supermajority. Honest nodes halt with `CLUSTER SPLIT`. The cluster cannot make progress.

This is correct BFT behavior: a 3-node cluster has no way to know which side of a 2-vs-1 split is the honest side without an external referee, which the substrate does not have. Honest nodes cannot proceed because doing so would risk committing the Byzantine state if the Byzantine node happened to be in the majority of two. The safe behavior is to halt.

Architecturally, this means **3-node clusters provide divergence detection, not Byzantine fault tolerance.** A 3-node cluster will detect that something is wrong (the cluster halts), but it cannot recover automatically. Recovery requires either operator intervention (identifying which node is faulty and removing it, then admitting a replacement to reach n=4) or merging with a neighboring cluster.

The implication for cluster admission: clusters that drop below n=4 are emergency states, not stable operating states. The §11.3.1 admission policy treats below-n=4 clusters with reduced shadow-period requirements precisely to grow them out of the BFT-degenerate regime as quickly as possible.

The v0.3 implementation tested this behavior explicitly. The `n3_one_liar_causes_cluster_split_for_honest_nodes` test (Appendix A) verifies that a 3-node cluster with one Byzantine node halts with `CLUSTER SPLIT` rather than proceeding. The test is small but the architectural lesson it embeds is significant: BFT protocols at small cluster sizes have non-obvious behavior that empirical testing surfaces and that pure-prose architecture documents may miss. The v0.2 architecture document treated "small cluster" as a working assumption without noting the n=3 discontinuity. The v0.3 implementation surfaced it. The treatise documents it. This is the iteration discipline §12.5 named.

### 13.3 The four frame outcomes

Each frame reaches one of four outcomes during the supermajority commit phase:

**Outcome one: clean commit.** Supermajority agreement on a hash, and the local node's hash matches. The frame is committed, the local node's state advances, and the protocol proceeds to frame N+1. This is the normal case and represents nearly all frames in a healthy cluster.

**Outcome two: commit with dissent.** Supermajority agreement on a hash, the local node's hash matches, but some peers reported divergent hashes. The frame is committed normally, and the dissenting peers are recorded in the local node's `dissent_log` (§14). The cluster proceeds, and the dissent-log accumulation is the mechanism by which persistent misbehavers are eventually identified and expelled. This case is the BFT bound working as designed: the cluster tolerates the divergence and continues.

**Outcome three: minority divergence.** Supermajority agreement on some hash, but the local node's hash does not match. The local node's state has diverged from the cluster supermajority. The local node halts with `MINORITY DIVERGENCE` and does not advance to frame N+1. Recovery requires state-sync from honest peers (v0.4 work; v0.3 nodes simply halt and require operator intervention).

**Outcome four: cluster split.** No hash reaches supermajority. This indicates either (a) more than ⌊(n-1)/3⌋ Byzantine nodes in the cluster, exceeding the BFT bound, or (b) a network partition that has split the cluster into two sub-quorums neither of which can independently reach supermajority. The cluster halts with `CLUSTER SPLIT` and progress is impossible until the partition is resolved or the Byzantine excess is identified and removed.

The four outcomes are exhaustive: every frame produces exactly one. The protocol's correctness depends on each node reaching the same outcome (every honest node sees the same supermajority tally), which the protocol guarantees via deterministic input ordering and identical state transition.

### 13.4 The MINORITY DIVERGENCE path: dead code for transport adversaries

The v0.3 reference implementation's testing surfaced a second BFT-related lesson worth naming. The `MINORITY DIVERGENCE` error path (outcome three above) is essentially **dead code for transport-layer adversaries**.

The intent of the path: a node whose internal state has corrupted detects this immediately by finding its own hash rejected by the supermajority. The intent is correct for the failure mode it was designed for — a node that genuinely has a corrupt internal state benefits from discovering this during the supermajority commit and halting before doing further damage.

In testing with transport-layer adversaries — adversaries that lie about their *outbound* state hash messages while running an honest internal state machine — the path turned out not to fire. A node lying about its broadcast hash sees: my own honest hash + (n-1) honest peers reporting honest hashes = unanimous agreement. The node's own internal hash is in the supermajority. The path does not trigger. The node commits successfully and is unaware that it broadcast garbage.

The substrate's *actual* misbehavior signal for transport-layer attacks is the dissent_log (§14), accumulated by *other* nodes who observed the lying node's broadcast diverge from the supermajority hash. The lying node's own behavior remains internally consistent; its lie is detected by its peers, not by itself.

The lesson: **error paths designed in the abstract for "obvious" failure modes often don't fire in the empirical failure modes that actually occur.** The architecture's load-bearing observability primitive is the dissent_log, not the MINORITY DIVERGENCE halt. The treatise names this honestly because the design history matters: if a future implementer sees `MINORITY DIVERGENCE` in the reference code and assumes it is the substrate's primary defense, they will misunderstand what the substrate actually relies on.

The path is preserved in the v0.3 reference implementation because it does correctly handle the case where a node's *internal* state has actually corrupted (memory error, deterministic-replay drift, fixed-point library bug). That case is rare but real; the path's existence is justified. The path's status as "primary defense" is what is wrong, not the path's existence.

### 13.5 The CLUSTER SPLIT path: when Byzantine excess or partition occurs

Outcome four — no hash reaches supermajority — indicates either Byzantine excess or network partition. The substrate cannot distinguish between these two causes from supermajority-commit information alone. Both are handled by the same response: halt the cluster and require external resolution.

For network partitions, the resolution is automatic when the partition heals. Nodes that were unreachable rejoin via the §11.3.6 unexpected-loss recovery (heartbeat detection, MissingNode commit, persistence-and-replay rejoin). Once enough nodes are reachable to form a supermajority, the cluster resumes.

For Byzantine excess, the resolution requires identifying and removing the excess Byzantine nodes. This is operator work in v0.3 (cluster operators inspect the dissent_log, identify persistent misbehavers, remove them via cluster-admission revocation). v0.4 will add automated expulsion for clusters whose dissent-log evidence reaches a confidence threshold.

A pathological case worth naming: a network partition that produces *two* sub-quorums, each with enough nodes to almost reach supermajority, but neither quite reaching it. Both sub-quorums halt with `CLUSTER SPLIT`. Neither can make progress. When the partition heals, the cluster resumes, but during the partition the cluster is effectively offline. This is correct BFT behavior — making progress in either sub-quorum during the partition would risk committing different states in the two sub-quorums, which would produce permanent divergence on partition heal — but it is a real availability cost that operators must factor into cluster sizing and geographic distribution.

The architectural mitigation: cluster topology should distribute nodes such that no single network failure mode (datacenter outage, regional internet failure, BGP hijack) puts more than ⌊(n-1)/3⌋ nodes on the wrong side of a partition. For an n=10 cluster tolerating f=3, that means no more than 3 nodes per failure domain. For n=64 tolerating f=21, 21 nodes per failure domain — generous but not unlimited. Geographic distribution is the substrate's primary defense against partition-induced availability loss, and clusters that operate entirely within a single AWS region or a single ISP's network have weaker partition resistance.

### 13.6 Composition with the rest of the architecture

BFT supermajority commit is the substrate's primary defense against Byzantine adversaries within a cluster. It composes with several other mechanisms in the architecture, each handling a class of threats that BFT alone does not address.

**Cryptographic verification of inputs (§8.4)** prevents adversaries from forging individual transitions. BFT handles the case where adversaries control multiple nodes and try to commit invalid state; cryptographic verification handles the case where an adversary is trying to forge a single transition's signature.

**Content-addressed history (§10)** provides defense in depth against cluster-level Byzantine collusion. Even if an entire cluster's supermajority is malicious, external parties can verify any byte's history independently from the chain, and cross-cluster transfers (§11.3.3) verify the chain rather than trusting the source cluster's testimony.
