**Dissent log accumulation (§14)** provides the long-running misbehavior signal that BFT alone does not produce. BFT tolerates ⌊(n-1)/3⌋ Byzantine nodes per frame; the dissent log identifies which specific nodes have been Byzantine across many frames and provides the basis for expulsion.

**Cluster admission gates (§11.2)** raise the cost of Sybil attacks against the BFT bound. An adversary attempting to control ⌊n/3⌋+1 nodes (exceeding the BFT bound) must clear the admission gates for each, which is expensive in shadow-period infrastructure and sponsorship recruitment.

**Cross-cluster verification (§11.3.4)** prevents a malicious cluster from infecting the broader substrate. Even if cluster A's BFT supermajority is colluding, cluster B verifies imports independently via the chain, and the substrate's gossip overlay surfaces inconsistencies between cluster A's claims and other clusters' records.

These five mechanisms compose into the substrate's defense in depth: BFT handles the per-frame Byzantine bound, cryptographic verification handles per-input forgery, content-addressed history handles cluster-level collusion at the byte level, dissent log handles long-running misbehavior identification, and cluster admission raises the cost of Sybil attacks. Each mechanism is necessary; no single mechanism is sufficient.

### 13.7 What BFT supermajority commit does not defend against

The discipline of naming what is *not* defended remains.

**Adversaries who control more than ⌊(n-1)/3⌋ of the cluster.** This is the hard BFT bound. An adversary at this scale can commit forged state. The substrate's response is to make achieving this scale expensive (cluster admission, sponsorship requirements, shadow periods) and to detect Byzantine clusters at the federation level (§11.3.4). At the cluster's BFT-supermajority level, the adversary wins. This is the same fundamental limit every BFT system has; the substrate does not transcend it.

**Adversaries who control the cryptographic primitives.** A cryptographic break in ed25519 or blake3 — a real preimage attack on blake3, a signature forgery against ed25519 — defeats the substrate's BFT layer because the attacker can forge state hashes that any honest verifier would accept. The substrate's response is migration to a successor primitive, which is a substrate-wide event (§19). Until such a break occurs, the substrate inherits the cryptographic primitives' security; if such a break occurs, the substrate must migrate.

**Adversaries who control the cluster's input distribution.** A node that controls *which transactions enter the cluster* (by being the only node clients can submit to, or by selectively dropping submissions) can selectively prevent commit of inputs without breaking BFT. The transactions never enter the input batch, so the BFT supermajority sees nothing to vote on. Defenses include diverse input-submission paths (clients submit to multiple nodes), gossip-based input replication (input received by any node is propagated to all peers before the next frame), and adversarial-input-collector identification via dissent log analysis. v0.3 implements basic gossip input replication; richer defenses are v0.4 work.

**Cluster-level Byzantine collusion** (entire supermajority colluding). Handled by content-addressed history and cross-cluster verification, as named above. BFT supermajority itself does not defend against this; the substrate's defenses are at higher levels.

**Application-layer fraud.** A user phished into authorizing a transfer signs a valid transition. BFT correctly commits the transition because the cryptographic verification passes. The fraud is at the application layer, and BFT has no capacity to detect intention. Defenses are at the application layer (anomaly detection, social-recovery, dispute resolution services) and the substrate is intentionally silent on them.

These five exclusions name the boundaries of BFT's contribution. The treatise's discipline is to be precise about each defense's scope so that the substrate's defense in depth is legible and not over-credited to any single mechanism.

The next section (§14) specifies the dissent log in detail. The dissent log is the substrate's actual misbehavior signal for transport-layer adversaries (§13.4) and the foundation that long-running misbehavior detection and cluster expulsion build on. By the close of §14, the BFT-related mechanisms in Part II are complete, and §15-§17 build out the remaining architectural components (live issuance, persistence, transports).
## §14. The dissent log

The dissent log is the substrate's misbehavior signal. §13.4 named it as the load-bearing primitive: the actual defense against transport-layer Byzantine adversaries, accumulated cumulatively across many frames, used by the cluster to identify persistent misbehavers and (in v0.4+) drive expulsion. This section specifies what the dissent log records, how it is populated, what it is used for, and why it is structurally a better misbehavior signal than per-frame error halts.

By the close of §14, the reader should understand why the substrate watches *patterns over time* rather than reacting to *individual divergences*, and why this is the architecturally correct response to real-world adversaries who lie selectively rather than continuously.

### 14.1 What the dissent log records

Each node maintains a local dissent log: a per-cluster, append-only record of frames in which one or more peers' state hashes diverged from the supermajority hash. The structure is small.

Each entry contains:

- **Frame number.** The frame at which the dissent was observed.
- **Dissenting peer set.** The pubkey(s) of nodes whose reported state hash did not match the committed supermajority hash for this frame.
- **Local node's hash.** The hash this node computed for the frame, for cross-reference and audit.
- **Supermajority hash.** The hash that won the supermajority vote. (May equal local node's hash, in which case the local node is in the supermajority. May differ, in which case the local node is in the minority — but per §13.3 outcome three, this case halts the node, so dissent log entries from a still-running node always have local hash = supermajority hash.)

Each node's dissent log is its own. Two nodes in the same cluster may have slightly different dissent logs depending on which peers' messages each received and processed in time, but for committed frames the union of dissent logs across honest nodes converges quickly. The substrate does not require dissent logs to be consensus-replicated; each node's local log is sufficient for the log's primary use cases.

The log is never truncated automatically. Long-running clusters accumulate large logs over time, and operators must decide their own retention policies. v0.3 does not specify retention; v0.4 will (likely "retain N most recent entries plus a content-addressed checkpoint of older entries").

### 14.2 How dissent log entries are populated

§13.3 outcome two — "commit with dissent" — is the populating event. A frame committed by supermajority but with one or more peers reporting divergent hashes produces a dissent log entry on every honest node that observed the divergence. The entry is written deterministically: any honest node observing the same supermajority tally writes the same entry.

The deterministic property matters. If dissent log writes were nondeterministic, different honest nodes would have different dissent logs, and any aggregation across nodes (for expulsion decisions, for auditor inspection) would be ambiguous. By making the writes deterministic — same supermajority tally produces same entry — the substrate ensures that honest nodes converge on identical dissent observations.

The peers populating the log do not have to be in the cluster's BFT-tolerable Byzantine count; the log records dissent regardless of count. A frame with f=⌊(n-1)/3⌋ Byzantine nodes producing a 2/3-supermajority commit still produces a dissent log entry naming each Byzantine peer. This is what allows the cluster to identify *who* is misbehaving even when the supermajority is reached.

### 14.3 What the dissent log is used for

The dissent log enables three operational responses that no per-frame mechanism alone provides.

**Persistent misbehaver identification.** A peer whose pubkey appears in many dissent log entries over time is a persistent misbehaver. The substrate's dissent log makes this identification numerical: a peer that appears in 0% of log entries is honest; a peer that appears in 1% is faulty (occasional bad transmission, transient bug); a peer that appears in 50%+ is malicious. The thresholds are operator-tunable, but the signal is unambiguous: dissent frequency is the substrate's quantitative measure of misbehavior.

**Cluster expulsion (v0.4 work).** When a peer's dissent frequency exceeds an operator-configured threshold, the cluster initiates expulsion via a special `Expulsion` input that requires BFT supermajority commit. The expelled peer is removed from the cluster's membership, its admission slot is freed, and its sponsor (per §11.2 gate three) accumulates reputation cost. Expulsion is a normal cluster operation, not an emergency response.

**Cluster-level reputation.** Aggregating dissent across many clusters produces a substrate-wide misbehavior map. A cluster whose nodes accumulate dissent across multiple peer clusters is a cluster with operational problems (or hostile intent). The substrate's gossip overlay (§11.4) can carry dissent attestations between clusters, allowing higher-layer reputation systems to score clusters and the operators behind them. This is design-only in v0.3; v0.4 will specify the gossip protocol for dissent attestations.

These three responses are graduated. Per-frame BFT supermajority handles each frame in isolation. Dissent log accumulation handles patterns over many frames. Cluster expulsion handles persistent misbehavior at the membership level. Cluster-level reputation handles systemic problems at the federation level. Each layer responds at its appropriate timescale, from milliseconds (BFT) to hours (expulsion) to days (reputation).

### 14.4 Why patterns over time are the right signal

The substrate could have used per-frame error halts as its primary misbehavior signal — any divergence triggers a halt, operators investigate. This is the design v0.3 originally specified before testing surfaced the dead `MINORITY DIVERGENCE` path (§13.4). The pattern-over-time signal is structurally better for three reasons.

**Reason one: real adversaries lie selectively.** A node that lies on every frame is easy to detect and easy to ignore. A node that lies on 1% of frames, choosing the frames carefully, is harder to detect from any single frame and may have specific attack goals (corrupting a specific transaction, stalling a specific issuer, biasing input ordering against a specific user). Per-frame halts do not distinguish between random transient errors and selective adversarial behavior. Pattern observation does.

**Reason two: false positives have real costs.** A cluster that halts on every divergence is operationally fragile. Network glitches, transient bugs, and brief operator errors all produce occasional divergences that are not malicious. Halting the entire cluster for each one converts every transient hiccup into a cluster-wide outage. Pattern-based response tolerates the noise floor while flagging the signal.

**Reason three: misbehavers learn.** An adversary whose first lie causes a cluster halt knows immediately that they have been caught and can adapt their attack. An adversary whose lies are silently logged but produce no immediate response may continue lying indefinitely, accumulating evidence that the cluster uses to identify them with high confidence before acting. The slower response is the more reliable response.

The substrate's choice — pattern over time, dissent log accumulation, graduated response — reflects forty years of distributed-systems experience with adversarial environments. It is the same choice cybersecurity systems make (security event logs accumulate; alerts fire on patterns, not individual events) and the same choice anti-fraud systems make (transactions are scored on patterns, not individual flags). The substrate inherits the discipline.

### 14.5 What the dissent log does not provide

**The dissent log does not prove malice.** A peer in the log may be malicious, may be faulty, may be running on slow hardware, may have intermittent network issues, may have a bug in its implementation. The log records *divergence*, not *intent*. Operators interpreting the log must combine it with other signals (peer's reputation, hardware diagnostics, commit history) to distinguish between cause classes. The substrate provides the data; humans interpret.

**The dissent log does not handle adversaries who never get caught.** A peer whose hash always matches the supermajority — even when the peer is technically Byzantine, because it happens to agree with whatever the honest majority decides — never appears in the dissent log. The log captures only *visible* misbehavior. Invisible misbehavior (a Byzantine peer that happens to agree on every frame) is not detected, and the substrate's response is the same as for any other invisible problem: it cannot be detected, so it cannot be acted on. The BFT bound's tolerance is what makes invisible Byzantine behavior tolerable: even if such a peer exists, they cannot prevent commits or corrupt state alone.

**The dissent log does not aggregate across clusters automatically.** Each cluster's log is local. Cross-cluster reputation (§14.3) requires the gossip overlay to propagate dissent attestations, which is v0.4 work. Until that propagation is implemented, each cluster's expulsion decisions are based only on local evidence.

**The dissent log does not handle compromised loggers.** A node whose dissent log is itself corrupt (the node is logging false dissents, or failing to log real ones) is a misbehavior the substrate's BFT supermajority commit handles only weakly: the corrupt logger's *votes* are still subject to supermajority, but its *log entries* are local and not consensus-replicated. The substrate's response to this attack class is at the cluster admission and reputation layers (§11.2, §14.3) rather than at the log layer itself. Compromised logger detection is genuinely v0.4 work, not yet specified.

These four exclusions name the dissent log's actual scope. The treatise's discipline is to be precise: the dissent log is the load-bearing observability primitive for transport-layer Byzantine misbehavior in BFT clusters. It is not a complete misbehavior detection system, and the substrate composes it with other mechanisms to handle the threats it does not.

The next section (§15) specifies live issuance — how authorized issuers mint new bytes during runtime, how the issuer registry is maintained, and how the issuance flow composes with the BFT-supermajority lockstep. By the close of §15, the substrate's mint-and-transfer protocol is fully specified. §16 specifies persistence; §17 specifies transports. By the close of Part II, the architectural picture covers every primitive the substrate composes, and Part III walks the threat model the architecture has to survive.
## §15. Live issuance

Smart bytes are not all created at genesis. The substrate must support **live issuance**: authorized issuers minting new bytes during runtime, with the same cryptographic and BFT machinery that governs transfers. A USD-byte issued by a GENIUS-licensed stablecoin provider on Tuesday afternoon must reach the cluster, be validated by the cluster's lockstep, be committed by BFT supermajority, and become available to the recipient — all within the substrate's normal protocol cycle.

This section specifies the mechanics. §15.1 names what an issuer is and how an issuer becomes one. §15.2 specifies the mint message format and the validation pipeline. §15.3 specifies how mints flow through lockstep alongside transfers. §15.4 names the operational properties live issuance has that genesis-only issuance cannot match. §15.5 names what live issuance does *not* yet handle in v0.3 and what v0.4 must address.

By the close of §15, the reader should understand the issuance flow end-to-end and recognize why issuance is intentionally unified with transfer at the protocol level rather than handled as a privileged out-of-band operation.

### 15.1 The issuer registry

An issuer is an entity authorized to mint bytes of a specific cargo type. The substrate's state carries an **issuer registry** — a deterministic data structure mapping (cargo_type, issuer_pubkey) pairs to authorization records. Every node in the cluster holds the same registry, updated by the same mechanism, producing the same content-addressed hash.

The registry is populated at genesis with a known set of issuers per cargo type. For USD-bytes: GENIUS-licensed stablecoin issuers, identified by their published ed25519 pubkeys. For joule-bytes: registered solar cooperatives, utility producers, individual rooftop systems with verified interconnect attestation. For governance-vote bytes: the registered members of the governance body whose votes the cargo represents. The genesis registry is part of the cluster's genesis state and is committed to the genesis state hash.

Adding or removing issuers post-genesis is **governance work**. v0.3 does not implement issuer-set mutation; the registry is fixed at genesis. v0.4 will implement a governance flow in which issuer-set changes are themselves submitted as inputs, validated by the cluster's lockstep, committed by BFT supermajority, and require quorum approval from existing issuers (typically a supermajority of the affected cargo type's existing issuers). The governance flow is intentionally slow — adding or removing issuers is not a routine operation — and uses cryptographic signatures from existing issuers as authorization rather than relying on any single administrative authority.

The registry's public visibility is a structural property: any party querying the cluster's state can see the full issuer set for each cargo type. There are no hidden issuers, no private mint authorities, no out-of-band issuance paths. If a USD-byte appears in the cluster claiming to be issued by a key that is not in the registry, the byte is rejected. The transparency is not optional; it is what makes issuer accountability possible.

### 15.2 The mint message

A live mint is an input message submitted to the cluster's lockstep, structurally similar to a transfer input but carrying mint-specific fields. The reference implementation calls the message `MintInput`. It contains:

- **Cargo type tag**: identifies the cargo class being minted (USD, joule, etc.). Determines which issuer-registry entry the cluster checks for authorization.
- **Cargo bytes**: the application-layer cargo specification, identical in structure to what would appear in a fresh byte's origin attestation. Includes the cargo's value, the issuer's redemption policy, and any cargo-specific metadata.
- **Issuer pubkey**: the ed25519 public key of the entity claiming authority to mint. Must match a registered issuer pubkey for the cargo type.
- **Recipient pubkey**: the ed25519 public key of the entity who will own the byte after minting.
- **Nonce**: a 16-byte random or sequentially-allocated value preventing collision between distinct mints with otherwise-identical content. Same role as the nonce in genesis-time origin attestations (§8.3).
- **Issuer signature**: an ed25519 signature by the issuer's private key over the canonical serialization of (cargo_type, cargo_bytes, recipient, nonce). Binds the issuer to the mint and prevents anyone else from forging the mint.

The mint message is submitted to a cluster node by the issuer or an authorized intermediary (a wallet, a custodial service, an integration). The submitting node adds the mint to its pending input queue (§9.4 step 1) and broadcasts it as part of the next frame's `InputBatch` (§9.4 step 2).

Validation happens during the deterministic transition (§9.4 step 5). For each mint in the frame:

1. **Issuer authorization check.** The cluster verifies that the issuer pubkey is in the issuer registry for the cargo type. If not registered, the mint is silently dropped — deterministic across all honest nodes, since the registry is consensus-replicated.

2. **Signature verification.** The cluster reconstructs the canonical message (cargo_type, cargo_bytes, recipient, nonce) and verifies the issuer's signature against the issuer's pubkey. Forged or tampered signatures fail verification; the mint is silently dropped.

3. **Nonce-based byte ID computation.** The cluster computes the byte's identity as `blake3(canonical_origin_attestation)` where the canonical origin attestation includes issuer pubkey, cargo_type, cargo_bytes, recipient, nonce, signature, and the current frame number. The frame number is included so that mints with otherwise-identical fields submitted in different frames produce different byte IDs — preventing a subtle race where a duplicate mint message in a different frame would otherwise collide with an existing byte.

4. **Duplicate detection.** If a byte with the computed identity already exists in the cluster's state, the mint is silently dropped. This handles the case where the same mint message is submitted to multiple cluster nodes simultaneously and arrives as duplicate `InputBatch` entries — the second-applied mint is a no-op.

5. **State insertion.** A new smart byte is constructed with the canonical origin attestation and inserted into the cluster's state. The byte's owner is the recipient; the byte's history is the origin attestation alone (no transitions yet); the byte's joule cost starts at the cost of this frame's processing.

Validation that fails any check is silent across all honest nodes. There is no error returned to the submitting issuer at the substrate level — the issuer queries the cluster's state after the frame commits to confirm the mint succeeded. (Application-layer wrappers may provide synchronous-feeling APIs by waiting on the cluster's state; this is a higher-layer convenience, not a substrate property.)

### 15.3 Mint and transfer composition within a frame

A frame can contain both mints and transfers. The substrate processes them in a specific order: **mints first, then transfers**, with each class processed in canonical hash-ordering within the class (§9.4 step 4 for transfers; same algorithm for mints).

The mints-before-transfers ordering enables a useful pattern: an issuer can mint a byte and the recipient can transfer it within the same frame. A user submitting a mint request to a stablecoin issuer can have their wallet automatically queue a follow-on transfer (e.g., paying a merchant) at the same lockstep frame. The mint applies first, the byte exists, the transfer applies second, and the merchant has the byte at frame commit. The two operations appear simultaneous to the application layer.

The cross-class ordering is deterministic: every honest node processes mints before transfers in every frame, and the canonical hash-ordering within each class is identical across nodes. The composition does not weaken determinism.

The substrate does not allow the reverse pattern (transfer before mint within the same frame) because a transfer requires the byte to already exist; a transfer of a byte that has not yet been minted has no valid previous-history-hash and fails verification. Applications that need this pattern must split it across two frames.

### 15.4 What live issuance enables

Genesis-only issuance — the simpler architecture, used by Bitcoin and many cryptocurrency designs — would require all bytes that will ever exist to be created at the cluster's launch. This is structurally inadequate for the substrate's design goals, for four reasons.

**Reason one: dynamic supply.** A USD-byte issuer redeems bytes (burning them) when users withdraw to bank accounts and mints new bytes when users deposit. The supply must move with deposit and withdrawal flows in real time. Genesis-only issuance cannot model this; the supply is fixed at launch. Live issuance is required for any cargo type with dynamic supply, which is most of them.

**Reason two: many issuers.** The substrate is multi-issuer for every cargo type — multiple stablecoin providers, multiple solar cooperatives, multiple governance bodies. Each issuer mints independently. Genesis-only issuance forces all issuers to coordinate before launch, which is operationally infeasible at substrate scale. Live issuance lets issuers operate independently, governed by the issuer registry.

**Reason three: time-bound cargo.** Some cargo expires (perishable goods, time-bound service claims). The cargo specification includes the expiration; new cargo must be minted as the time-bound supply is consumed. Genesis-only issuance produces only stale cargo over time. Live issuance is required for any time-sensitive cargo type.

**Reason four: issuer governance.** The substrate's design includes evolving issuer sets — new issuers admitted (post-v0.4 governance), failing issuers removed, cargo-type creation as new use cases emerge. Genesis-only issuance fixes the issuer set forever, which forecloses substrate evolution. Live issuance is the operational mechanism that lets the substrate grow over time.

These four reasons compose into a single architectural commitment: live issuance is not a feature added to a genesis-only base; it is the substrate's primary issuance mechanism, and genesis issuance is just the special case of bytes minted at frame zero.

### 15.5 What live issuance does not yet handle

The discipline of naming what is design-only continues.

**Issuer governance for set mutation.** v0.3 fixes the issuer set at genesis. v0.4 must implement: signed governance proposals from existing issuers, supermajority voting among affected-cargo-type issuers, time-locked execution of approved changes, and graceful handling of issuers whose private keys have been compromised. The mechanics are well-understood (multi-signature governance is implemented in many adjacent systems) but the substrate's specification is not yet written.

**Issuer key rotation.** An issuer whose private key has been compromised should be able to rotate to a new key without losing authorization for the cargo type. The mechanics involve a signed rotation message from the old key (proving control until the rotation moment) authorizing the new key, with the substrate's registry updated atomically. v0.3 does not implement; v0.4 priority high.

**Issuer reserve attestations for redeemable cargo.** A USD-byte issuer claims their bytes are backed by dollar reserves; the substrate currently has no protocol-level mechanism for the issuer to attest, periodically and verifiably, that the reserves still exist. Layer-2 services (Chainlink-style oracles, regulator audit publication, on-chain reserve commitments) can provide this, but the substrate's protocol does not require it. v0.4 may specify a standard issuer attestation flow; v1.0 leaves this to layer-2.

**Issuer accountability for bad mints.** If an issuer mints bytes inappropriately (against insufficient reserves, in violation of regulatory rules, with internal fraud), the substrate accepts the mints because they are cryptographically valid. The accountability mechanism is at the legal and regulatory layer, not the substrate layer. v0.3 has no protocol-level response; v0.4 may include cluster-level expulsion of issuers via the same dissent-log mechanism that handles peer expulsion (§14.3), but the design is preliminary.

**Cross-cluster issuer registries.** Each cluster has its own issuer registry. If a byte issued by cluster A's issuer is transferred to cluster B (§11.3.3), cluster B must verify the issuer's authorization at the time of mint, which requires either replicating cluster A's registry (expensive) or trusting cluster A's BFT-supermajority signed export attestation (cheaper but adds trust). The substrate's design is the latter — cluster B trusts cluster A's signature on the export attestation, which includes the issuer's authorization at mint time. v0.4 must specify the cross-cluster issuer-registry attestation format precisely.

These five deferred items are real engineering work. None is open-research-grade; each has known approaches in adjacent systems. The treatise's discipline is to name them so the gap between v1.0 and a full production substrate is legible.

### 15.6 Why issuance is unified with transfer at the protocol level

A note on architectural philosophy. The substrate could have handled issuance as a privileged out-of-band operation — issuers submit mints through a separate channel, the cluster validates them out-of-protocol, the bytes appear in state without going through the standard input batch. Many systems take this approach. The substrate does not.

Issuance flows through the same lockstep that handles transfers, with the same `InputBatch` carrying both kinds of inputs, the same canonical hash-ordering within each class, the same BFT supermajority commit. The unification is intentional, for two reasons.

**Reason one: consistency.** A substrate that handled issuance through a separate code path would be vulnerable to bugs in that path that don't affect transfers. The path's separateness is itself a risk. By unifying, every issuance check is covered by the same cryptographic and BFT machinery that protects every transfer. Bugs in the substrate's transfer-validation code are bugs in issuance-validation code; security audits of one are security audits of the other.

**Reason two: protocol cleanliness.** A unified protocol has one input type, one validation pipeline, one ordering algorithm, one commit mechanism. The conceptual surface area is smaller. Engineers learning the substrate's protocol learn one thing, not two; testers writing protocol tests test one path, not two. Implementations in different languages have one set of behaviors to match.

The cost is that mint validation is slightly more complex than transfer validation (one extra check: issuer registration). The benefit is that everything else is identical. The substrate accepts the slight cost for the substantial benefit.

This is the same architectural choice that protocols designed for longevity have repeatedly made: minimize the number of distinguished message types, validate every type through the same pipeline, share infrastructure across all messages. The substrate's unification serves the same purpose — every input goes through the same lockstep, the same canonical ordering, the same BFT supermajority commit, with cargo-class differences handled by validation logic rather than by separate protocols.

The next section (§16) specifies persistence — checkpoint and replay log mechanics, the genesis-seeding requirement that v0.3 testing surfaced, and the bootstrap story for new nodes joining a cluster. By the close of §16, the substrate's storage model is complete. §17 specifies transports, and Part II closes with the architectural picture covering every primitive the substrate composes.
## §16. Persistence: checkpoint and replay log

A node that has never persisted its state cannot recover from a crash, cannot survive a planned restart, and cannot keep its position in the cluster across an operator's hardware refresh. Persistence is therefore not an optional feature of the substrate; it is a correctness requirement for any node operating in production.

This section specifies the substrate's persistence model: checkpoint-and-log, the genesis-seeding requirement that v0.3 testing surfaced, and the bootstrap story for new nodes joining a cluster. The section is short because the design is simple. The simplicity is the design's load-bearing property — persistence systems that are clever fail in clever ways, and the substrate's persistence is intentionally unclever.

### 16.1 The two-file design

Each node persists state to disk via two files:

**`checkpoint.bin`** — a full serialization of the node's State at the moment of the most recent checkpoint. The State serialization includes the frame number, every smart byte the cluster currently holds, and the issuer registry. The checkpoint is content-addressed: its hash is the cluster's state hash at the checkpoint frame, identical across all honest nodes. (The dissent log is per-node-local — see §14 — and is persisted separately by each node's operational tooling rather than as part of the consensus-replicated checkpoint.)

**`log.bin`** — an append-only sequence of length-prefixed `FrameInputs` records, one per committed frame since the most recent checkpoint. Each entry records the canonical-ordered transfers and mints that the cluster committed in that frame. The log entries are *not* the local node's submitted inputs; they are the *cluster's committed inputs*, identical across all honest nodes.

The two files together are a complete record of the node's state at any frame from the last checkpoint forward. To recover the node's state at any point, load the checkpoint and replay the log up to the desired frame.

The reference implementation uses bincode for the serialization format, with `blake3` content-addressing and length-prefix framing for the log. The format is not normative — any conforming implementation may choose its own serialization, provided the recovery semantics produce identical state. The substrate's conformance specification (the document accompanying this treatise) gives the canonical wire format that cross-implementation interoperability requires; nodes that interoperate with each other use the canonical format, while nodes that operate single-implementation clusters may use any format.

### 16.2 The recovery procedure

A node starting up performs:

**Step 1: open the checkpoint.** If `checkpoint.bin` exists, deserialize it and use the resulting State as the node's starting state. The deserialized state's frame number indicates how far the checkpoint advanced.

**Step 2: open the log.** If `log.bin` exists, iterate through its entries in order. For each entry, verify that the entry's frame number equals the current state's frame number (any mismatch is a corruption, fail loudly). Apply the entry's inputs to the state via the standard deterministic transition (§9.4 step 5).

**Step 3: enter normal operation.** The node's frame number now equals the cluster's last committed frame plus one. The node rejoins the cluster's lockstep at this frame, accepting incoming `InputBatch` and `StateHash` messages from peers, processing them normally.

A new node — never previously run — has neither file. Its starting state is the genesis state of its cluster, which it must obtain. §16.3 specifies how.

### 16.3 The genesis-seeding requirement

The v0.3 reference implementation surfaced a real bug worth naming as a load-bearing lesson. A naive persistence design — checkpoint and log only, no special handling of first-time startup — produces silently-wrong behavior the first time a node starts in a non-trivial cluster.

The bug: a node starting with no checkpoint and no log has an *empty* state (frame zero, no bytes, no issuers). The node connects to peers, receives `InputBatch` messages for the cluster's actual current frame, attempts to process them — and every transfer fails verification, because every transfer references a byte the node doesn't have. The transfers are silently dropped (the substrate's standard behavior on verification failure, §9.4 step 5). The node's state hash diverges from the cluster's supermajority hash. The node halts with `MINORITY DIVERGENCE` (§13.3 outcome three) or, if other nodes are also fresh, the cluster halts with `CLUSTER SPLIT`.

The bug is silent in the bad case: a freshly started node with empty state will appear to be running normally for several frames before the divergence becomes visible. There is no useful error message ("transfer references unknown byte_id" is logged, but it is logged at debug level if at all, and an operator unfamiliar with the failure mode will not connect the dots).

**The fix** is to require the node's first-time startup to seed itself with the cluster's *genesis state* — the canonical starting state that all cluster members share. The reference implementation provides this via `Persistence::open_with_genesis(dir, interval, genesis_state)`. On first open with no existing checkpoint, the genesis state is written immediately as `checkpoint.bin`, and subsequent startups read it as the authoritative starting state.

The protocol-level requirement: a node must obtain the cluster's genesis state from some authoritative source before participating in lockstep. The authoritative sources are:

- **Genesis bootstrap**: at cluster founding, the genesis state is generated by the founding members and signed by all of them. New members admitted later receive the genesis state from existing members as part of the admission flow (§11.2).
- **Checkpoint replication**: an established cluster periodically publishes content-addressed checkpoints to the substrate's gossip overlay (§11.4). A new node joining the cluster downloads the most recent published checkpoint, verifies its content-addressed hash against the cluster's BFT-supermajority signed attestation, and uses it as the starting state.
- **Cross-cluster import**: a node admitted to a cluster created by splitting from a parent cluster (§11.3.2) receives the new cluster's initial state from the split protocol's `SplitFrame`.

In each case, the node's startup state is *cryptographically anchored* to the cluster's history rather than being constructed locally. A node that cannot anchor — that has no genesis state, no checkpoint, no split-frame snapshot — cannot participate. The substrate refuses to run nodes whose starting state is not verifiable.

The genesis-seeding requirement is the substrate's third major v0.3 implementation lesson, alongside phase buffering (§12) and the dead `MINORITY DIVERGENCE` path (§13.4). The pattern is the same in each: a load-bearing detail that pure-prose architectural design did not anticipate, surfaced in milliseconds of running code, and now documented honestly. A treatise that ships v1.0 without naming these would be misleading; the substrate's approach is to name them and update.

### 16.4 The checkpoint cadence

A cluster operating at 1000 frames per second produces 1000 log entries per second. Without periodic checkpointing, the log grows unboundedly and recovery time grows linearly with uptime. The substrate's persistence layer therefore includes a **checkpoint cadence**: every N committed frames, the node rewrites the checkpoint with the current state and truncates the log.

The cadence N is operator-tunable. The tradeoff is straightforward:

- **Frequent checkpoints** (small N, e.g., 100 frames) produce faster recovery (less log to replay) at the cost of more disk writes (the entire state serialized every 100 frames).
- **Infrequent checkpoints** (large N, e.g., 100,000 frames) produce slower recovery (long log to replay on startup) but minimize disk write overhead during normal operation.

The reference implementation defaults to N=10,000, which produces approximately one checkpoint per ten seconds at 1000 fps. This is suitable for clusters whose state size is on the order of megabytes; larger states may require larger N to keep checkpoint cost low. The substrate does not commit to a default; operators tune for their workload.

The checkpoint write itself is durable: the implementation writes to a temporary file, calls `fsync`, and renames atomically to `checkpoint.bin`. This guarantees that a crash during checkpoint write does not produce a partial checkpoint — the operation is atomic from the recovery procedure's perspective. The log's `fsync`-on-append is currently per-frame (slow for high-throughput operation); v0.4 should batch `fsync` across multiple frames with the corresponding loss of last-N-frames durability on a hard crash, which is acceptable because honest peers can replay the missing frames after rejoin (§16.5).

### 16.5 What persistence does not solve

The discipline of naming what is *not* solved continues.

**State sync from honest peers.** A node whose local state has corrupted (disk failure, file system corruption, malicious tampering) cannot recover from its own checkpoint and log alone. Recovery requires fetching the cluster's current state from honest peers, verifying the cluster's BFT-supermajority signature on the state, and replacing the corrupt local state. v0.3 does not implement this; nodes whose state has corrupted halt and require operator intervention. v0.4 priority high.

**Bounded log retention with archival.** The substrate's persistence as specified retains all committed frames between checkpoints. A node whose checkpoint cadence is large and whose uptime is long will have a very long log. v0.4 should specify archival policies — older log entries can be compressed, moved to slower storage, or deleted entirely once enough subsequent checkpoints have advanced past them.

**Cross-node persistence consistency.** Each node's persistence is local and independent. Two nodes in the same cluster may have checkpoints at different frames, logs of different lengths, and `fsync` policies of different aggressiveness. The substrate is correct under this heterogeneity (because state is consensus-replicated by lockstep, not by file-replication), but operators may want consistency across nodes for operational reasons (faster recovery, simpler troubleshooting). v0.4 may specify operator tooling for cross-node consistency; the protocol does not require it.

**Encryption at rest.** Checkpoints and logs contain potentially sensitive data — the full byte state, including who owns what cargo. The substrate does not encrypt persistence by default; nodes operating in environments where disk theft is a concern must use OS-level full-disk encryption or filesystem-level encryption. v0.4 may specify a substrate-level encryption layer; v1.0 does not.

**Integrity verification of persistence.** A node loading its checkpoint trusts the checkpoint's content. An adversary with disk access could tamper with the checkpoint to inject false bytes, alter the issuer registry, or otherwise corrupt the node's starting state. The substrate's defense against this is the cluster's lockstep itself — any tampered state will produce divergent state hashes during the next supermajority commit, surfacing the problem within milliseconds. The substrate does not currently sign checkpoints with the node's own key; v0.4 should, allowing offline integrity verification independent of the cluster.

These five exclusions name the persistence layer's actual scope. The treatise's discipline is to be precise about what the layer provides (durable storage of state and log, recovery from clean shutdown and crash) and what it does not (state-sync, archival, encryption, integrity verification). The substrate composes persistence with the cluster's lockstep, the gossip overlay, and operator tooling to produce a complete operational story; persistence alone does not.

The next section (§17) specifies the two transports the v0.3 reference implementation supports — in-process mpsc and TCP — and the transport-agnostic interface that makes the substrate portable to other transports (QUIC, WebSocket, custom protocols). By the close of §17, Part II is complete and the architectural picture covers every primitive the substrate composes.
## §17. The two transports

The substrate's lockstep protocol exchanges messages between cluster nodes. *How* those messages travel — over what wire, with what framing, with what reliability and latency characteristics — is the **transport layer**. The substrate is intentionally transport-agnostic at the protocol level: any reliable, ordered, bidirectional message channel between peers is sufficient. The v0.3 reference implementation provides two transports — in-process mpsc and TCP — and the abstraction that lets future implementations swap in QUIC, WebSocket, or custom transports without changing the protocol.

This section closes Part II by specifying the transport interface, the two reference transports, and the properties future transports must satisfy. By the close of §17, the architectural picture covers every primitive the substrate composes; Part III then walks the threat model the architecture has to survive.

### 17.1 The transport interface

A substrate transport is any object satisfying two operations:

**`send_to(peer: NodeId, msg: LockstepMsg) -> Result<(), Error>`** — deliver a message to a specific peer in the cluster. The transport guarantees that if the call returns Ok, the peer will eventually receive the message exactly once, in the order it was sent relative to other messages sent to the same peer. If the call returns Err, the message may or may not have been delivered, and the cluster's lockstep treats this as if the peer is missing for this frame (the BFT supermajority handles missing peers per §13).

**`recv() -> Result<LockstepMsg, Error>`** — receive the next available message from any peer. The transport returns messages in arrival order, not in cluster-frame order. The lockstep protocol's phase buffering (§12) handles temporal reordering across peers and frames.

Peer set membership is not a transport concern. The `Node` holds the peer list directly; the transport is given a peer ID by the node and asked to deliver to that peer. Cluster-membership-changing operations (admission, expulsion, departure) update the node's peer list; the transport is informed only by virtue of receiving send_to calls for new peers and not for departed ones.

These two operations are the entire transport surface. The protocol does not require connection management, encryption, authentication, or any other transport-layer feature; if a specific transport implementation provides them, they are transport-internal concerns the protocol does not reach into.

The transport interface is deliberately minimal. A substrate implementation could add a third operation — `broadcast(msg) -> Result<(), Error>` — to express "send this to all peers" as a single call rather than as N `send_to` calls. The reference implementation does not, because broadcast at the transport level can hide partial-delivery failures (some peers receive, some don't) that the protocol needs to handle explicitly. Per-peer `send_to` makes the partial-delivery case visible and the protocol's response (lockstep proceeds to phase buffering, then to BFT supermajority on whatever messages arrived) correct.

### 17.2 The in-process mpsc transport

The first reference transport is **in-process multiple-producer-single-consumer (mpsc) channels**, used for testing, embedded deployments, and single-machine multi-cluster setups. Each node holds an mpsc receiver; sending from peer X to peer Y means cloning peer Y's sender (held in a peer-to-sender map) and pushing the message onto it.

Properties:

- **Reliability**: messages cannot be lost. Channel sends always succeed (unless the receiver has been dropped, which is a programming error rather than a transport failure).
- **Ordering**: per-peer order is preserved. Messages from peer X to peer Y arrive in send order.
- **Latency**: sub-microsecond. The transport is a thread-safe queue in shared memory; message delivery is the cost of a mutex acquisition plus a notification.
- **Throughput**: bounded by the receiver's processing speed and the channel's capacity. The reference implementation uses unbounded channels; production deployments should use bounded channels with a backpressure policy.
- **Failure mode**: receiver-dropped is the only failure mode, and it indicates a bug rather than an operational problem.

The mpsc transport is what the v0.3 reference implementation uses for its property tests (Appendix A). It is not a production transport for distributed clusters — every node is in the same process — but it is the correct transport for testing the protocol's correctness without confounding network effects, and for embedded deployments where a single process operates an entire cluster.

### 17.3 The TCP transport

The second reference transport is **framed bincode over TCP**, used for production-class distributed clusters. Each node listens on a configured TCP port; peers establish persistent TCP connections and exchange length-prefixed bincode-serialized messages.

The framing protocol is straightforward:

- 4 bytes: little-endian message length (`u32`)
- N bytes: the bincode-serialized `LockstepMsg`

A receiver reads the length prefix, allocates a buffer of that size, reads the buffer, and deserializes. A sender computes the message length, writes the prefix, writes the body. No additional framing, no escape sequences, no message boundaries beyond the length prefix.

Properties:

- **Reliability**: TCP guarantees in-order, exactly-once delivery within a connection. Connection failures (TCP reset, peer disconnection, network partition) require reconnection; the reference implementation does not auto-reconnect, leaving reconnection logic to the cluster's operational tooling. v0.4 should add automatic reconnection with backoff.
- **Ordering**: TCP guarantees per-connection order. The substrate's protocol per-peer is satisfied because each peer pair has its own TCP connection.
- **Latency**: dominated by network round-trip time, not transport overhead. A cluster operating across geographically-distributed datacenters will have per-message latency in the 10-100ms range; a cluster in a single datacenter, sub-millisecond.
- **Throughput**: bounded by network bandwidth and CPU available for serialization. The v0.3 reference implementation has been stress-tested at n=3 over real TCP at ~986 frames/second wall-clock; this scales down with cluster size per the §11.1 scaling table.
- **Failure modes**: connection failures (peer down, network down, TCP reset), partial messages on connection drop (handled by length-prefix re-read on reconnect), and slow peers blocking the cluster's progress (handled by phase buffering and BFT supermajority).

The TCP transport is what the v0.3 reference implementation uses for cross-process testing and for the substrate's distributed-cluster verification. Stress runs at 10,000 frames over real TCP across multiple processes confirm the protocol's stability under sustained operation.

The TCP transport is not encrypted by default. Production deployments should layer TLS over TCP (the reference implementation supports this via Rust's `tokio-rustls` bindings, configured at the transport level rather than the protocol level), or run the cluster's TCP traffic over an encrypted tunnel (WireGuard, IPsec). The substrate's protocol does not require encryption — the message contents are signed cryptographically per-input, so an adversary observing the wire learns transaction patterns but cannot forge or alter messages — but most operators will want encryption for confidentiality of metadata (which nodes communicate at what rates, which inputs originate from which peers).

### 17.4 Future transports

The transport interface (§17.1) is small enough that other transports can be added without protocol changes. Three are anticipated for v0.4-v1.x:

**QUIC.** The natural successor to TCP for distributed cluster operation. QUIC's per-stream multiplexing eliminates head-of-line blocking, its connection migration handles network changes gracefully, and its built-in TLS 1.3 makes encryption transport-native rather than transport-layered. The substrate's protocol maps cleanly to QUIC streams: one stream per peer-pair direction, length-prefixed messages within each stream. v0.4-v1.0 priority.

**WebSocket.** Required for browser-based substrate clients. A WebSocket-capable substrate node can be a JavaScript or WebAssembly application running in a browser tab, participating in a cluster (as a read-only observer or as a full member, depending on the cluster's admission policy). WebSocket framing is straightforward — the substrate uses the binary message type with the same length-prefix framing as TCP — and the abstraction works without protocol changes. v0.4 priority for browser clients; v1.x for full-member browser participation.

**Tor or onion-routing transports.** For substrate operation in adversarial network conditions (state-level censorship, BGP hijack, mass surveillance), the substrate can run over Tor or equivalent onion-routing networks. The transport implementation wraps the underlying TCP-over-Tor connection in the substrate's framing protocol; the protocol itself is unaffected. Latency is significantly higher (per-message round-trips include Tor's onion-circuit overhead, often hundreds of milliseconds), so cluster sizing must account for this. Useful as a backup transport when normal TCP is unavailable. v1.x specification.

The transport-agnostic design means the substrate's protocol does not need revision when new transports ship. Each new transport implementation is an independent engineering project that conforms to the §17.1 interface. The protocol's correctness properties — determinism, BFT supermajority commit, phase buffering, content-addressed history — are preserved across transport choices.

### 17.5 What the transport layer does not solve

The discipline of naming what is *not* solved continues.

**Authentication.** The substrate's transport layer does not authenticate peers. Authentication is at the *protocol* layer — every message is signed by the node's ed25519 key, and signature verification happens during input processing (§9.4 step 5). A transport that delivered messages from an impostor would deliver messages whose signatures don't verify, and the impostor's messages would be silently dropped. The transport does not need to know which peer is "really" peer X; the cryptographic verification handles that.

**Encryption.** As named in §17.3, encryption is transport-layered (TLS over TCP, encryption native to QUIC) rather than protocol-required. The substrate operates correctly over plaintext transports; encryption is operator's choice based on threat model.

**Connection management.** The reference implementation does not implement automatic reconnection, connection pooling, or NAT traversal. These are operational concerns that production deployments handle through their networking stack (load balancers, service meshes, NAT-traversal software like UPnP or hole-punching). v0.4 should specify the reconnection protocol's correctness properties (a reconnected peer must resume at the cluster's current frame, not at the last frame before disconnection); the implementation is operator's choice.

**Quality of service.** The substrate has no transport-layer QoS — no message prioritization, no traffic shaping, no congestion-aware backoff beyond what TCP provides. Production deployments operating over shared network infrastructure may want QoS; this is an operational concern outside the substrate's scope.

**Geographic optimization.** Cluster operators may want peers preferentially connected to geographically-nearby peers to minimize latency. The substrate provides no transport-layer mechanism for this; it is a deployment-time concern (operators choose which peers to connect to, the transport delivers).

These five exclusions name the transport layer's actual scope. The substrate's contribution at this layer is the abstraction (§17.1) and the two reference implementations (§17.2-§17.3), not a complete production-class networking stack. Operators compose the substrate's transport with the operational tooling appropriate to their deployment.

### Part II close

Part II has specified the substrate's architecture across ten sections. §8 specified the smart byte schema. §9 specified deterministic simulation as the consensus mechanism. §10 specified the byte history as a content-addressed chain. §11 specified cluster topology and node admission. §12 specified the phase buffering requirement. §13 specified BFT supermajority commit. §14 specified the dissent log. §15 specified live issuance. §16 specified persistence. §17 has just specified the transport layer.

The architecture composes into a system that performs the institutional middle's three functions (matching, clearing, trust signaling — §3) at the cost structure §4 established (cheap cryptography + ubiquitous deterministic computation + per-operation energy measurement, composed for byte-denominated commerce). The smart byte (§8) is the load-bearing primitive; the lockstep simulation (§9) is the consensus mechanism; the chain (§10) is the deep-history verification; the cluster topology (§11) is the scaling mechanism; the BFT commit (§13) is the Byzantine defense; the dissent log (§14) is the observability primitive; live issuance (§15) is the supply-management mechanism; persistence (§16) is the durability mechanism; transports (§17) are the wire-level abstractions.

Three load-bearing details surfaced in v0.3 implementation testing rather than from architectural design: phase buffering (§12), the dead `MINORITY DIVERGENCE` path in favor of the dissent log (§13.4), and the genesis-seeding requirement (§16.3). Each is documented honestly with its origin in implementation rather than design. The substrate's epistemic posture is treatise-and-implementation-pair, with the treatise updating as the implementation surfaces what the prose missed.

Several v0.4+ items are explicitly deferred: cluster splitting (§11.3.2), cross-cluster transfer (§11.3.3), cluster-level Byzantine handling (§11.3.4), state sync from honest peers (§16.5), issuer governance for set mutation (§15.5), and several others. Each is named with its priority and its specification gap. The substrate ships v1.0 with these explicitly incomplete; v0.4 work will close them.

Part III walks the threat model the architecture has to survive. The threat model is the largest part of the treatise by design — multiple categories of attack engaged in detail, with each defended (or honestly acknowledged as inadequately defended) and with the AI-layer threat treated as a load-bearing strategic engagement. By the close of Part III, the reader should understand what the substrate survives, what it does not yet survive, and what the substrate's authors are actively working to defend against. Parts IV-VII engage workarounds, retrofits, distribution implications, and the closing argument.
# Part III — The Threat Model

Part I established why the substrate is necessary. Part II specified what the substrate is. Part III specifies what the substrate has to survive.

The threat model is the largest part of the treatise by design. The substrate's case rests on its surviving adversarial reading. A treatise that names the substrate's defenses without engaging the attacks they defend against produces a credibility gap on contact with anyone who reads carefully — anyone capable of mounting the attacks the treatise is silent on. The substrate's authors mount the attacks first, in the open, with the substrate's defenses named precisely against each.

Part III walks ten attack categories: §19 cryptographic, §20 lockstep protocol, §21 cluster topology, §22 issuer, §23 state, §24 economic, §25 social, §26 legal, §27 physical, §28 AI-layer. The categorization is the substrate's own; alternative threat-model taxonomies exist (STRIDE, MITRE ATT&CK, PASTA) and any of them could be applied to the substrate orthogonally. The categorization here is chosen because each category corresponds to a distinct defense layer in the substrate's architecture, which makes the treatise's "what defends against what" map easier to read than a generic taxonomy would.

§28 (AI-layer attacks) carries load-bearing strategic engagement. The treatise has been honest from §1 onward that the substrate competes against AI-mediated rent capture; §28 is where the competition's specific attack vectors are named and engaged.

## §18. The threat model meta-discipline

A threat model is the document that names the adversaries, the attacks the adversaries can mount, the defenses the system provides, and — critically — the attacks for which the system has no defense or only a partial defense. The fourth element is what distinguishes a credible threat model from a marketing document. A threat model that defends against every attack is a threat model that has not engaged the attacks honestly.

This section specifies how Part III's threat model is structured, what assumptions it operates under, what it includes, what it excludes, and how to read it. By the close of §18, the reader should be able to evaluate any specific attack engagement in §19-§28 against a clear standard of what counts as adequate engagement and what counts as evasion.

### 18.1 The adversary classes

Part III engages five adversary classes. Each is named, the resources each can deploy are specified, and each is mapped to the attack categories where it predominates.

**Adversary class one: opportunistic cryptographic attackers.** Adversaries with cryptanalytic capability — researchers who discover weaknesses in primitives, intelligence agencies with quantum or near-quantum compute, criminal organizations with access to leaked private keys. Resources: cryptographic expertise, possibly significant compute. Predominant in §19 (cryptographic attacks). The substrate's defenses are primitive choice (ed25519, blake3, conservative parameters), migration paths if a primitive is broken, and the absence of cryptographic monocultures (multiple keys per user, multiple signature classes per cargo type).

**Adversary class two: protocol-level attackers operating within the substrate.** Adversaries who control one or more substrate nodes, can submit inputs, can manipulate timing, can lie to peers. Resources: substrate-level participation, possibly multiple nodes if cluster-admission gates can be cleared. Predominant in §20 (lockstep protocol attacks), §21 (cluster topology attacks), §23 (state attacks). The substrate's defenses are BFT supermajority commit, dissent logging, content-addressed history, and cluster admission gates.

**Adversary class three: economic and social attackers operating around the substrate.** Adversaries who do not directly attack the protocol but attack the economic, social, and operational structures the substrate depends on. Resources: capital, social legitimacy, ability to fork or rebrand, ability to manipulate search results and discourse. Predominant in §22 (issuer attacks), §24 (economic attacks), §25 (social attacks on the substrate itself). The substrate's defenses are reference-implementation provenance, conformance test vectors, multi-issuer plurality, and the substrate's protocol-level absence of features (no token, no fee market, no foundation) that adversaries could attempt to capture.

**Adversary class four: state-level attackers.** Adversaries with legal, regulatory, and physical-coercive power over the substrate's operators, issuers, and infrastructure. Resources: jurisdiction-specific legal authority, sanctions, coordinated international pressure, BGP and infrastructure access, physical seizure capability. Predominant in §26 (legal attacks) and §27 (physical attacks). The substrate's defenses are jurisdictional distribution of operators, gossip-overlay path diversity, content-addressed history allowing replay from any honest peer, and the substrate's framing as protocol-not-service to limit operator obligations. The defenses are honestly partial; §27 names this directly.

**Adversary class five: AI-mediated incumbents.** Adversaries who own the AI layer being deployed across the institutional middle and who race to capture the byte-denominated commerce stack before the substrate ships. Resources: enormous compute, enormous data, regulatory access, vendor relationships, marketing scale, and the ability to offer substrate-equivalent functionality as a service while charging substrate-rent-equivalent fees. Predominant in §28 (AI-layer attacks). The substrate's defenses are the substrate's open-source licensing, reference-implementation provenance, multi-issuer plurality, the treatise itself as counter-narrative, and the substrate's protocol-level independence from AI inference for its core verification path.

These five classes overlap in real adversaries — a single state intelligence agency might deploy class-one cryptanalysis, class-two protocol participation, class-four legal pressure, and class-five AI-augmented narrative attack simultaneously. The taxonomy is for the substrate's defense organization, not for adversary classification; real adversaries combine techniques from multiple classes.

### 18.2 The threat-model assumptions

Every threat model rests on assumptions. Naming them honestly distinguishes a credible threat model from one that has hidden its weak points.

**Assumption one: cryptographic primitives are secure unless and until they are publicly broken.** The substrate assumes ed25519 signatures are unforgeable without the private key, that blake3 collisions cannot be efficiently found, and that the underlying number-theoretic problems remain hard. If any of these fails — if a polynomial-time elliptic-curve discrete-log algorithm is published, if a blake3 collision attack is announced — the substrate's cryptographic layer fails and migration is required. §19 walks the migration path; the assumption itself is named here.

**Assumption two: ⌊(n-1)/3⌋ Byzantine bound on cluster nodes.** The substrate assumes that no more than one-third of any cluster's nodes are Byzantine at any given time. Beyond this bound, BFT supermajority commit can be defeated. The cluster admission gates (§11.2) raise the cost of clearing this bound, but the substrate is honest that a sufficiently-resourced adversary controlling enough cluster nodes can corrupt cluster state. §21 engages this directly.

**Assumption three: at least one honest peer reachable for any byte that needs verification.** The substrate's content-addressed history means a byte can be verified by any party holding its full chain, but obtaining the chain requires reaching at least one honest party who holds it. If the substrate's gossip overlay is so thoroughly suppressed that no honest party can be reached, the substrate's verification claim fails. §27 (physical attacks) engages this; the assumption is that complete network isolation is harder than the substrate's adversaries can typically achieve, but the assumption is conditional, not unconditional.

**Assumption four: at least one issuer per cargo type remains uncaptured.** The substrate's multi-issuer architecture means a user can switch issuers if their preferred issuer becomes captured, regulated into uselessness, or compromised. If every issuer for a given cargo type becomes captured simultaneously, the substrate's freedom-from-forced-reliance claim fails for that cargo type. §22 (issuer attacks) engages this; the assumption rests on issuer plurality being defensible across regulatory and economic pressure.

**Assumption five: substrate-level adoption is achievable before the AI-mediated rent layer consolidates.** The substrate's strategic case rests on its acquiring independent constituency before AI companies finish capturing the institutional-middle's transformation. If the consolidation completes first, the substrate's strategic claim — that two trajectories are still available — collapses to one trajectory. §28 (AI-layer attacks) engages this directly. The assumption is the most contestable in the whole threat model, and the substrate ships with this contestability acknowledged.

These five assumptions are not the substrate's hidden weaknesses; they are the conditions under which the substrate's claims hold. A reader who wants to falsify the substrate's case should attack the assumptions, not the architectural mechanisms. The substrate's authors believe the assumptions hold and are willing to be shown wrong.

### 18.3 What an adequate attack engagement looks like

Each attack in §19-§28 is engaged against a fixed standard. The standard has four elements, in order:

**Element one: the attack is named precisely.** A threat-model entry that says "an adversary attacks the substrate" is not an engagement; it is a placeholder. The substrate's threat model names the specific mechanism by which the attack succeeds, the resources the adversary requires, and the conditions under which the attack works.

**Element two: the substrate's defense is specified.** The treatise names which substrate-level mechanism (BFT, dissent log, content-addressing, cluster admission, etc.) defends against the attack, and how the defense composes with other defenses to produce defense-in-depth. If multiple defenses apply, all are named.

**Element three: the residual risk is acknowledged honestly.** No defense is perfect. Each engaged attack ends with a sentence or paragraph naming what the substrate's defense does not stop, what conditions the defense fails under, and what the residual risk's magnitude is. A threat-model entry without a residual-risk acknowledgement is not an adequate engagement.

**Element four: the operational response is specified.** If the attack succeeds despite the substrate's defenses, what is the operator's recovery path? Is the affected cluster recoverable, or is it lost? Are affected bytes recoverable from honest peers, or are they orphaned? Does the substrate continue operating in the rest of the federation, or does the attack propagate? The operational response is the threat-model element that turns "the substrate has been attacked" into "here is what to do about it."

The four-element standard is consistently applied across §19-§28. Entries that do not meet the standard are flagged as such — typically because the attack class is genuinely under-engaged in v1.0 and the treatise is honest about it rather than pretending otherwise.

### 18.4 What is intentionally outside the threat model

Several attack classes are not engaged in Part III, by design. Naming them prevents the threat model from being read as a claim of completeness when it is not.

**User-level operational security.** A user who shares their private key, falls for a phishing attack, fails to back up their key, or chooses an untrustworthy issuer produces losses the substrate cannot prevent. Layer-2 services (custodial wallets, social-recovery, hardware tokens) reduce these losses, but the substrate is silent on user-level operational security. §6.3 named this absence as a structural cost; Part III does not re-engage it.

**Application-layer fraud and dispute.** A user phished into authorizing a transfer signs a valid transition that the substrate accepts. The fraud is at the application layer, where intention verification belongs. Layer-2 fraud-detection services (anomaly detection, rate limiting, dispute-resolution arbitration) compose with the substrate but do not extend its threat model.

**Long-term cryptographic obsolescence.** The substrate's primitive choice (ed25519, blake3) is conservative for the next 10-20 years. Quantum computing development beyond that horizon may require post-quantum primitives. §19 names migration paths for known-broken primitives; §19 does not engage timeline-uncertain quantum threats in detail.

**Sociopolitical change at planetary scale.** The substrate operates within economic and political structures that may themselves change radically. A worldwide collapse of internet infrastructure, a global currency reset, a transition to a planetary command economy — these would change the conditions under which the substrate operates so substantially that engaging them within the threat model would require speculation the treatise should not perform. The substrate is robust to most realistic political change at the substrate-operating layer; civilizational-scale change is outside scope.

**Internal authorial integrity.** The treatise assumes that the substrate's authors and the reference implementation's contributors operate in good faith. If the original authors are themselves adversaries — if the reference implementation contains a deliberate backdoor — the substrate's threat model has been compromised at its source. The defenses against this are reproducible builds, multi-implementation plurality, signed releases with multi-maintainer review, and the explicit invitation to readers to verify the implementation. §25 (social attacks) engages this in part, but the assumption that the substrate's authors are operating in good faith is, like all threat models, a conditional claim.

These five exclusions name the threat model's actual scope. A reader concerned about any of them should engage the corresponding layer-2 or higher-level concerns; the substrate provides the mechanisms below them and is silent on the layers above.

### 18.5 How to read Part III

Each section §19-§28 follows a consistent structure:

**Subsection X.1: the attack class.** What the attack class is, who deploys it, what resources are required, what the attack class's relationship to other attack classes is.

**Subsections X.2 through X.N: specific attacks within the class.** Each attack is engaged against the four-element standard from §18.3 — named precisely, defense specified, residual risk acknowledged, operational response specified.

**Final subsection: composition with other categories.** How the attack class's defenses compose with defenses from other categories, and where in the substrate's defense-in-depth structure this category sits.

Readers can read Part III sequentially or jump to specific attack categories. The cross-references are dense — each attack typically references the substrate-level mechanisms (BFT, dissent log, content-addressing) that defend against it, with forward and backward pointers to related attacks and defenses elsewhere in Part III.

The reading discipline §18 asks of the reader: hold the substrate's claims against the threat model. The substrate claims to provide four freedoms (§6.1) at four costs (§6.2) under a specific threat model. Part III specifies the threat model. If any specific attack engagement in §19-§28 fails the four-element standard, the substrate's freedom claim is correspondingly weakened. Readers who find inadequate engagements are invited to flag them; the substrate's errata mechanism (specified in Appendix F, "Open problems") will document any inadequacies that survive review and any fixes that subsequent revisions deliver.

### 18.6 The strategic frame, retained

The treatise has been honest from §1 onward that the substrate competes against an AI-mediated rent-capture trajectory. Part III's organization reflects that competition. §19 through §27 engage the attacks any byte-denominated-commerce protocol must survive — attacks that would exist regardless of the AI-layer dynamics. §28 engages the specific attacks that arise from AI companies' position as the most capable adversaries in the substrate's strategic environment.

The strategic frame is not a separate concern from the technical threat model; it is the reason the technical threat model has the specific shape it does. A substrate threat model that did not engage AI-layer attacks would be incomplete given the substrate's actual deployment environment. A substrate threat model that engaged *only* AI-layer attacks would be incomplete given the broader range of adversaries the substrate must survive. Part III engages both, in proportion to the actual threat each represents.

By the close of Part III, the reader should accept that the substrate's defenses compose into a system that survives the realistic attack surface its deployment environment presents, that the residual risks are honestly named, and that the substrate's freedom claim from §6 holds under the threat model specified here. Part IV then engages the workarounds — the alternative architectures critics propose instead of the substrate — each treated seriously, each shown to fail at scale or against the adversaries §18-§28 named.

The next section (§19) walks cryptographic attacks. The substrate's reliance on ed25519 and blake3 is a load-bearing dependency; §19 engages every plausible threat to that dependency and specifies the migration paths if the threats are realized.
## §19. Cryptographic attacks

The substrate's load-bearing cryptographic primitives are ed25519 (signatures) and blake3 (hashes), composed via canonical bincode serialization. Every byte's identity is a blake3 hash. Every transition is signed by ed25519. Every state-hash supermajority commit is blake3 over a canonically-serialized state. If either primitive is broken, or if the composition is broken, or if implementation flaws expose the primitives' security properties, the substrate's cryptographic layer fails and the substrate's higher-level claims fail with it.

This section walks seven cryptographic attack classes against the four-element rubric specified in §18.3: the attack named precisely, the defense specified, the residual risk acknowledged, the operational response specified. The attacks are §19.1 signature forgery, §19.2 hash collision, §19.3 replay attacks, §19.4 key compromise, §19.5 quantum adversaries, §19.6 weak randomness, §19.7 timing side channels. §19.8 closes by specifying the substrate's cryptographic-primitive migration path — the operational mechanism by which the substrate transitions to successor primitives if the current ones are broken.

By the close of §19, the reader should accept that the substrate's cryptographic layer is conservative, well-defended for known threats, honestly acknowledged as conditional on the underlying primitives' continued security, and equipped with a documented migration path for when the conditions fail.

### 19.1 Signature forgery

**Attack.** An adversary produces an ed25519 signature that verifies against a victim's public key without possessing the victim's private key. If successful, the adversary forges transitions: transferring the victim's bytes, minting bytes claiming to be from a registered issuer, or producing fraudulent state-hash attestations that other cluster members would accept as valid.

The attack succeeds if any of the following holds: (a) the discrete-log problem in the ed25519 group becomes efficiently solvable, breaking the security assumption underlying the entire signature scheme; (b) an implementation flaw exposes the private key during signing or storage; (c) the signature scheme's malleability properties allow modification of valid signatures into other valid signatures over different messages; (d) the canonical message construction has ambiguity allowing two distinct semantic interpretations of the same byte sequence.

**Defense.** Ed25519's security against signature forgery without private-key access reduces to the discrete-log problem on edwards25519, which has been studied since Curve25519's 2006 introduction and has no known efficient classical attack. The discrete-log problem on this curve is at the same security level (~128-bit symmetric equivalent) as the underlying assumptions for TLS 1.3, SSH, and the bulk of the modern internet's cryptographic infrastructure. The substrate inherits the security level the wider ecosystem depends on.

Implementation hardening: the substrate's reference implementation uses the `ed25519-dalek` Rust crate, which is itself derived from the curve25519-dalek implementation Bernstein and Lange consulted on.
