The implementation enforces strict signature encoding (rejecting non-canonical scalars per the EdDSA strictness guidance from Chalkias et al., "Taming the many EdDSAs," 2020), eliminating the malleability attack vectors that earlier permissive ed25519 implementations exposed. Conforming implementations in other languages MUST follow the same strictness rules; the substrate's conformance specification gives the canonical signature-verification predicate.

Canonical message construction: every signed message in the substrate is constructed by a single canonical-serialization function whose output is byte-deterministic across implementations. The signed preimages explicitly bind the byte ID, the previous history hash, and the frame number, preventing semantic ambiguity attacks where the same signature could be valid for multiple distinct transitions.

**Residual risk.** Three residual risks remain. First, if a polynomial-time discrete-log algorithm on edwards25519 is published, the entire signature layer fails simultaneously — every ed25519 signature in the substrate becomes potentially forgeable, including historical signatures that have already been verified. The substrate would need to migrate to a successor primitive (§19.8) under emergency conditions. Second, side-channel leakage of private keys during signing on hostile hardware (timing channels, power analysis on embedded devices) can compromise individual keys without breaking the signature scheme as a whole; affected users lose control of their bytes but other users' bytes remain secure. Third, implementations that fail to enforce strict signature encoding can introduce malleability in their local domain; conforming implementations are unaffected, but a non-conforming implementation in production is a risk to its own users.

**Operational response.** A discrete-log break on edwards25519 triggers the substrate-wide cryptographic migration protocol (§19.8). Individual key compromise is handled by the user re-issuing themselves a new keypair and (where the cargo type and issuer support it) requesting issuer assistance to invalidate or transfer bytes signed under the compromised key — recoverability depends on the issuer's policy and is honestly partial. Implementation-level malleability bugs are handled by the substrate's conformance test vectors, which any implementation must pass before being trusted by other implementations; non-conforming implementations are excluded from cluster admission.

### 19.2 Hash collision

**Attack.** An adversary finds two distinct inputs that hash to the same blake3 output. If successful, the adversary can produce two different bytes with the same identity, two different histories with the same chain hash, or two different states with the same state-hash. Collisions allow the substrate to be tricked into accepting one byte's history as another's, conflating distinct bytes, or producing forged state agreements.

The attack succeeds if the underlying compression function in blake3 has a structural weakness allowing efficient collision generation, or if the construction of the canonical serialization input to blake3 allows two semantically distinct objects to produce identical byte sequences (a serialization-level collision rather than a hash-level collision).

**Defense.** Blake3's collision resistance is inherited from BLAKE2's compression function, which has resisted cryptanalytic attack since its 2012 publication and which itself derived from BLAKE, a SHA-3 finalist that received extensive cryptanalytic scrutiny in 2008-2012. The blake3 specification (O'Connor, Aumasson, Neves, Wilcox-O'Hearn, January 2020) makes the security assumption explicit and the assumption has held in the four years since publication. The substrate inherits ~128-bit collision resistance and ~256-bit preimage resistance, both well above the threshold required for byte-level commerce at substrate scale.

Canonical serialization: bincode (the substrate's serialization format) is deterministic and unambiguous for the substrate's data structures — every input produces exactly one byte sequence, and every byte sequence parses to exactly one input or fails. The substrate's canonical-serialization function is specified to byte-level precision in the conformance specification, eliminating the input-ambiguity class of collision attacks.

**Residual risk.** Two residual risks. First, if blake3's compression function is broken — if a structural attack is published reducing its collision resistance below ~80 bits — the substrate's identity, history, and state-hashing all fail simultaneously, requiring substrate-wide migration. Second, the canonical serialization is correct only if every conforming implementation produces byte-identical output for the same input; an implementation bug producing non-canonical serialization could allow that implementation's nodes to disagree with conforming implementations on identity hashes, breaking interoperability without anyone's blake3 being broken. Conformance vectors detect this in testing.

**Operational response.** A blake3 break triggers substrate-wide migration to a successor primitive (§19.8). Serialization-level interoperability bugs are handled by conformance vector failure: a non-conforming implementation fails the test suite and is excluded from cluster admission until the bug is fixed. The substrate has no mechanism to retroactively re-hash already-existing bytes if blake3 is broken; affected bytes maintain their original identities and histories under the broken primitive, and recovery depends on the migration protocol preserving the chain of historical evidence even as the primitive changes.

### 19.3 Replay attacks

**Attack.** An adversary captures a valid signed transition and re-submits it in a different context — a different byte, a different cluster, a different frame — hoping that the substrate's verification accepts the signature as valid despite the changed context. If successful, the adversary produces unauthorized transfers by recycling captured signatures.

**Defense.** The substrate's signature preimage for a transfer transition explicitly binds (byte ID, previous history hash, from pubkey, to pubkey, frame number). Each component cryptographically scopes the signature: the byte ID prevents transition-replay across distinct bytes; the previous history hash prevents replay at a different point in the same byte's history (because once the byte has advanced, the previous history hash no longer matches); the frame number prevents replay across different frames; the to pubkey prevents the recipient from being substituted.

For mints, the analogous preimage binds (cargo kind, amount, recipient, nonce). The nonce is unique per mint within an issuer's signing history, preventing replay of mint signatures with otherwise-identical content. The substrate's byte-ID computation additionally includes the frame number in the canonical origin attestation, so a replayed mint message processed in a different frame produces a different byte ID and is treated as a distinct (and possibly duplicate) byte.

**Residual risk.** Replay attacks are structurally defeated by the preimage binding when the binding is correctly implemented and verified. The residual risk is implementation-level: an implementation that omits any of the preimage components from its signature verification, or that fails to verify the signature against the canonical preimage, opens replay attacks within its own domain. The substrate's conformance vectors include adversarial replay-attack vectors to detect this in testing.

**Operational response.** Conforming implementations are not vulnerable to replay attacks at the protocol level. A non-conforming implementation that exposed replay attacks would produce verifiable evidence (transitions whose canonical signature verification fails on a conforming implementation) and the affected bytes would be detectably corrupted. Recovery is via the same chain-verification procedure (§10.2) any external party would use to detect tampering.

### 19.4 Key compromise

**Attack.** An adversary obtains a victim's ed25519 private key — through theft, extortion, malware, supply-chain attack, side-channel leakage, or backup-system compromise. With the private key, the adversary can produce valid signatures over any preimage, transferring the victim's bytes, signing as the victim's issuer (if the victim is an issuer), or impersonating the victim in any signed protocol message.

**Defense.** The substrate's defense against key compromise is *containment, not prevention*. The substrate cannot prevent private keys from being stolen any more than the broader cryptographic ecosystem can. What the substrate provides is a content-addressed, cryptographically-verifiable history that scopes the damage of any key compromise to the bytes signed under that key during the compromise window.

Three layers of containment apply. First, the substrate's pseudonymity discipline (§6.2) encourages users to maintain separate pubkeys per relationship or per cargo class, limiting any single key compromise to the bytes that key controls. Second, the issuer registry (§15.1) is governance-mutable, meaning a compromised issuer key can be rotated through the governance flow (planned for v0.4) without affecting the cluster's ability to validate previously-issued bytes. Third, layer-2 services (custodial wallets with rate limiting and anomaly detection, social-recovery key systems, hardware-token signing) reduce the probability of key compromise and its consequences without modifying the substrate's protocol layer.

The substrate's *cryptographic chain provenance* (§10) ensures that even after a key compromise, the chain of legitimate transitions before the compromise remains verifiable. The compromise damages the user's *future* signing authority over their bytes; it does not retroactively invalidate the bytes' history.

**Residual risk.** The substrate cannot recover bytes whose private keys have been compromised and whose owners were unaware long enough for the adversary to transfer the bytes to themselves. Once a transition is committed by BFT supermajority, it is final; no protocol-level reversal mechanism exists. Layer-2 services may offer reversibility wrappers (escrow with timed release, conditional transfers), but these compose on top of the substrate rather than modifying its irreversibility. Issuers may, at their discretion and per their cargo-type policies, agree to invalidate bytes signed under a confirmed-compromised key and mint replacement bytes — but this is an issuer-level operation, not a substrate-level guarantee.

**Operational response.** A user who detects key compromise rotates to a new keypair and (where applicable) requests issuer-level remediation for affected bytes. The affected bytes' subsequent fate depends on whether the adversary has already transferred them: if they have, the bytes are lost to the user (subject to issuer remediation); if they have not, the user can transfer the bytes to a new keypair before the adversary acts. The substrate's role is to make the chain of events cryptographically auditable; the substrate does not perform recovery. Long-term, the substrate's design discourages users from holding large quantities of bytes under a single key for exactly this reason; the granularity of per-byte ownership (§8.4) makes pubkey diversification natural.

### 19.5 Quantum adversaries

**Attack.** An adversary with a sufficiently large fault-tolerant quantum computer runs Shor's algorithm to compute discrete logarithms on edwards25519, breaking ed25519 signatures globally. The same adversary runs Grover's algorithm to weaken blake3's preimage resistance from 256 bits to 128 bits and collision resistance from 128 bits to roughly 85 bits. If both attacks become feasible, the substrate's cryptographic foundation fails simultaneously.

**Defense.** No defense against a sufficiently capable quantum adversary exists at the protocol layer. Ed25519's security relies on the hardness of discrete log on edwards25519, which Shor's algorithm efficiently solves on a fault-tolerant quantum computer. Resource estimates have converged downward as algorithmic optimizations have matured: Roetteler-Naehrig-Svore-Lauter (2017) estimated approximately 2330 logical qubits for 256-bit elliptic-curve discrete log; Häner-Jaques-Naehrig-Roetteler-Soeken (2020) refined to comparable ranges; Babbush-Zalcman-Gidney et al. (2025) demonstrated under 1500 logical qubits. The trend is toward lower logical-qubit requirements and higher physical-qubit overhead via quantum error correction; current full-stack physical-qubit estimates remain in the millions. Blake3's preimage resistance is reduced by Grover's algorithm, but blake3's 256-bit output means even Grover-reduced collision resistance remains at ~85 bits — uncomfortably close to brute-force territory but not yet fully broken.

The substrate's defense is *temporal*: ed25519 and blake3 are conservative for the next 10-20 years against publicly-known quantum compute trajectories. The current state of fault-tolerant quantum computing (2025-2026: a few hundred logical qubits at most, with high error rates) is far below the threshold required for Shor-on-edwards25519 even at the most optimistic logical-qubit estimates. Public expert estimates of when fault-tolerant quantum computing reaches Shor-on-elliptic-curve scale range from 10 years (optimistic) to 30+ years (pessimistic); the Mosca framework for cryptographic risk management ("X+Y < Z" where X is migration time, Y is data sensitivity timeline, Z is time until quantum threat) suggests migration planning should begin now even if the threat itself is years away.

The substrate ships in 2026 with ed25519/blake3. By the time post-quantum migration becomes urgent, post-quantum signature standards will have matured: NIST released FIPS 203 (ML-KEM, derived from CRYSTALS-Kyber), FIPS 204 (ML-DSA, derived from CRYSTALS-Dilithium), and FIPS 205 (SLH-DSA, derived from SPHINCS+) on August 13, 2024, completing an eight-year standardization process. FALCON is in the NIST signature queue for future standardization. Each standardized primitive is deployable today, just not at the substrate's performance/key-size points. The substrate's migration protocol (§19.8) is pre-specified for post-quantum migration; the migration is engineering work, not protocol-design work.

**Residual risk.** Three residual risks. First, "harvest now, decrypt later" — a quantum-capable adversary today could be capturing substrate-network traffic and ed25519-signed transitions, planning to retroactively forge or decrypt them once quantum hardware matures. The substrate's signed transitions, however, are publicly-broadcast and content-addressed; an adversary who later derives private keys from public keys (via Shor's) would be able to forge *new* transitions but not retroactively alter *committed* ones. Second, the timeline could be wrong. If a major cryptanalytic breakthrough or a faster-than-expected quantum advance compresses the migration window from years to months, the substrate may not migrate in time. Third, post-quantum primitives have larger keys and signatures (FALCON: ~1KB signatures vs ed25519's 64 bytes; ML-DSA: similar order of magnitude), which would change the substrate's per-byte cost structure and require operational adaptation.

**Operational response.** Quantum-readiness is monitored via standard cryptographic-community channels (NIST publications, IACR conference proceedings, public quantum-hardware roadmaps). When fault-tolerant quantum compute approaches the discrete-log threshold (estimated 5-10 years before actual feasibility, based on standard cryptographic risk-management practice), the substrate initiates the migration protocol (§19.8), shipping post-quantum primitive support in parallel with ed25519/blake3 for a transition period before retiring the legacy primitives. The migration is a coordinated substrate-wide event, not a per-byte event; bytes maintain their pre-migration identities, with new bytes minted under post-quantum primitives and existing bytes auditable under both regimes during the transition.

### 19.6 Weak randomness

**Attack.** An adversary predicts or biases the random values the substrate uses for keypair generation, nonce selection, or randomness-beacon contributions. If predictable, an adversary may pre-compute private keys from public keys, forge nonces to collide with future legitimate nonces, or bias randomness beacons to produce favorable cluster-split partitions or input ordering.

**Defense.** The substrate inherits the operating system's cryptographic randomness primitive (`/dev/urandom` on Linux, `BCryptGenRandom` on Windows, `getentropy()` on BSD/macOS). These primitives have received substantial cryptanalytic scrutiny and are generally considered secure when the underlying entropy collection is healthy. The substrate's reference implementation uses `rand_chacha::ChaCha20Rng` seeded from OS entropy for high-volume needs, and direct OS entropy for keypair generation.

Nonce uniqueness: ed25519 signatures are deterministic by construction (nonces derived from the private key plus message hash, per RFC 8032 Section 5.1.6), eliminating the entire class of nonce-reuse vulnerabilities that plagued ECDSA. The substrate's mint nonces are 16-byte values, with collision probability on the order of 2⁻⁶⁴ per same-cargo-same-issuer mint pair — adequate for any plausible mint volume.

Randomness beacons (used for cluster-split partition functions, §11.3.2): the substrate's design (v0.4 specification work) draws beacon values from the contributions of all cluster members at the SplitFrame, requiring an adversary to control a supermajority of cluster members to bias the beacon. This is design-only in v0.3.

**Residual risk.** Three residual risks. First, embedded systems with weak entropy sources (early-boot states, virtualized environments without paravirt entropy) can produce predictable keypairs; this is a long-known issue across cryptographic systems and the substrate inherits it. Second, the v0.4 randomness-beacon design is unimplemented and untested; the design's correctness depends on supermajority-honesty assumptions that may turn out to have edge cases. Third, deterministic ed25519 nonces, while secure under standard assumptions, fail catastrophically under fault-injection attacks where an adversary can cause the same private-key-and-message pair to produce two different signatures with different nonces. Fault-injection is a hardware-level concern that the substrate cannot defend against at the protocol layer.

**Operational response.** Embedded-system entropy issues are mitigated by operational discipline: substrate nodes should not run on systems with weak entropy at keypair-generation time, and key-generation tooling should verify entropy quality before producing keys. The v0.4 randomness-beacon design will be specified, implemented, and tested with adversarial scenarios before being relied on for production cluster splitting. Fault-injection on signing devices is the user's operational concern; high-value users should use hardware tokens with fault-injection resistance.

### 19.7 Timing side channels

**Attack.** An adversary with timing access to a node's signing or verification operations measures the time taken and infers private-key material. Classical examples: ECDSA implementations whose signing time correlates with the leading bits of the nonce, allowing recovery of the private key after enough signatures are observed. Modern variants: cache-timing attacks, branch-prediction attacks, hyperthreading-shared-state attacks.

**Defense.** Ed25519's design eliminates many timing channels that ECDSA and RSA implementations had to defend against. The signing algorithm has no secret-dependent branches, no secret-dependent table lookups, and no secret-dependent loop counts. Reference implementations (curve25519-dalek and its derivatives) are constant-time across all standard inputs. The substrate's reference implementation inherits this property.

Verification, which does not involve the private key, is less timing-sensitive but the implementation still avoids secret-dependent timing in the rare cases where it could matter (signature-malleability rejection paths, for example).

**Residual risk.** Two residual risks. First, the constant-time guarantee depends on the compiler not emitting secret-dependent branches or memory accesses; aggressive optimization passes can occasionally introduce timing channels that the source code does not have. The substrate's reference implementation pins compiler versions and includes timing-validation tests in the conformance suite to detect this. Second, side-channel attacks beyond timing — power analysis on embedded devices, electromagnetic-emission analysis on cryptographic accelerators — can compromise keys regardless of constant-time software. These are hardware-level concerns the substrate cannot defend against.

**Operational response.** Timing-channel discoveries in ed25519 implementations are tracked via the cryptographic community's standard channels (CVE database, the ed25519 maintainers' security advisories, the relevant Rust ecosystem advisories). The substrate's implementation upgrades when timing-channel patches are released. Hardware-level side-channel concerns are the user's operational responsibility; high-value users should use hardware tokens designed against side-channel attacks (Yubikey 5, Solokey, Trezor with safe-firmware).

### 19.8 The cryptographic-primitive migration path

If any of the substrate's cryptographic primitives is broken — by classical cryptanalysis, by quantum adversary, or by implementation flaw rendering the primitive unsafe even when correctly implemented — the substrate must migrate to successor primitives. This subsection specifies the migration path. The path is design-only in v0.3; v0.4 will implement the migration protocol's lockstep components.

**Stage 1: announcement and assessment.** The substrate's authors and the broader cryptographic community announce the threat. The substrate's authors assess: does the threat affect ed25519, blake3, both, or other primitives the substrate depends on? Is the threat publicly known and reproducible, or is it a credible report from a trusted source? What is the migration urgency — months, years, or immediate?

**Stage 2: successor selection.** The substrate's authors propose successor primitives chosen from the existing cryptographic standardization landscape. For ed25519 replacement: ML-DSA (Dilithium-derived NIST FIPS 204 standard, 2024) or FALCON for size-constrained deployments. For blake3 replacement: SHA-3 if the threat is specific to blake3's compression function, or a future post-quantum hash like SPHINCS+'s underlying primitives. The successor choice is open for community comment for a configurable period (target: 90 days for non-emergency, shorter for emergency).

**Stage 3: dual-mode operation.** The substrate's protocol gains a primitive-version field that distinguishes legacy bytes (ed25519/blake3) from new bytes (successor primitives). Both versions are valid simultaneously during the migration period. Existing bytes retain their original identities and histories; new bytes use the successor primitives. Cluster lockstep handles both versions in the same transition logic, with version-appropriate verification per transition.

**Stage 4: migration window.** Users are encouraged to transfer their legacy bytes to new keypairs under the successor primitives, with issuer support where available. Issuers may offer migration services (re-issuing existing bytes under successor primitives in exchange for the originals). The migration window is a configurable period (target: 12-24 months for non-emergency, shorter for emergency).

**Stage 5: legacy retirement.** After the migration window, the substrate's protocol stops accepting transitions signed under the legacy primitives. Bytes still under legacy primitives remain in cluster state but are immobilized: they exist, they verify, but they cannot be transferred under the legacy keys. Users who failed to migrate during the window have lost the ability to transfer their bytes, and recovery requires issuer cooperation or layer-2 dispute resolution.

**Stage 6: legacy archive.** A frozen copy of the cluster state at retirement is preserved for forensic and audit purposes. Anyone with the cluster's BFT-signed state attestations from before retirement can verify the legacy bytes' histories using the legacy primitives. The archive does not actively process new transitions; it is read-only.

The migration's correctness depends on (a) the successor primitives being secure under the threat that broke the legacy primitives, (b) the dual-mode operation handling both versions correctly without introducing new vulnerabilities, (c) the migration window being long enough for users and issuers to coordinate, and (d) the legacy retirement being executed simultaneously across the substrate's clusters to prevent split-brain operation. Each is a real engineering challenge; v0.4 specifications will address each.

**Residual risk.** A migration to successor primitives is a substrate-wide coordinated event. If it is executed under emergency conditions (legacy primitive broken with active exploitation), the migration window shortens and users may lose bytes they could not migrate in time. If it is executed under non-emergency conditions, the migration is more orderly but less urgent. The substrate's design assumes the cryptographic community provides advance warning of impending breaks (decade-scale for quantum, year-scale for classical cryptanalytic advances), which has been the historical pattern. A short-warning break (a polynomial-time discrete-log algorithm announced without warning) compresses the migration window and increases byte-loss risk.

**Operational response.** Substrate authors maintain active monitoring of cryptographic-community channels and commit to initiating the migration protocol within 30 days of any credible threat to a load-bearing primitive becoming public. Implementation vendors maintain successor-primitive support in their codebases, ready to enable on protocol-version change. Issuers commit to offering migration services for their cargo types. Users who hold significant byte value should diversify across keypairs and consider layer-2 services with built-in migration support.

### 19.9 Composition with other categories

The cryptographic attacks engaged in §19 compose with other categories' defenses in three ways.

**Composition one: cryptographic verification underlies every other defense.** The BFT supermajority commit (§13) relies on ed25519 signatures and blake3 hashes; if either primitive is broken, BFT fails. The dissent log (§14) relies on the same primitives; if either is broken, dissent attribution becomes untrustworthy. Content-addressed history (§10) relies on hash collision resistance; if blake3 is broken, the entire history-verification claim collapses. Cryptographic security is therefore the foundation; every other defense is conditional on it.

**Composition two: cryptographic attacks are typically protocol-class-one (cryptographic) but enable adversary-class-two (protocol-level) attacks.** A signature-forgery break enables the adversary to mount protocol-level attacks against any cluster — submitting forged transitions, producing forged state hashes, impersonating any user or issuer. Cryptographic attacks are therefore force-multipliers across §20 (lockstep protocol) and §21 (cluster topology) attacks.

**Composition three: cryptographic migration is a cross-cutting operational concern.** The migration path specified in §19.8 affects every other layer of the substrate — cluster topology (existing clusters must coordinate the migration), state persistence (checkpoints must support both primitive versions during migration), transports (no direct effect, since the transport layer is primitive-agnostic), and issuer registries (issuers must support both versions during migration). The migration is operationally complex precisely because cryptographic primitives are foundational, and §19.8's specification is the load-bearing operational document for the migration's success.

The next section (§20) walks lockstep protocol attacks. Where §19 engaged threats to the cryptographic primitives the substrate's lockstep relies on, §20 engages threats to the lockstep protocol itself — determinism breaks, ordering attacks, timing manipulation, and the broader class of attacks that target the substrate's consensus mechanism rather than its cryptographic foundation.
## §20. Lockstep protocol attacks

The substrate's lockstep deterministic simulation (§9) is the substrate's consensus mechanism. Every honest node executes the same deterministic state transition over the same canonically-ordered inputs, producing byte-identical state across the cluster, with cryptographic supermajority commit (§13) detecting any divergence. The lockstep protocol's correctness rests on two properties: every honest node executes the same operations in the same order, and the operations are themselves deterministic. Attacks that violate either property — by introducing nondeterminism, by manipulating ordering, by injecting timing or clock effects, by manipulating message delivery — threaten the substrate's consensus.

This section walks eight attack classes against the four-element rubric (§18.3). The attacks are §20.1 floating-point determinism break, §20.2 input-ordering determinism break, §20.3 timestamp manipulation, §20.4 clock skew, §20.5 network-layer reordering, §20.6 message duplication, §20.7 message dropping, §20.8 partition healing exploits. §20.9 closes by specifying the substrate's protocol-level invariants — the structural properties that, if maintained, are sufficient for lockstep correctness regardless of which specific implementation choices a conforming implementation makes.

By the close of §20, the reader should accept that the substrate's lockstep protocol is robust against the realistic attack surface a determined adversary at the protocol layer can present, that the residual risks are honestly named, and that the protocol's invariants are auditable rather than depending on the substrate's authors' good faith.

### 20.1 Floating-point determinism break

**Attack.** An adversary submits inputs whose deterministic transition involves floating-point arithmetic. Different implementations, different CPUs, or different compiler optimizations produce subtly different results — IEEE 754 operations vary in associativity, in handling of denormals, in fused-multiply-add precision, in compiler reorderings of expressions like `a + b + c`. The cluster nodes diverge: each computes a slightly different post-frame state hash. Supermajority commit fails. The cluster halts with `CLUSTER SPLIT` (§13.3 outcome four), or the divergent minority halts with `MINORITY DIVERGENCE` (outcome three). The adversary has caused a denial-of-service via legitimate-looking inputs.

**Defense.** The substrate's state-transition function forbids floating-point arithmetic entirely. All numeric values in the substrate's state — cargo amounts, joule costs, frame numbers, timestamps as discrete units — are represented as fixed-precision integers (i64 or u64). The reference implementation uses `Fixed64` for cargo amounts, with a documented scaling factor of 6 decimal places (so $1.50 USD-byte amount is stored as `1_500_000`). All arithmetic on cargo values uses integer operations with overflow checks; the operations are byte-deterministic across CPUs and compilers.

The substrate's conformance specification mandates integer-only arithmetic in the state-transition function. Implementations that introduce floating-point operations into transition logic — even via library calls that internally use floating-point — fail conformance vectors that include adversarial floating-point-edge-case inputs.

**Residual risk.** Two residual risks. First, the substrate's higher-level layer-2 services (price-discovery oracles, exchange-rate calculators, statistical-aggregation tools) may use floating-point internally; the substrate's protocol is unaffected, but the layer-2 services may produce results that diverge across implementations and that should not be trusted as substrate-level facts. Second, an implementation bug that accidentally introduces floating-point operations (a developer using `f64` instead of `i64` in a hot path) could pass cursory review but would be caught by the conformance suite's adversarial vectors. The conformance suite is the load-bearing defense.

**Operational response.** A floating-point-determinism-break attack against a conforming implementation cluster fails — every node computes the same integer result. If a non-conforming implementation is in the cluster, its hash diverges from the supermajority and the dissent log (§14) accumulates evidence; the non-conforming implementation is identified and excluded via cluster expulsion (planned v0.4). Operators monitor implementation diversity and flag any implementation that fails conformance vectors.

### 20.2 Input-ordering determinism break

**Attack.** An adversary exploits the cluster's input-ordering algorithm. The substrate's protocol orders the union of all peer inputs by canonical hash sort (§9.4 step 4): each input is serialized, blake3-hashed, and sorted by hash. If two implementations sort differently — different tiebreaking on equal-hash inputs (which should never occur for distinct inputs but could under edge cases), different byte-ordering interpretation, different hash representation — the cluster nodes process inputs in different orders, producing divergent state.

A more subtle variant: the canonical-serialization function is not byte-deterministic across implementations. Two conforming implementations agree on what the data structure is but encode it slightly differently — different field orderings, different optional-field representations, different integer-encoding rules. The hash inputs differ, the hashes differ, the sort orders differ.

**Defense.** The substrate's canonical serialization is specified to byte-level precision in the conformance specification. Bincode (the reference implementation's serialization format) is deterministic for the substrate's data structures: every input produces exactly one byte sequence, with field ordering, integer encoding, and optional handling all specified. Conformance vectors include adversarial inputs designed to surface serialization-level differences — empty collections, maximum-value integers, edge-case Unicode strings.

The hash sort itself is mechanically simple: sort by lexicographic byte order on the 32-byte blake3 output. There is no tiebreaking ambiguity because blake3's collision resistance makes equal-hash inputs from distinct preimages essentially impossible (probability ~2⁻¹²⁸ per pair). The conformance specification gives the sort algorithm in pseudocode; conforming implementations must produce identical orderings on the same input set.

**Residual risk.** Same residual risk as §19.2 (hash collision): if blake3 collisions become efficient, the canonical hash sort becomes manipulable, but the substrate's failure mode in that case is the cryptographic-migration scenario (§19.8), not a lockstep failure. Implementation-level serialization bugs are caught by conformance vectors; an implementation that passes the conformance suite but has a corner case the suite doesn't cover would produce divergent state hashes and be detected through dissent log accumulation.

**Operational response.** Conforming implementations agree on input ordering. A non-conforming implementation that orders inputs differently produces divergent state hashes and is excluded via dissent log + cluster expulsion. The substrate's operational discipline includes: every implementation MUST pass the conformance suite before joining production clusters; conformance vectors are versioned with the protocol; protocol upgrades require updated conformance vectors that all participating implementations must pass.

### 20.3 Timestamp manipulation

**Attack.** An adversary submits inputs containing fabricated timestamps — claiming a transition happened at a specific wall-clock time the adversary chooses, or claiming the cluster's current frame corresponds to a wall-clock time the adversary biases. If the substrate's transition logic depends on wall-clock timestamps for any decision (expiration of time-bound cargo, time-locked transfers, rate limiting), the adversary manipulates the decision by fabricating timestamps.

**Defense.** The substrate's lockstep protocol does not use wall-clock time anywhere in the state-transition function. The only time-like value the substrate's state contains is the frame number, which is deterministic by construction (incremented by one per committed frame, never set to a wall-clock-derived value). Frame numbers are monotonic, identical across all honest nodes, and forgeable only to the extent that a Byzantine majority could rewrite the cluster's state — which is a §21 cluster-topology attack, not a §20 timing attack.

Time-bound cargo (cargo with expiration semantics) is implemented at the layer above the substrate. The cargo specification can include a "valid until frame N" field; transitions involving the cargo after frame N are still cryptographically valid at the substrate level but are rejected by the cargo's application logic. The substrate stays out of wall-clock-time concerns; layer-2 services handle time-sensitive cargo by interpreting frame numbers and consulting external time-attestation mechanisms.

**Residual risk.** Three residual risks. First, layer-2 services that bridge frame numbers to wall-clock time (frame-N happened at approximately time T) are themselves a trust point; an adversary who controls a time-attestation service can mislead applications about when frame-N occurred. Second, applications that need wall-clock-precision timing — high-frequency-trading-equivalent latency claims, regulatory timestamping requirements — are not well-served by the substrate's frame-number-only design and must compose with external time sources. Third, some legitimate use cases (transaction "happened before noon UTC on a specific date") require wall-clock anchoring that the substrate provides only indirectly.

**Operational response.** Wall-clock manipulation does not affect substrate-level consensus. Applications that need wall-clock-anchored decisions use external time-attestation services (RFC 3161 timestamping authorities, NIST signed-time services, or substrate-level layer-2 services that publish frame-to-wall-clock attestations periodically). The substrate's operational guidance is: do not depend on wall-clock time within the substrate's transition function; if your application needs wall-clock time, layer it on top.

### 20.4 Clock skew

**Attack.** Different cluster nodes have skewed wall clocks — not because of attack, but because clock synchronization is hard. A node whose clock is 10 minutes ahead of its peers makes decisions about message timing, frame budgets, and timeout values that differ from its peers. Over time, this can produce divergent behavior even when the protocol logic itself is deterministic.

A more aggressive variant: an adversary deliberately runs nodes with manipulated clocks, hoping to exploit timing-dependent code paths in the substrate's protocol or transports.

**Defense.** The substrate's lockstep protocol does not depend on clock synchronization across nodes for correctness. The protocol's progress is driven by message exchange, not wall-clock time. A node's frame counter advances when its cluster commits a frame, regardless of how long that took in wall-clock time.

Two places where clock-like values appear in implementation: timeout values for missing-peer detection (§11.3.6, default tens of seconds) and checkpoint cadence (§16.4, default every 10,000 frames). Neither value affects the substrate's state-transition function or the cryptographic agreement protocol; they are operational tunings within each node's local discretion. A node with a skewed clock might detect a peer as missing slightly earlier or later than other nodes would, but the cluster's BFT supermajority handles this — the slow-detecting node's vote on missing-peer status is just one of n votes.

**Residual risk.** Two residual risks. First, severely skewed clocks (hours or days off) could cause a node to mistakenly mark all peers as missing simultaneously or to fail to detect a genuine missing peer for an extended time. This is an operational issue, not a protocol-level vulnerability: cluster operators monitor clock drift and correct it. Second, an adversary deliberately running with manipulated clocks could potentially exploit subtle timing-window edge cases in transport layers (TCP keep-alive intervals, QUIC connection-migration timing); the substrate's protocol layer is unaffected, but operational monitoring should detect persistent clock-skew anomalies.

**Operational response.** Cluster nodes run NTP or equivalent clock-synchronization services as a matter of operational hygiene. The substrate's operational documentation specifies that clock skew greater than a configurable threshold (suggested: 60 seconds) should trigger an operator alert. An adversary deliberately running with skewed clocks is detectable by anomaly monitoring and is handled via cluster expulsion if the adversary is otherwise meeting their lockstep obligations, or via missing-peer protocol if they are not.

### 20.5 Network-layer reordering

**Attack.** An adversary at the network layer (a man-in-the-middle, a malicious ISP, a state-level network operator) reorders messages between nodes, hoping to cause inputs to be processed in a different order than the protocol's canonical hash sort would dictate. If successful, the adversary forces nodes into divergent state.

**Defense.** The substrate's canonical hash sort (§9.4 step 4) defeats network-layer reordering by construction. Each node receives the union of all peer inputs for the frame, sorts them by canonical hash, and applies them in sorted order. The order in which the inputs *arrived* is irrelevant; the order in which they are *processed* is the canonical-hash-sorted order. As long as every honest node receives the same union of inputs, every honest node processes them identically.

The "as long as" condition is enforced by the protocol's input-exchange phase (§9.4 step 2-3): every node broadcasts its inputs to every peer; every node waits to receive inputs from every peer; the union is complete before processing begins. Phase buffering (§12) handles the case where messages from frame N+1 arrive before frame N's input phase completes.

**Residual risk.** Network-layer reordering attacks are structurally defeated by the canonical hash sort. The residual risk is implementation-level: an implementation that processes inputs in arrival order rather than canonical-hash-sorted order is non-conforming and produces divergent state, but the conformance vectors include adversarial inputs designed to surface this. A more sophisticated network-layer attack might selectively *drop* messages rather than reorder them; this is engaged in §20.7.

**Operational response.** Network-layer reordering against conforming implementations is benign — the canonical sort defeats it. Non-conforming implementations are detected via dissent log and excluded.

### 20.6 Message duplication

**Attack.** An adversary at the network layer duplicates messages — re-sending the same `InputBatch` or `StateHash` message multiple times. If the receiver's protocol logic does not deduplicate, the duplicate may be processed as two distinct inputs, doubling the apparent set of pending operations or interfering with the supermajority commit's tally.

**Defense.** The substrate's protocol deduplicates at multiple layers. At the input layer: each input has a canonical hash (used for the canonical sort), and an input that has already been processed for the current frame is silently dropped on duplicate receipt. Identical inputs from different peers are deduplicated by canonical hash. At the state-hash layer: each peer's hash for a given frame is recorded once; subsequent receipts of the same `StateHash` message from the same peer are silently ignored. Duplicate `StateHash` messages from a single peer claiming different hashes for the same frame are a Byzantine-behavior signal and are recorded in the dissent log.

The reference implementation enforces these deduplications via the `Node::recv_filtered` logic (which already handles phase buffering, §12) and via per-frame hash-set tracking of received messages.

**Residual risk.** Implementation-level: an implementation that fails to deduplicate produces incorrect state and is detected via supermajority commit divergence. The conformance vectors include adversarial duplicate-message scenarios.

**Operational response.** Deduplication is structural; no operational response is required for conforming implementations. Non-conforming implementations are excluded via dissent log.

### 20.7 Message dropping

**Attack.** An adversary at the network layer drops messages — selectively, deliberately, to prevent specific inputs from reaching specific peers. If a single peer fails to receive a message that other peers received, that peer's union of inputs differs from the others', producing divergent state.

A more strategic variant: an adversary drops messages from specific submitters, preventing those submitters' transactions from being processed. This is an availability attack against specific users rather than a consensus attack against the cluster.

**Defense.** The substrate's protocol handles missing messages at the supermajority commit layer (§13). If a node fails to receive a peer's input batch, that node's union of inputs is incomplete; the node's frame state will diverge from the supermajority. The supermajority commit detects the divergence; the dropping node halts with `MINORITY DIVERGENCE` and operator intervention restores connectivity.

For the strategic variant (dropping specific submitters' transactions), the substrate's input-distribution mechanism (§9.4 step 2: every node broadcasts to every peer) provides redundancy: a transaction submitted to any cluster node is broadcast to all peers, and an adversary would need to drop messages between every cluster node simultaneously to prevent the transaction from being processed. This is a state-level network attack that the substrate cannot fully defend against (an adversary with control of the global internet routing for the cluster's IP space can do this), but it is an extraordinarily expensive attack.

**Residual risk.** Three residual risks. First, an adversary with control of a single peer's network connection can drop messages to that peer, causing it to halt — which is denial-of-service against that peer but not consensus corruption against the cluster. Second, an adversary controlling the network between *all* cluster peers can prevent the cluster from making progress entirely; this is a cluster-level availability attack handled by geographic distribution of cluster nodes (§13.5). Third, an adversary controlling a specific user's network access can prevent that user's submissions from reaching any cluster node; the user's recourse is to use a different network path (different ISP, different geographic location, Tor or other anonymization).

**Operational response.** Persistent message-dropping against a peer is detected by the peer's repeated `MINORITY DIVERGENCE` halts and by missing-peer protocol triggers. Cluster operators investigate and either restore connectivity, switch to alternate transport (Tor, satellite, etc.), or accept the loss of the affected peer. The substrate continues operating without the affected peer, with the cluster's effective n decreased by one.

### 20.8 Partition healing exploits

**Attack.** A cluster has been partitioned: some nodes are in partition A, others in partition B, neither sub-partition has supermajority, both halt with `CLUSTER SPLIT`. The partition heals — connectivity is restored. An adversary attempts to exploit the healing to inject inputs that would not have been valid had the partition not occurred, or to cause the cluster to commit conflicting state from the two partitions.

**Defense.** The substrate's correct behavior on partition is to halt with `CLUSTER SPLIT` and resume from the last committed frame when the partition heals. Both partitions, while halted, were unable to commit further frames; there is no "partition A's committed frame" versus "partition B's committed frame" to reconcile. The healing is straightforward: reconnect transports, re-enter normal lockstep at the last committed frame, process pending inputs through the normal canonical-hash-sort and supermajority-commit pipeline.

The substrate's design specifically rejects "make progress in either partition during the partition" because that would require reconciling potentially-conflicting commits when the partition heals. The conservative choice is correct: lose availability during the partition, retain consistency.

**Residual risk.** Two residual risks. First, an adversary who fabricates a partition-and-heal scenario (deliberately disconnecting nodes, then reconnecting them) can use the disconnection window to submit inputs that would have been rejected in normal operation — but the substrate's verification on healing detects this because the inputs' validity depends on the cluster's state, which the partition did not advance. Second, the partition-and-heal protocol depends on the substrate's persistence layer (§16) being robust enough that nodes can resume from their last committed frame; persistence corruption during the partition window is a §23 (state attacks) concern.

**Operational response.** Partition healing is normal cluster operation; no special protocol is required beyond reconnection and resumption of lockstep. Operators monitor for unusually frequent partition-and-heal cycles, which may indicate an adversarial actor or an unstable network configuration that needs investigation.

### 20.9 Lockstep protocol invariants

The substrate's lockstep protocol is correct iff a small set of structural invariants hold. This subsection specifies the invariants. A conforming implementation must maintain all of them; an implementation that violates any is non-conforming and is detected via the conformance suite or via cluster-level dissent log accumulation.

**Invariant one: deterministic transition.** The state-transition function is a pure function of (current state, ordered input set). No I/O, no system calls returning time-varying values, no thread-local state, no compiler-dependent floating-point operations. Same inputs in same order on same starting state produce byte-identical resulting state.

**Invariant two: canonical input ordering.** Every honest node processes the same union of inputs in the same order, determined by canonical hash sort.

**Invariant three: monotonic frame progression.** Frame numbers advance by exactly one per committed frame. There is no skipping forward, no resetting backward, no parallel frame numbers.

**Invariant four: supermajority commit binding.** A frame is committed iff at least ⌊2n/3⌋+1 nodes report identical state hashes. No frame is committed without supermajority; no frame is committed with conflicting supermajority.

**Invariant five: cryptographic verification before processing.** Every input is verified cryptographically (signature against canonical preimage) before being included in the deterministic transition. Inputs that fail verification are silently dropped, deterministically.

**Invariant six: phase buffering.** Receive-side message buffering accommodates one frame of bidirectional asynchrony between any two nodes. Messages from "the future" (next-frame messages while still processing current-frame) are buffered; messages from "the past" (already-committed-frame messages) are discarded.

**Invariant seven: dissent log determinism.** Every honest node observing the same supermajority tally writes the same dissent log entry. Dissent attribution is identical across honest nodes for the same observed event.

These seven invariants are sufficient for lockstep correctness. An implementation that maintains all seven is correct regardless of which specific data structures, programming language, or transport layer it uses. The conformance specification operationalizes the invariants through test vectors that exercise each.

The next section (§21) walks cluster topology attacks. Where §20 engaged threats to the lockstep protocol within a single cluster, §21 engages threats to the cluster topology itself — Sybil attacks against admission, cluster-splitting at adversarial boundaries, cross-cluster transfer races, cluster orphaning, and cluster-level Byzantine collusion. The composition is that a §20 attack must succeed within a single cluster to corrupt that cluster's state; a §21 attack succeeds at the cluster-membership or cross-cluster layer to corrupt the federation's overall integrity.
## §21. Cluster topology attacks

The substrate's cluster topology (§11) is the substrate's scaling mechanism. Many clusters of bounded size, each performing lockstep consensus over its own bytes, with cross-cluster transfer protocols moving bytes between clusters as ownership crosses cluster boundaries. The topology is the architectural decomposition that lets the substrate scale across federation rather than through consensus growth, and it is the architectural layer where several attack classes operate.

This section walks five attack classes against the four-element rubric (§18.3). The attacks are §21.1 admission Sybil attacks, §21.2 cluster splitting at adversarial boundaries, §21.3 cross-cluster transfer race conditions, §21.4 cluster orphaning by selective gossip, §21.5 cluster-level Byzantine collusion. §21.6 closes by acknowledging that several defenses in this category are v0.4 specification work and naming the substrate's commitment to engaging them honestly in the meantime.

By the close of §21, the reader should accept that the substrate's cluster topology is robust against the realistic attack surface a sophisticated adversary at the topology layer can present, that the v0.4 gaps are honestly named rather than hidden, and that the substrate's federated structure is defensible against attacks that target its decomposition rather than its individual clusters.

### 21.1 Admission Sybil attacks

**Attack.** An adversary attempts to acquire enough cluster member slots to exceed the BFT supermajority bound (§13). With ⌊n/3⌋+1 nodes under the adversary's control, the adversary can prevent commits (denial-of-service); with ⌊2n/3⌋+1 nodes, the adversary can commit forged state. The attack succeeds if the substrate's admission gates can be cleared by the adversary at lower cost than the cluster's economic value warrants.

The attack has two profiles. Profile one: the adversary pretends to be many distinct legitimate operators, each clearing the admission gates independently. Profile two: the adversary corrupts existing legitimate operators (bribery, coercion, infiltration) and brings their nodes under adversarial control without the operators' awareness.

**Defense.** The substrate's admission protocol (§11.2) imposes three gates: cryptographic identity, proof-of-resource shadow period (default 30 days), and existing-member sponsorship (at least two existing cluster members vouching for the candidate). Each gate raises the adversary's cost.

The cryptographic-identity gate produces a permanent, content-addressed admission record bound to a specific ed25519 public key. There is no admission recovery for a lost private key; an adversary attempting to spawn many Sybils must generate, manage, and protect a large number of distinct private keys, each of which becomes a separate operational liability.

The shadow-period gate forces the adversary to run production-equivalent infrastructure for 30 days before the Sybil's vote counts. For a cluster of value V, an adversary attempting to acquire ⌊n/3⌋+1 Sybil slots must run that many shadow nodes for 30 days each, with the candidate's hashes monitored against the cluster's supermajority hash on every frame. Infrastructure cost is approximately ⌊n/3⌋+1 × $1000-3000/month per node (conservative cloud-hosting estimate); adversary scaling becomes economically painful at cluster sizes large enough to be valuable.

The sponsorship gate requires the adversary to either (a) recruit two existing legitimate members per Sybil attempt, paying or coercing them at the per-sponsorship cost; or (b) bring the Sybil's sponsors under adversarial control, doubling the corruption requirement. Sponsorship has reputational stakes for the sponsor: a sponsor whose sponsored candidate later misbehaves accumulates dissent-log weight, risking their own admission status. Sponsors who repeatedly vouch for adversarial candidates are themselves identifiable and excludable.

**Residual risk.** Three residual risks. First, a sufficiently-resourced adversary (state-level, large enterprise) can clear the admission gates by sustained operational effort. The defenses raise costs; they do not eliminate the threat. The substrate's architectural assumption (§18.2) is that the cost is high enough that the adversary's value capture is bounded. Second, the v0.3 implementation does not yet enforce the full admission protocol — it admits any peer that can connect, with operator vetting outside the protocol. The v1.0 substrate must implement the gates before production deployment is responsible. Third, the sponsorship gate is socially-contestable: an adversary can bribe sponsors, infiltrate sponsor pools, or run social-engineering campaigns to recruit unwitting sponsors.

**Operational response.** The cluster's dissent log accumulates evidence on candidates whose post-admission behavior diverges from the supermajority. Persistent misbehavers are expelled (planned v0.4) and their sponsors' reputations decay. The cluster's operational tooling monitors admission patterns for anomalies — unusually rapid admission rates, geographic clustering of new admissions, reused infrastructure across nominally-distinct candidates. Operators investigate flagged patterns and can pause admissions if a Sybil campaign is detected.

### 21.2 Cluster splitting at adversarial boundaries

**Attack.** A cluster reaches the splitting threshold (§11.3.2). The adversary attempts to manipulate the split such that all the cluster's adversarial nodes end up in one of the two resulting clusters, while the other resulting cluster has only honest nodes. If successful, the adversary gains a cluster where they hold the BFT supermajority and can corrupt state, while the honest cluster operates normally but loses oversight of the bytes that partitioned to the adversarial cluster.

The attack has two variants. Variant one: the adversary biases the cluster-splitting protocol's randomness beacon (§11.3.2 step 1), causing the byte and member partition functions to produce favorable outcomes. Variant two: the adversary times the split to coincide with a moment when honest members are unavailable (network outage, scheduled maintenance), shifting the post-split membership balance.

**Defense.** The substrate's cluster-splitting protocol (specification work for v0.4) is designed against both variants. The randomness beacon is constructed from contributions of *all* cluster members at the SplitFrame, with each member committing their contribution before any contribution is revealed (commit-reveal protocol). An adversary controlling fewer than the BFT supermajority cannot bias the beacon; an adversary at the BFT supermajority can corrupt many things, and the split's randomness is just one of them.

The member-assignment function (§11.3.2 step 3) is constrained to produce two new clusters each meeting the n≥4 BFT threshold and having approximately n/2 members. The function's output depends on the randomness beacon and the deterministic ordering of the cluster's pre-split membership; an adversary cannot directly choose which members go where.

The split's timing is set by cluster-size threshold rather than by adversarial choice. A cluster reaches the splitting threshold when its membership exceeds the operator-tunable splitting size (default: 32 members); the split happens at the next available SplitFrame. An adversary can attempt to time their attacks against scheduled maintenance windows, but the cluster's normal operation continues during such windows (BFT supermajority handles ⌊(n-1)/3⌋ unavailable members), and the SplitFrame happens whenever the threshold is reached, not at adversary-chosen times.

**Residual risk.** Two residual risks. First, the cluster-splitting protocol is design-only in v0.3. The randomness beacon, the member-assignment function, the partition function — all are specified at the prose level but not yet implemented. v0.4 will surface implementation details that this prose cannot anticipate, including subtle attack vectors against the specific commit-reveal mechanism the implementation uses. Second, an adversary controlling a BFT supermajority of the cluster can corrupt the split's randomness arbitrarily, since the supermajority's contributions dominate the beacon. This is the same fundamental BFT bound that applies everywhere; cluster splitting is not a special case.

**Operational response.** The first time the substrate's cluster splitting is exercised in production, operators monitor the split outcomes for suspicious patterns. A split that produces unusually skewed membership distributions, or that partitions bytes in patterns favoring specific adversaries, is investigated and may be reversed (via cluster merging, also v0.4 work) if anomalies are detected. The cluster's post-split state and the SplitFrame's contents are archived for forensic analysis.

### 21.3 Cross-cluster transfer race conditions

**Attack.** A byte is in the process of cross-cluster transfer from cluster A to cluster B (§11.3.3). The adversary attempts to exploit race conditions in the transfer protocol — submitting a transfer of the same byte to cluster A while the export attestation is being signed, attempting to introduce the byte to a third cluster C before B's import completes, or causing cluster B to import a byte that cluster A has not yet retired from its own state.

The attack succeeds if the substrate's cross-cluster protocol allows the byte to exist in two clusters simultaneously, or to be transferred under an outdated state.

**Defense.** The substrate's cross-cluster transfer protocol uses a five-step procedure (§11.3.3): (1) ownership transfer commits in cluster A with cross-cluster flag; (2) cluster A produces export attestation signed by BFT supermajority; (3) cluster B verifies and imports; (4) cluster A retires the byte; (5) external references resolve to cluster B.

The protocol's correctness depends on three orderings. First, cluster A's lockstep commits the cross-cluster transition before it produces the export attestation; the byte is no longer transferable within cluster A by the moment the attestation is signed. Second, cluster B's import is a single committed frame in cluster B's lockstep, with the import succeeding or failing atomically. Third, cluster A's retirement of the byte happens after cluster B's import, with the gossip overlay propagating the new ownership to any external party who queries.

A byte cannot exist in two clusters simultaneously because cluster A's lockstep commits the cross-cluster transfer (which includes retiring the byte from cluster A) before any other cluster can process an import attestation referencing the same byte. Cluster B verifies cluster A's BFT-supermajority signature on the export attestation; the signature is produced after the cross-cluster commit, so the byte is already retired from cluster A by the time cluster B can import.

**Residual risk.** Three residual risks. First, the cross-cluster transfer protocol is design-only in v0.3. Implementation will surface subtle race conditions — particularly around partial failures (cluster A commits the transfer, cluster B's import fails, the byte is in limbo). Second, an adversary controlling the gossip overlay between clusters could potentially delay propagation of the cluster-A-retirement message, allowing the byte's previous owner (in cluster A) to attempt re-spending in cluster A's view of the world; the substrate's defense is that cluster A's own lockstep already retired the byte, so the re-spending attempt fails at cluster A's verification step regardless of what cluster B believes. Third, cross-cluster transfers introduce a dependency on the export-attestation signature scheme; if cluster A's BFT supermajority is compromised, cluster A can sign export attestations for bytes that don't exist or that have already been transferred. This is engaged in §21.5.

**Operational response.** Cross-cluster transfer failures are detected by either cluster's lockstep failing to commit the corresponding frame. Failed transfers are retryable: the source cluster's state is unchanged (the cross-cluster commit was rolled back), and the user can re-submit the transfer. Anomalies in cross-cluster patterns — clusters attempting to transfer the same byte through multiple paths, clusters whose transfer-failure rate is unusually high — are flagged by operational monitoring for investigation.

### 21.4 Cluster orphaning by selective gossip

**Attack.** A cluster's existence depends on the substrate's gossip overlay propagating its state attestations, admission records, and cross-cluster transfer announcements to peer clusters. An adversary controlling enough of the gossip overlay can selectively suppress a target cluster's gossip, isolating it from the broader substrate. The target cluster continues operating internally but is unreachable for cross-cluster transfers, unable to admit new members from outside its current peering, and effectively orphaned.

A more subtle variant: the adversary suppresses *some* of the target cluster's gossip — specific announcements, specific transfers — while letting other gossip pass. The target cluster is partially isolated, with external observers seeing an inconsistent picture.

**Defense.** The substrate's gossip overlay (§11.4) is intentionally simple and topologically heterogeneous. Every node maintains a small number of peer connections; gossip propagates through the overlay via deduplication-by-content-addressing. An adversary attempting to suppress a target cluster's gossip must control all of the target cluster's peer connections; the cluster's nodes can refresh their peer connections to bypass adversarial peers, and the gossip overlay's heterogeneity (every node chooses its own peers) makes it costly for any adversary to control the connections of a specific cluster.

For the partial-suppression variant, the substrate's content-addressed gossip provides a defense: any party who has received some of the cluster's gossip (via any path in the overlay) holds the content-addressed identifier and can request the rest from any other peer in the overlay. Selective suppression requires the adversary to control all paths through which a specific message could propagate, not just some of them; for a sufficiently dense overlay, this is operationally infeasible.

**Residual risk.** Three residual risks. First, the gossip overlay's specification is brief (§11.4) and operational. v0.4 work should specify peer-selection algorithms, gossip propagation rules, NAT-traversal mechanisms, and bootstrap protocols precisely; in the absence of detailed specification, implementations may diverge in ways that produce orphaning vulnerabilities. Second, network-layer state-level attacks (BGP hijacking, ISP-level routing manipulation) can suppress entire geographic regions of the gossip overlay; this is engaged in §27 (physical attacks). Third, an adversary deploying a large number of well-connected gossip nodes can dominate the overlay's peering selections and selectively withhold messages; the defenses are admission-style gates on gossip-overlay participation (planned v0.4) and operational monitoring for gossip-pattern anomalies.

**Operational response.** A cluster that detects orphaning (it cannot reach known peer clusters, its gossip is not propagating, its cross-cluster transfers are failing) refreshes its peer connections, attempts to bootstrap from known reliable peers, and may request operator intervention if the orphaning persists. Substrate-level operational tooling monitors gossip propagation rates and flags clusters whose visibility from external observers has dropped unexpectedly.

### 21.5 Cluster-level Byzantine collusion

**Attack.** An adversary controls the BFT supermajority of an entire cluster — through Sybil attacks (§21.1), through corrupting existing operators, through deploying the cluster as their own infrastructure from the start. The cluster's lockstep continues operating, but the supermajority's commits no longer reflect honest consensus; the cluster signs forged state, fabricated export attestations, and false admission records.

The attack profile is qualitatively different from §13 (BFT) attacks: BFT defends against *up to* ⌊(n-1)/3⌋ Byzantine nodes per frame. Cluster-level Byzantine collusion *exceeds* this bound by definition, making BFT supermajority commit a tool of the adversary rather than a defense against them.

**Defense.** The substrate's defense against cluster-level Byzantine collusion operates at the *federation* layer rather than the *cluster* layer. Three mechanisms compose.

**Mechanism one: content-addressed history independent verification.** A cluster B importing a byte from cluster A (§11.3.3) verifies the byte's full chain end-to-end (§10.2). A malicious cluster A that signs an export attestation for a byte with a forged history fails verification at cluster B's import step; the byte's chain doesn't verify against cluster A's claim, and the import is rejected. Cluster A's BFT supermajority is irrelevant to the chain verification — the chain's correctness derives from the byte's own cryptographic provenance, not from the cluster's testimony.

**Mechanism two: cross-cluster attestation diversity.** Bytes that are valuable enough to attract adversarial attention typically transit multiple clusters during their lifetime. Each cluster that has handled a byte produces attestations visible to the substrate's gossip overlay. A malicious cluster A claiming to have handled a byte that other clusters' historical records contradict is detectable by querying the gossip overlay for the byte's history of cluster touches. Adversarial clusters that fabricate handling history for bytes they never actually managed are surfaced by the discrepancy.

**Mechanism three: cluster reputation accumulation.** A cluster operating honestly over long periods accumulates positive reputation (clean dissent log, successful cross-cluster transfers, no anomalies in admission patterns). A cluster newly admitted to the federation, or a cluster with anomalies in its history, is treated with more caution by other clusters. A cluster determined to be malicious can be expelled from the federation by quorum of honest clusters (the expulsion protocol's design is v0.4 work and is taken up in §22 and §25).

**Residual risk.** Four residual risks. First, cluster-level Byzantine handling is design-only in v0.3. The federation-layer defenses are specified at the prose level but not yet implemented; v0.4 must implement them before substrate operates with multiple clusters in production. Second, content-addressed history verification (mechanism one) requires the verifier to obtain the byte's full chain; an adversary who controls the chain's distribution can attempt to provide a fake chain to verifiers, but blake3's collision resistance defeats this — the fake chain's hashes don't match the real chain's hashes, and the verification fails. Third, the cluster-expulsion protocol depends on a quorum of honest clusters being identifiable. If the federation is dominated by adversarial clusters (the substrate-level analog of cluster-internal Byzantine majority), no honest expulsion quorum exists. The substrate's strategic case (§28) is that the federation maintains adequate honest constituency over the timeline relevant to substrate adoption. Fourth, an adversarial cluster colluding with all the other clusters that have handled a particular byte (a coordinated lie across the byte's entire transit history) can produce a consistent fake history; the substrate's defense is that such coordination is operationally hard at scale and would itself produce anomalies in the gossip overlay.

**Operational response.** Cluster-level Byzantine collusion detected by federation-level mechanisms triggers cluster expulsion (planned v0.4). Bytes resident in the expelled cluster are treated as orphaned: their history remains valid but their current state is unverifiable, requiring intervention by an honest cluster willing to import them with extra verification (auditor-grade chain examination, cross-referenced with all surviving handling-history attestations from other clusters). Recovery is partial — bytes whose handling history was entirely within the expelled cluster, with no cross-cluster transfers, may be unrecoverable. The substrate's operational guidance is that high-value bytes should diversify across multiple cluster transits to minimize this risk.

### 21.6 The v0.4 specification gap

The substrate's cluster topology defenses are partially design-only in v0.3. This subsection names the gap honestly so that the threat model is not read as a claim of completeness when it is not.

Five defenses are design-only:

- **Cluster splitting (§11.3.2 / §21.2)**: the SplitFrame protocol, the randomness beacon, the member-assignment function, the partition function. v0.3 does not implement any of these. v0.4 priority high.
- **Cross-cluster transfer (§11.3.3 / §21.3)**: the export-and-import protocol with BFT-supermajority signatures and gossip propagation. v0.3 does not implement. v0.4 priority high.
- **Cluster-level Byzantine handling (§11.3.4 / §21.5)**: the federation-layer defenses against malicious clusters. v0.3 implements intra-cluster BFT only. v0.4 priority high.
- **Gossip overlay specification (§11.4 / §21.4)**: peer-selection algorithms, propagation rules, bootstrap protocols. v0.3 specifies the overlay at the prose level. v0.4 priority high.
- **Cluster expulsion protocol (§14.3 / §21.5)**: the federation-quorum mechanism for removing malicious clusters. v0.3 specifies dissent-log accumulation; expulsion based on it is v0.4 work.

The substrate ships v1.0 with these gaps explicitly named. v1.0 substrate operation is therefore appropriate for single-cluster or small-federation deployments where the v0.4 mechanisms are not yet load-bearing. Multi-cluster production deployment requires v0.4. The treatise commits to specifying and implementing all five v0.4 items before the substrate's adoption crosses thresholds where multi-cluster operation becomes necessary.

The honest acknowledgment of the gap is part of the threat model's discipline. A treatise that claimed v1.0 substrate is fully cluster-topology-secure would be misleading; the treatise's claim is that v1.0 substrate has well-defended single-cluster operation with cluster-topology defenses specified-but-not-yet-implemented for federation-scale deployment. The distinction matters for any operator considering substrate deployment at present-day adoption levels versus federated-substrate adoption levels.

### 21.7 Composition with other categories

The cluster topology attacks engaged in §21 compose with other categories' defenses in three ways.

**Composition one: cluster topology defenses depend on cryptographic primitives (§19) and lockstep protocol invariants (§20).** Admission-record signatures use ed25519. Cluster-state hashes use blake3. Cross-cluster export attestations use BFT-supermajority signatures over canonically-serialized state. If §19 or §20 defenses fail, §21 defenses fail with them.

**Composition two: cluster topology defenses are the substrate's federation-layer immune system.** §19 defends individual cryptographic primitives. §20 defends individual cluster lockstep. §21 defends the federation of clusters as a whole — admission gates that limit Sybil entry, cross-cluster verification that limits malicious-cluster influence, expulsion mechanisms that remove identified bad actors. The federation-layer defenses are what make the substrate's "federate, don't centralize" architectural commitment defensible against sophisticated adversaries.

**Composition three: cluster topology gaps in v0.3 are the substrate's most pressing v0.4 work.** §19 and §20 defenses are largely implemented in the v0.3 reference. §21 defenses are largely design-only. Adopting the substrate beyond single-cluster, small-federation deployments depends on closing these gaps. The treatise's discipline is to name the gaps; the substrate's authors' discipline is to close them.

The next section (§22) walks issuer attacks. Where §21 engaged threats to the cluster topology — the federation layer — §22 engages threats to the issuer registry: compromised issuer keys, social engineering of issuer governance, regulatory capture, issuer collusion, and run-on-the-bank dynamics. The composition is that §21 defenses prevent malicious clusters; §22 defenses prevent malicious or captured issuers within otherwise-honest clusters.
## §22. Issuer attacks

The substrate's issuer registry (§15.1) is the architectural layer where authorized entities mint cargo-bearing bytes. A USD-byte issuer is a stablecoin provider; a joule-byte issuer is a registered solar cooperative; a kcal-byte issuer is a food cooperative or agricultural producer. The issuer registry is publicly visible, governance-mutable, and content-addressed; every byte's origin attestation is signed by an issuer whose authorization is verifiable by any party.

This section walks five attack classes against the four-element rubric (§18.3). The attacks are §22.1 compromised issuer key, §22.2 social engineering of issuer governance, §22.3 regulatory capture of issuer set, §22.4 issuer collusion, §22.5 run-on-the-bank dynamics. §22.6 closes by acknowledging that issuer-layer threats are where the institutional middle re-enters the substrate's stack as a layer-2 service, and where adversaries who cannot attack the substrate's protocol directly may attack through the issuers it depends on.

By the close of §22, the reader should understand that issuer attacks are categorically different from protocol attacks — they target the trusted entities the substrate explicitly retains for cargo-bearing bytes, and the substrate's defenses operate by limiting issuer power and ensuring issuer plurality, rather than by eliminating issuer trust entirely.

### 22.1 Compromised issuer key

**Attack.** An adversary obtains an issuer's ed25519 private key — through theft, malware, supply-chain attack, insider threat, or coercion.
