With the key, the adversary mints bytes claiming to be issued by the legitimate issuer: USD-bytes that aren't backed by dollar reserves, joule-bytes that don't correspond to actual energy production, kcal-bytes that no actual food was grown for. The forged bytes flow into circulation, get transferred to legitimate counterparties, and accumulate cryptographic provenance that masks their fraudulent origin.

The attack is particularly damaging because the substrate's chain verification (§10.2) does not detect forged-but-cryptographically-valid mints. The forgery is at the issuer's authorization layer, not at the substrate's cryptographic layer; the chain verifies, the signatures verify, the issuer registry confirms the issuer pubkey is authorized — but the *underlying claim* (that backing reserves exist for the USD-bytes, that energy was actually produced for the joule-bytes) is false.

**Defense.** The substrate's defense against compromised issuer keys is *containment plus rotation*. The substrate cannot prevent issuer-key compromise any more than it can prevent user-key compromise (§19.4); it can only limit the damage and provide a path to recovery.

Three layers of containment apply. First, **issuer key rotation** (planned v0.4): an issuer detecting compromise can rotate to a new keypair via a governance-mutable update to the registry. The rotation is a signed attestation from the *old* key authorizing the *new* key, with a timestamped frame number that scopes the compromise window. Bytes minted under the old key after the rotation timestamp are detectable as suspicious. Bytes minted before the rotation but after the actual compromise are harder — the substrate cannot retroactively determine when the compromise actually occurred — but the issuer's own off-substrate records and reserve attestations help establish the boundary.

Second, **issuer reserve attestations** (v0.4 specification work, may be implemented as layer-2): the substrate's protocol does not currently require issuers to attest periodically to their reserves, but the issuer registry's content-addressed structure makes such attestations natural. An issuer that publishes monthly reserve attestations (signed with the issuer's key, content-addressed in the substrate's gossip overlay) creates a cumulative record. A forged-mint attack inflates the issuer's apparent issuance beyond the reserve attestations; the discrepancy is detectable by anyone auditing the public attestations.

Third, **layer-2 issuer remediation services**: external auditors, regulatory bodies, and dispute-resolution services can examine an issuer's claims against external evidence (banking records, energy-grid reports, agricultural production records). When forgery is identified, the affected bytes can be invalidated through issuer cooperation or layer-2 dispute resolution. This is not a substrate-level guarantee but an operational reality: real issuers operate within real legal and regulatory frameworks that provide accountability the substrate's protocol does not.

**Residual risk.** Three residual risks. First, the v0.4 issuer-key-rotation protocol is unimplemented; v0.3 fixes the issuer set at genesis with no rotation mechanism. An issuer-key compromise in v0.3 has no protocol-level recovery and requires a substrate-wide governance event to update the registry. Second, the time between actual compromise and detection can be substantial; an adversary with a stolen issuer key can mint a large volume of forged bytes before the issuer notices, with the bytes acquiring cryptographic provenance that makes them indistinguishable from legitimate bytes at the chain-verification layer. Third, layer-2 remediation depends on external accountability mechanisms that may themselves be captured or compromised; if regulators are corrupt or auditors are bribed, the remediation fails.

**Operational response.** An issuer detecting compromise rotates keys (post-v0.4) and publishes a frame-stamped advisory through the gossip overlay. Counterparties who hold bytes minted under the compromised key during the suspect window can examine the issuer's reserve attestations to assess legitimacy, request issuer-level remediation if available, or pursue layer-2 dispute resolution. Bytes whose legitimacy cannot be established are treated as having impaired value at the application layer, with the chain remaining verifiable but the underlying claim discounted by counterparties. The substrate's role is to make the cryptographic provenance auditable; remedy depends on layer-2 mechanisms.

### 22.2 Social engineering of issuer governance

**Attack.** An adversary attacks the issuer-set governance flow (planned v0.4) through social engineering rather than through cryptographic or protocol-level means. The adversary submits proposals to add their own front company as an issuer, recruits enough existing issuers to vote for the proposal (through bribery, influence, or genuine persuasion), and gains issuance authority without actually backing the issued cargo with real reserves or production.

A more subtle variant: the adversary attacks individual existing issuers' decision-making rather than seeking new admission — convincing a USD-byte issuer to relax their reserve requirements, or convincing a joule-byte issuer to issue bytes against future production rather than already-delivered production. The issuer remains nominally the same; the cargo's quality silently degrades.

**Defense.** The substrate's defenses against governance social engineering operate at the *transparency* and *plurality* layers rather than at the technical layer.

The issuer registry is publicly visible (§15.1). Every issuer's pubkey is in the registry, every governance event that changes the registry is committed to the cluster's lockstep, every change produces a content-addressed record visible to any party. An adversary attempting to add themselves as an issuer cannot do so privately; the addition is auditable.

Issuer plurality is structural. The substrate is multi-issuer per cargo type; users can choose which issuer's bytes to accept and transact with. An issuer whose governance has been corrupted (relaxed reserves, accepted forged production claims) loses user trust as the corruption becomes visible — through reserve attestation discrepancies, through external auditor reports, through user-side risk assessment. The corrupted issuer's bytes trade at a discount at the application layer; eventually the issuer loses market share to honest competitors. The substrate doesn't prevent the corruption; it makes the corruption visible and provides users with alternatives.

The governance flow itself (v0.4 design) requires supermajority approval from existing issuers of the affected cargo type — not from a single administrator. An adversary seeking to add themselves as an issuer must convince a supermajority of existing issuers, each of whom has their own reputational stake in the cargo type's integrity. Supermajority capture is harder than single-administrator capture, though not impossible at sufficient resources.

**Residual risk.** Three residual risks. First, the issuer-governance flow is design-only; v0.4 specification will surface the actual mechanics, including exact supermajority thresholds and challenge periods. Second, governance attacks operate on long timescales (weeks to months for a proposal cycle) where adversaries can exert sustained pressure on individual existing issuers; the substrate has no protocol-level defense against patient corruption, only the visibility-and-plurality structural defense. Third, the "users choose which issuer's bytes to accept" defense depends on users having useful information about issuer quality; users without access to reserve attestations, audit reports, or comparative analytics are operating with information asymmetry that adversaries can exploit.

**Operational response.** Anomalous governance proposals (unusually rapid admission requests, requests from front companies with no production history, requests targeting cargo types with high concentration risk) are flagged by issuer-governance monitoring. The substrate's gossip overlay propagates governance events to all clusters and external observers; anomalies are identifiable and can drive community response. Issuer-quality reporting services (layer-2) consolidate reserve attestations, audit findings, and operational track records into comparative information that users can consult. The substrate provides the infrastructure for transparency; layer-2 services and user vigilance provide the actual quality assessment.

### 22.3 Regulatory capture of issuer set

**Attack.** A state or coalition of states uses regulatory authority to constrain the substrate's issuer set. Regulators can impose capital requirements that only large institutions can meet, demand reporting and disclosure that only regulated entities can provide, license-gate the right to issue cargo of certain types, and outright prohibit issuance of cargo types they wish to control (digital currencies competing with state-issued ones, energy markets the state wishes to monopolize, food production outside state-approved channels).

The attack succeeds if it reduces the substrate's issuer plurality to one or zero per cargo type — recreating the institutional middle's monopoly structure within the substrate's nominal multi-issuer architecture.

**Defense.** The substrate's structural defenses against regulatory capture are partial. The substrate cannot prevent states from regulating; it can only ensure that regulatory pressure on one jurisdiction's issuers does not eliminate issuance globally.

**Defense one: jurisdictional plurality.** The substrate's issuer set spans multiple jurisdictions. A USD-byte issuer in the United States is regulated by U.S. authorities; a USD-byte issuer in Switzerland is regulated by Swiss authorities; a USD-byte issuer in Singapore is regulated by Singapore. Regulatory pressure in any one jurisdiction does not eliminate issuance globally; users in regulated jurisdictions can transact with issuers in less-regulated jurisdictions, with the cross-border friction being legal and operational rather than substrate-level.

**Defense two: the substrate's cargo-class neutrality.** The substrate is cargo-class-agnostic. A regulator banning USD-byte issuance does not affect joule-byte, kcal-byte, or compute-hour-byte issuance. Users whose regulatory environment makes specific cargo types unavailable can shift to functionally-similar cargo types (joule-bytes denominated in energy units rather than dollar-equivalents; community-currency-bytes for local exchange).

**Defense three: the substrate's protocol-level absence of issuer-friendly features.** The substrate has no built-in mechanism for issuer-level lockup of bytes, freeze of user accounts, mandatory KYC integration, or any of the regulatory-compliance features that traditional banking infrastructure embeds. An issuer wanting to add such features can do so at the application layer (a custodial wallet that voluntarily implements KYC and freeze), but the substrate's protocol doesn't make such features universal. Regulators demanding universal compliance must do so through the application layer or through the issuer's voluntary cooperation; they cannot mandate it at the substrate's protocol layer.

**Defense four: layer-2 anonymizing intermediaries.** Privacy-focused layer-2 services (mixing services, zero-knowledge wrappers, confidential transactions) can provide users in regulated jurisdictions with access to less-regulated issuers' bytes without exposing the cross-border flow to regulatory observation. The substrate's protocol-level transparency (every transition visible to chain verification) does not prevent layer-2 obfuscation; users who want privacy can compose it on top of the substrate, at their own discretion.

**Residual risk.** Three residual risks, deeply interconnected. First, jurisdictional plurality fails if regulators coordinate globally — international agreements like the FATF travel rule already constrain stablecoin operations across jurisdictions, and a coordinated multi-state ban on substrate participation could substantially shrink the available issuer set. Second, layer-2 privacy services are themselves regulated (Tornado Cash sanctions, the EU MiCA framework's restrictions on privacy coins); using them may itself become illegal in regulated jurisdictions, putting users at legal risk regardless of the substrate's protocol-level neutrality. Third, the substrate's strategic case (§28) rests on the substrate acquiring independent constituency before global regulatory coordination matures; if regulatory coordination wins the race, the substrate's freedom claims are constrained.

**Operational response.** Issuers facing regulatory capture in their home jurisdictions can either comply (becoming a more regulated, more compliant issuer with reduced freedom) or relocate (moving operations to more permissive jurisdictions). The substrate's federation supports both responses without protocol-level changes; the issuer's clusters continue operating, the issuer's bytes remain valid, the issuer's regulatory profile changes. Users in regulatory environments where their preferred issuers have become unavailable can shift to alternatives or to layer-2 anonymizing intermediaries, with the legal risks named above.

### 22.4 Issuer collusion

**Attack.** Multiple issuers of a cargo type collude to manipulate market conditions, coordinate price fixing, or jointly attack a competing issuer. The substrate's multi-issuer architecture is undermined by coordination among the supposedly-competing issuers.

Examples: USD-byte issuers colluding to charge identical fees, eliminating price competition. Joule-byte issuers in a region coordinating to suppress production reporting, inflating the apparent value of issued bytes. A coalition of established issuers refusing to support governance admission of new issuers, freezing the issuer set at advantageous incumbent levels.

**Defense.** The substrate's defenses against issuer collusion are *transparency-based* and *exit-based* rather than coordination-prevention-based.

**Transparency defense.** Every issuer's actions are visible. Mint volumes, reserve attestations (when published), governance votes (in v0.4), and pricing structures are all observable. Collusion that produces visible patterns — identical fees across nominally-competing issuers, synchronized issuance pauses, simultaneous governance votes — is detectable by external observers, antitrust regulators, and competing actors. The substrate makes collusion visible; it does not prevent collusion from occurring.

**Exit defense.** Users dissatisfied with the cargo type's issuer landscape can exit. They can hold less of the cargo type, switch to functionally-similar cargo types under different issuers, or compose layer-2 alternatives that bypass colluding issuers entirely. Exit options constrain issuer collusion economically: collusion that is too aggressive drives users to alternatives, eroding the colluding issuers' shared advantage.

**Substrate-level absence of issuer-friendly features.** Just as with regulatory capture, the substrate's protocol-level neutrality limits the leverage colluding issuers have. They cannot lock users into their cargo type; they cannot prevent users from transacting with bytes from competing issuers; they cannot impose substrate-wide rules that benefit only colluders.

**Residual risk.** Three residual risks. First, transparency requires observers — auditors, journalists, antitrust regulators with substrate-monitoring capability. The substrate makes information available; users still have to attend to it. Second, exit defense depends on alternatives existing; in cargo types where regulatory or operational barriers limit issuer entry, exit options may be inadequate. Third, the substrate's neutrality cuts both ways: it does not stop colluding issuers from coordinating off-substrate (private agreements, market-making coordination, joint lobbying for regulatory protection). The substrate's defenses are protocol-internal; off-substrate coordination is a layer-2 and policy-layer concern.

**Operational response.** Antitrust enforcement against colluding issuers operates through normal regulatory channels (FTC, DOJ Antitrust Division, EU Competition authority, equivalent bodies globally). The substrate's transparency provides evidence; regulators provide enforcement. User-level response is exit and switching, supported by layer-2 services that aggregate issuer-quality information and facilitate cargo-type transitions.

### 22.5 Run-on-the-bank dynamics

**Attack.** A USD-byte issuer's reserve adequacy comes into question — through a reserve attestation that fails to verify, an external audit that finds discrepancies, a regulatory action that signals trouble, or a market-wide flight from stablecoins. Users rush to redeem their USD-bytes for actual dollars before the issuer's reserves are exhausted. The redemption demand exceeds the issuer's liquid reserves; the issuer suspends redemptions; the byte's market price collapses; users holding the byte at the moment of suspension face significant loss.

The dynamic is not adversarial in the traditional sense — there is no single attacker — but it is a structural failure mode that an adversary can trigger, accelerate, or exploit. An adversary holding a short position on a specific issuer's bytes can publicize information designed to trigger a run; an adversary controlling competitor issuers can coordinate FUD campaigns; an adversary with regulatory leverage can time enforcement actions to maximize disruption.

**Defense.** The substrate's defenses against run dynamics are *informational* and *plural* rather than mechanical.

**Informational defense.** Issuer reserve attestations (v0.4 specification work) provide ongoing evidence of reserve adequacy. An issuer publishing daily reserve snapshots makes its reserves auditable continuously, not just at scheduled audit dates. Users can assess reserve adequacy in real time; runs become information-driven rather than panic-driven, and an issuer with verifiably-strong reserves can resist runs that would topple a weakly-attested issuer.

The substrate does not currently mandate reserve attestations, but the issuer registry's content-addressed structure makes them natural to add at the layer-2 level. Issuer-quality reporting services consolidate attestations and audit findings into comparative information that users can act on before runs become critical.

**Plurality defense.** Run dynamics affect specific issuers, not the cargo type as a whole. Users running from a failing USD-byte issuer can redirect their redemption demand to other USD-byte issuers, as long as the alternatives are not also stressed. The substrate's multi-issuer architecture limits contagion: a single issuer's failure does not necessarily cascade to other issuers of the same cargo type, particularly if the failing issuer's distress is idiosyncratic (specific reserve issue, specific regulatory action) rather than systemic (general loss of confidence in the cargo type).

**Cargo-type-level diversification.** Users concerned about run risk in any single cargo type can hold multiple cargo types, balancing across issuers, jurisdictions, and asset classes. The substrate's cargo-neutral architecture supports this naturally; users build their own portfolios across the substrate's available issuance.

**Residual risk.** Three residual risks. First, run dynamics can be triggered by genuine issuer failures (insolvency, reserve theft, operational collapse) that the substrate cannot prevent. Some issuers will fail; users holding those issuers' bytes will lose value. The substrate's claim is that the *substrate* doesn't fail — chain verification continues, the cluster keeps committing frames, other issuers keep operating — but specific issuers' failures are not protected against. Second, systemic crises (general loss of confidence in stablecoins, broader financial-system stress) can affect multiple issuers simultaneously, making the plurality defense weaker. The substrate has no protocol-level response to systemic stress; it inherits the broader financial system's vulnerabilities to the extent that its cargo types depend on the broader financial system. Third, the substrate's transparency (real-time visibility of issuance volumes, on-chain verification of transitions) can itself accelerate runs — making bad news visible faster than traditional banking's information opacity does. This is a structural cost of the substrate's transparency and one the treatise should be honest about.

**Operational response.** Issuer-level response to runs is the issuer's responsibility — managing liquidity, communicating reserve adequacy, coordinating with regulators and market makers. The substrate's role is to maintain protocol-level operation regardless of any specific issuer's health. Users facing run risk on bytes they hold can attempt redemption while it remains possible, transfer to other issuers' bytes, or convert to other cargo types. Users acting before the run becomes critical have better outcomes than users acting at the run's peak; the substrate's transparency at least makes the timing visible.

### 22.6 The institutional middle re-enters via issuance

A meta-observation worth naming explicitly. Issuer attacks are categorically different from substrate-protocol attacks. The substrate is honest in §6.4 and §15 that it retains issuer trust as a *structural feature* — cargo-bearing bytes (USD, joule, kcal, compute-hour) require external attestation of the cargo's existence. The substrate provides cryptographic provenance for the *byte* but cannot provide it for the *underlying claim* the byte represents. The substrate eliminates the institutional middle's role in matching, clearing, and trust signaling for transfers, but does not eliminate the institutional middle's role in *vouching for the cargo* at the issuance moment.

This is the substrate's deepest structural compromise. The institutional middle re-enters the substrate's stack at the issuer layer, smaller and more constrained than its pre-substrate role, but real. A USD-byte issuer is a stablecoin provider — typically a regulated financial institution with banking relationships, reserve management, audit obligations. A joule-byte issuer is a registered solar cooperative with grid-interconnection attestation. A kcal-byte issuer is an agricultural cooperative with production records. Each is a smaller, more transparent institution than the institutional middle's pre-substrate dominance, but each is still an institution with the operational and trust requirements that institutions impose.

The substrate's freedom claim is *partial freedom* rather than *complete freedom*. §6.1 named four freedoms; §22 makes explicit that the freedoms are bounded by issuer trust. Where issuers are diverse, transparent, and competitive, the substrate's freedom claim is robust. Where issuers are captured, colluding, or single-points-of-failure, the substrate's freedom claim weakens at exactly that cargo type.

The substrate's ongoing work — both in protocol design and in community-building — is to maintain the conditions under which issuer plurality is sustainable. Adversaries who cannot attack the substrate's protocol may attack through the issuer layer, and the substrate's defenses at that layer are structural (transparency, plurality, exit) rather than cryptographic. This is the most important honest disclosure §22 makes.

### 22.7 Composition with other categories

Issuer attacks compose with other categories' defenses in three ways.

**Composition one: cryptographic primitives (§19) and protocol invariants (§20) are *necessary but not sufficient* for issuer-layer security.** A perfectly-implemented substrate protocol with a compromised issuer key produces forged-but-cryptographically-valid bytes. Issuer-layer defenses (reserve attestations, key rotation, governance plurality) operate above the protocol layer.

**Composition two: cluster topology defenses (§21) protect issuer-registry integrity at the cluster level.** A malicious cluster (§21.5) could refuse to honor a legitimate issuer's mints, or could falsely claim authorization for an unauthorized issuer; cluster-topology defenses (federation-layer verification, cluster expulsion) prevent cluster-level corruption from compromising the issuer registry.

**Composition three: issuer attacks are the bridge between protocol-level and economic/social attacks (§24-§25).** Many issuer attacks — collusion, run dynamics, regulatory capture — operate through economic and social mechanisms rather than through protocol exploitation. §22's treatment is the protocol-adjacent face of issues that §24 (economic) and §25 (social) develop further. Readers concerned about the substrate's institutional-middle exposure should read §22, §24, and §25 together.

The next section (§23) walks state attacks — corruption of the substrate's persisted state through memory error, adversarial replay, state-sync poisoning, and checkpoint tampering. Where §22 engaged threats to the issuer layer that the substrate retains, §23 engages threats to the substrate's own state-management infrastructure: the persistence layer (§16), the recovery procedures, and the data integrity guarantees the substrate's clusters depend on for continuous operation.
## §23. State attacks

The substrate's state is the live, in-memory representation of the cluster's bytes, issuer registry, and frame number, persisted to disk via the checkpoint+log mechanism (§16). State integrity is foundational: every transition the substrate validates depends on the current state being correct, every BFT supermajority commit binds the post-frame state hash, every recovery procedure relies on the persisted state being authentic. An attack that corrupts state — silently or detectably, on disk or in memory, during normal operation or during recovery — threatens the substrate's continuous-operation claim.

This section walks four attack classes against the four-element rubric (§18.3). The attacks are §23.1 state corruption via memory error, §23.2 state fork via adversarial replay, §23.3 state-sync poisoning, §23.4 checkpoint tampering. §23.5 closes by specifying the substrate's state-integrity invariants — the structural properties that, if maintained, are sufficient for state correctness across normal operation and recovery scenarios.

By the close of §23, the reader should accept that the substrate's state-management layer is robust against the realistic attack surface that targets stored and in-flight state, that the persistence layer's intentional simplicity (§16's "intentionally unclever" framing) is itself a defense, and that recovery procedures are auditable rather than depending on operator trust.

### 23.1 State corruption via memory error

**Attack.** A node's in-memory state corrupts during normal operation. The corruption may be benign (single-bit flip from cosmic ray, ECC-uncorrected hardware error) or adversarial (memory-tampering rootkit, hypervisor-level attack on virtualized substrate nodes, deliberate fault injection on physical hardware). The corrupted state diverges from the cluster supermajority on the next frame's commit. If the corruption is silent and the node fails to detect it, the node continues operating with corrupted state, producing divergent state hashes and accumulating dissent log entries from peers.

The attack profile is: the node itself is honest in intent, but its memory has corrupted; or the node's hardware has been compromised, but the node's software is unaware.

**Defense.** The substrate's primary defense against memory-error state corruption is *the lockstep protocol itself*. Every committed frame produces a state hash (§9.4 step 6). Honest peers compare their hashes; the supermajority commit (§13) detects divergence within milliseconds of the corruption affecting the state-transition output. The corrupted node halts with `MINORITY DIVERGENCE` (§13.3 outcome three) or, if the corruption is widespread, the cluster halts with `CLUSTER SPLIT`. The supermajority's hash agreement is the substrate's continuous-validity check on every node's state; corruption that affects state-relevant memory is detected on the next frame.

Secondary defenses operate at the hardware and OS layers. ECC RAM corrects most single-bit errors before they reach software. Server-class CPUs include MCE (Machine Check Exception) handlers that detect uncorrectable errors and halt the affected process. The substrate's reference implementation does not require ECC, but production deployments are advised to use it. Operating-system-level kernel-page-table-isolation, ASLR, and other hardening measures reduce the attack surface for memory-tampering rootkits.

For deliberate fault injection on physical hardware (Rowhammer attacks, deliberate undervolting, cold-boot attacks on encryption keys), the substrate has no protocol-layer defense — these are hardware-level attacks that operating systems and hardware vendors must address. The substrate's protocol-layer claim is that *whatever* the cause of the memory error, *the corruption is detected* by the supermajority commit before it can be acted on.

**Residual risk.** Three residual risks. First, memory corruption that affects code paths rather than state data (a corrupted instruction in the verification routine, a corrupted pointer in the state-transition logic) can cause the node to compute the wrong state without producing a recognizable hash divergence — the wrong state's hash is also wrong, and the node's behavior becomes unpredictable rather than detectably divergent. This is a hardware-level concern; the substrate's protocol cannot defend against arbitrary code corruption. Second, a Byzantine majority of nodes simultaneously experiencing the same memory corruption (coordinated cosmic-ray storm, coordinated hardware attack across a cluster) would produce supermajority-corrupted state. The probability is operationally negligible for non-coordinated errors but is the structural failure mode for coordinated hardware attacks. Third, brief memory-error windows where corruption occurs and is corrected (silent corruption transiently affecting transition output, then resolving) may produce dissent log entries that operators investigate but cannot reproduce.

**Operational response.** A node halting with `MINORITY DIVERGENCE` triggers operator investigation. The node's hardware is examined (memory tests, ECC error logs, MCE history); if hardware error is found, the hardware is replaced and the node rejoins via the persistence-and-replay procedure (§16.2). If no hardware error is found, the node's process is restarted from the checkpoint and replay; if the corruption recurs, the node's software is examined for protocol-conformance bugs. Persistent unexplained divergence triggers cluster-level expulsion (planned v0.4) until the cause is identified.

### 23.2 State fork via adversarial replay

**Attack.** An adversary attempts to cause two parts of the cluster to commit different frames at the same frame number — a state fork. The classic Byzantine general's problem in concrete form: if the cluster's lockstep loses synchronization, with some nodes processing one set of inputs and other nodes processing another, the cluster may end up in two distinct committed states for the same frame number.

The attack profile: an adversary controlling a subset of cluster nodes (under the BFT bound) coordinates with adversarial network conditions to feed different inputs to different honest nodes for the same frame. The cluster's BFT supermajority commit is supposed to prevent this — both partitions of nodes would fail to reach supermajority — but the adversary attempts to engineer conditions where supermajority is reached *separately* in two partitions, producing two valid-looking state branches.

**Defense.** The substrate's defense against adversarial state forks is *the BFT supermajority bound combined with the input-broadcast invariant*.

The BFT supermajority bound (§13) requires ⌊2n/3⌋+1 nodes to commit a frame. For two distinct state branches to both achieve supermajority, the adversary would need to control more than ⌊(n-1)/3⌋ nodes — exceeding the BFT bound. Below the bound, the adversary cannot achieve supermajority for an alternate state branch; the honest supermajority's hash dominates the commit.

The input-broadcast invariant (§9.4 steps 2-3) ensures every honest node receives the union of all peer inputs for the frame. An adversary attempting to feed different inputs to different nodes would need to suppress the input-broadcast messages selectively — preventing some honest nodes from seeing some inputs while letting other honest nodes see them. This is the network-layer attack already engaged in §20.7 (message dropping); the defenses there apply: the affected honest nodes detect the divergence on the next supermajority commit and halt with `MINORITY DIVERGENCE`, preventing the fork from reaching commit.

The substrate's protocol therefore makes adversarial state forks structurally infeasible *below the BFT bound*. Above the bound, the adversary can corrupt state arbitrarily; this is the §21.5 cluster-level Byzantine collusion case, with federation-layer defenses applying.

**Residual risk.** Three residual risks. First, the input-broadcast invariant requires every honest node to send to every peer; an implementation that fails this invariant (a node that selectively skips peers in its input broadcast) creates the conditions for adversarial fork attacks. Conformance vectors include adversarial broadcast-skipping scenarios. Second, in clusters at exactly n=4 (the BFT-meaningful minimum), the bound tolerates f=1 Byzantine node and supermajority is 3; an adversary controlling 1 node plus adversarial network conditions affecting 1 honest node can produce 2-vs-2 hash splits that prevent commit (similar to the n=3 BFT degeneracy in §13.2 but at n=4). Cluster admission policies (§11.3.1) treat clusters at exactly n=4 as marginal and seek to grow them. Third, partition healing (§20.8) is where state-fork concerns are most acute; the substrate's correct behavior is to not commit during partition (preserving consistency over availability), but an implementation that commits during partition can produce true forks that must be reconciled.

**Operational response.** A cluster-level state fork attempt produces detectable evidence: dissent log entries on every honest node showing peers reporting divergent hashes for the same frame, supermajority commit failures, and the cluster halting with `CLUSTER SPLIT`. Operators examine the dissent log to identify the adversarial nodes and the network conditions that enabled the attack, restore connectivity, and either expel the identified adversaries or pause cluster operation until the conditions are remediated.

### 23.3 State-sync poisoning

**Attack.** A node recovering from extended unavailability (crash, network partition, planned maintenance) requests state-sync from peers to catch up to the cluster's current frame. An adversary poisons the state-sync response — providing a forged state, a state from a different cluster, or a state with adversarial modifications. If the recovering node accepts the poisoned state, it rejoins the cluster with corrupt state, producing dissent on every subsequent frame.

The attack is particularly relevant for nodes that have been unavailable long enough that their local checkpoint+log is too stale for replay. v0.3 does not implement state-sync — recovering nodes must be brought back via persistence-and-replay only. v0.4 adds state-sync from honest peers; §23.3 engages the attack class that v0.4 must defend against.

**Defense.** The substrate's defense against state-sync poisoning operates through *cryptographic anchoring* of received state to the cluster's known-good attestations.

The recovering node has access to the cluster's BFT-supermajority signed state-hash attestations from before its unavailability — either through the gossip overlay's published checkpoints, through its own pre-unavailability persistence, or through cluster-level beacons that publish state hashes periodically. The recovering node knows the *content-addressed hash* of the legitimate state at any frame within the attestation history.

When the node requests state-sync, it requests state at a specific frame. Multiple peers respond with their copies of the state at that frame. The recovering node verifies each response's content-addressed hash against the cluster's BFT-signed attestation for that frame; only state matching the attested hash is accepted. A peer providing a poisoned state produces a hash mismatch and is rejected; an honest peer providing the correct state matches the attestation and is accepted.

The verification is therefore *cryptographic*, not *trust-based*. The recovering node does not need to trust the peers individually; it trusts the cluster's BFT-supermajority signature on the historical attestation, which is verifiable against the cluster's known pubkeys. Multiple peers responding with the same content-addressed state hash that matches the attestation is conclusive evidence of correctness.

**Residual risk.** Three residual risks. First, the recovering node must obtain a trustworthy cluster-state attestation for some frame in the recent past. If the node has been completely offline (no pre-unavailability persistence, no access to gossip overlay during unavailability), bootstrap is harder — it must be admitted as a new node (§11.2 admission gates) rather than as a recovering existing node. Second, the cluster's BFT-supermajority signature on state attestations requires ⌊2n/3⌋+1 honest signatures; if cluster-level Byzantine collusion has occurred (§21.5), the historical attestations themselves may be forged, and state-sync verification produces false confidence. The federation-layer defenses (§21.5) apply. Third, v0.4 implementation will surface details about the state-sync protocol itself (chunking, retries, simultaneous-peer-request strategies) that this prose cannot anticipate; the protocol's correctness depends on the implementation getting these right.

**Operational response.** State-sync requests that fail verification are retried against different peers; persistent verification failures across multiple peers indicate either adversarial coordination (the recovering node should escalate to operator investigation) or stale attestations (the recovering node should obtain fresher attestations from the gossip overlay before retrying). State-sync that succeeds with a verified state is followed by normal lockstep rejoin; the recovering node enters lockstep at the synced frame and proceeds.

### 23.4 Checkpoint tampering

**Attack.** An adversary with disk access modifies a node's `checkpoint.bin` file. When the node starts up (or recovers from a crash) and loads the checkpoint, it uses the modified state as its starting point. The modifications might be subtle (a single byte's owner changed, an issuer's pubkey replaced) or wholesale (a fabricated state from a different cluster substituted entirely).

The attack profile is: the node's hardware and operating system are intact, but the persistence files have been tampered with by an attacker who gained file-system access — through stolen disk images, shared-storage access misconfigurations, or insider threats on the node's host system.

**Defense.** The substrate's defense against checkpoint tampering composes two mechanisms: *immediate detection via supermajority commit* and *cryptographic integrity verification before load* (planned v0.4).

The immediate-detection defense is structural and works in v0.3 today. A node loading a tampered checkpoint enters lockstep with corrupt state. On the next frame's supermajority commit, the node's state hash diverges from the supermajority. The node halts with `MINORITY DIVERGENCE`. The tampering is detected within milliseconds of the node attempting to participate in cluster operation. The node cannot commit corrupt state to the cluster because BFT supermajority requires honest peers' agreement, which the tampered state will not produce.

The cryptographic-integrity defense (v0.4 work) adds proactive verification before the corrupted state ever enters lockstep. The proposed mechanism: at checkpoint write time, the node signs the checkpoint with its own ed25519 key, producing a `checkpoint.sig` file alongside `checkpoint.bin`. At checkpoint load time, the node verifies the signature before deserializing. A tampered checkpoint fails signature verification and is rejected; the node either restores from a backup checkpoint or halts and requires operator intervention.

The cluster's BFT-signed state attestations from the gossip overlay (§21.4) provide an additional offline verification path. A node concerned about checkpoint integrity can compare its checkpoint's content-addressed hash against the cluster's published attestations; mismatches indicate tampering.

**Residual risk.** Three residual risks. First, v0.3 does not sign checkpoints; the immediate-detection defense via supermajority commit is the only defense, and it operates after the tampered state has been loaded. A node that loads tampered state and immediately participates in lockstep will be detected, but the tampered state is briefly in memory before detection. Second, persistence-files-on-shared-storage configurations (NFS-mounted persistence, shared-disk virtualization) are particularly vulnerable to file-system-level tampering and should be avoided in production. Third, encryption-at-rest is not currently part of the substrate's persistence specification (§16.5); production deployments using OS-level full-disk encryption have additional defenses but the substrate doesn't mandate them.

**Operational response.** Checkpoint tampering detected by `MINORITY DIVERGENCE` halt triggers operator investigation. The node's persistence files are examined for evidence of tampering (unexpected modification times, mismatches against backup copies, signature failures if v0.4 signing is in place). The node's host system is examined for compromise. Recovery is via restoring known-good persistence (from backup or from peer state-sync) and rejoining the cluster after the node's host is verified clean.

### 23.5 State-integrity invariants

The substrate's state-management layer is correct iff a small set of invariants hold across normal operation and recovery scenarios. This subsection specifies the invariants. Conforming implementations maintain all of them; implementations violating any are detected via conformance vectors or via cluster-level dissent log accumulation.

**Invariant one: state is content-addressed.** Every state has a unique blake3 hash, computed over the state's canonical serialization. Two nodes with the same state produce the same hash; two nodes with different states (even by a single bit) produce different hashes. The hash is the substrate's primary state-integrity check.

**Invariant two: state advances only via committed frames.** A node's state advances when its cluster commits a frame; never otherwise. There is no manual state mutation, no out-of-band state injection, no state modification outside the lockstep. A state at frame N+1 is the deterministic transition of the state at frame N applied to frame N+1's committed inputs.

**Invariant three: persistence is content-verifiable.** A node can verify its persisted state's authenticity against external attestations — its own signature (v0.4), the cluster's BFT-supermajority signature, or the gossip overlay's published checkpoints. Persistence cannot be silently modified without detection.

**Invariant four: recovery is bounded by known-good attestations.** A recovering node anchors its state to a frame for which it has a verifiable attestation. State-sync that cannot be anchored to a known-good attestation is rejected; the recovering node treats unanchored state as untrusted.

**Invariant five: divergence halts the diverging node.** A node whose state diverges from the supermajority halts. It does not continue operating with corrupt state; it does not attempt to "fix" itself in place; it does not propagate corrupt state to peers. The halt is permanent until operator intervention or v0.4 state-sync recovery.

**Invariant six: dissent-log determinism (per §20.9 invariant seven).** Honest nodes observing the same supermajority tally produce identical dissent log entries. Dissent observations are reproducible across honest nodes, providing a consistent basis for misbehavior identification.

**Invariant seven: state hashes are forward-deterministic.** Given the state at frame N and the canonical-ordered inputs of frame N+1, the state hash at frame N+1 is uniquely determined. Two nodes computing the post-frame state hash from identical inputs and identical starting state produce identical hashes; this is the foundation that the BFT supermajority commit binds to.

These seven invariants compose with the §20.9 lockstep protocol invariants to specify the substrate's state-management correctness. An implementation that maintains all seven §23.5 invariants and all seven §20.9 invariants is correct regardless of which specific data structures, programming language, or operating system it uses. The conformance specification operationalizes the invariants through test vectors that exercise each.

### 23.6 Composition with other categories

State attacks engaged in §23 compose with other categories' defenses in three ways.

**Composition one: state integrity rests on cryptographic primitives (§19) and lockstep protocol invariants (§20).** The content-addressed state hash uses blake3; the BFT-signed state attestation uses ed25519; the deterministic transition that advances state requires the canonical input ordering and floating-point-free arithmetic invariants of §20.9. State integrity is conditional on both lower layers' correctness.

**Composition two: state-sync poisoning bridges to cluster topology (§21).** A recovering node requesting state-sync depends on the gossip overlay's correct propagation of cluster-state attestations; orphaning attacks (§21.4) on the gossip overlay can suppress the attestations the recovering node needs. The composition of §21 and §23 defenses is what makes recovery robust against both per-cluster and federation-level adversaries.

**Composition three: state attacks are typically detected, not prevented.** Unlike cryptographic attacks (§19) which the primitive's security prevents directly, state attacks largely succeed at the moment of corruption — what the substrate provides is *immediate detection* via supermajority commit, not prevention. The defense-in-depth strategy is: detect rapidly, halt the affected node, isolate the corruption, recover via verifiable mechanisms. Recovery, not prevention, is the substrate's primary state-integrity guarantee, and the treatise is honest about this distinction.

The next section (§24) walks economic attacks. Where §23 engaged threats to the substrate's data-integrity infrastructure, §24 engages threats to the substrate's economic-mechanism layer: joule-cost spoofing, economic griefing, fee manipulation, and MEV-equivalent extraction. The composition is that §23 defenses ensure the substrate computes the right state; §24 defenses ensure that the economic incentives operating on the substrate don't produce systematically harmful behavior even when state is correct.
## §24. Economic attacks

The substrate has no native token. There is no substrate-level coin to mint, stake, hold, or speculate on. There is no fee market — transitions cost nothing at the protocol layer. There is no leader auction, no validator set with inflationary rewards, no MEV-extractable transaction ordering. The substrate's design intentionally excludes the economic mechanisms that produce the most-studied attack surface of blockchain systems. This is itself a defense: many economic attacks that plague proof-of-work and proof-of-stake networks have no analog in the substrate because the targets simply do not exist.

But the substrate is not economically inert. Cluster operators bear costs (compute, storage, network, key management, operational labor) and may charge fees at the application layer for their services. Issuers operate businesses that issue cargo-bearing bytes, with their own economic models. Users transact with each other, exchanging cargo of various kinds, with prices and counterparty incentives operating around the protocol. Adversaries can attack these economic mechanisms even when the substrate's protocol layer is incorruptible.

This section walks four attack classes against the four-element rubric (§18.3). The attacks are §24.1 joule-cost spoofing, §24.2 economic griefing, §24.3 fee manipulation, §24.4 MEV-equivalent extraction. §24.5 closes by specifying what the substrate's economic-mechanism layer does *not* attempt to do — the architectural choices that make the substrate economically simpler than its blockchain alternatives, and the costs and benefits of that simplicity.

By the close of §24, the reader should understand that the substrate's economic-attack surface is small but non-zero, that the absences are structural defenses rather than oversights, and that the substrate's economic-mechanism layer composes with the issuer layer (§22) and the social layer (§25) to handle the attacks that protocol-level absence does not eliminate.

### 24.1 Joule-cost spoofing

**Attack.** The substrate's smart byte schema (§8.6) includes a two-part joule cost: an estimated component computed deterministically by the substrate, and an attested component reported by cluster operators based on actual energy measurements (RAPL counters, smart-meter telemetry). An adversary attempts to manipulate the joule cost — inflating it for transitions where the adversary benefits from higher reported costs (justifying higher application-layer fees the cluster operator collects), or deflating it where lower costs benefit the adversary (subsidizing competitors out of the market through artificially-cheap claimed operation).

The attack profile: a cluster operator reports false energy-attestation data, with cryptographic signatures that verify but values that don't correspond to actual measurements. Or: a substrate-using application aggregates joule-cost data across many transitions for compliance reporting (carbon accounting, energy efficiency claims), and an adversary manipulates the aggregate by spoofing the attestation values on transitions they control.

**Defense.** The substrate's defense against joule-cost spoofing is *bifurcated reporting plus external verification*.

The two-part joule cost separates *deterministic estimation* (which all honest nodes compute identically and which is therefore non-spoofable at the protocol layer) from *attested measurement* (which is operator-reported and is therefore spoofable but separately auditable). Applications that need joule-cost data for high-stakes purposes (carbon accounting, regulatory compliance) can use the deterministic estimate as a lower bound and treat the attested measurement as an operator claim subject to verification.

The deterministic estimate is computed by counting protocol-level operations (signature verifications, hash computations, byte-level transitions) and multiplying by published per-operation energy costs. The estimate is *conservative*: it captures the energy the substrate's transition function necessarily consumes, but does not include the energy spent on transport (which varies by network), persistence (which varies by storage), or operational overhead (which varies by deployment). An adversary cannot manipulate the deterministic estimate because every honest node computes the same value.

The attested measurement is *contestable*. A cluster operator's energy attestation is signed by the operator and committed to the cluster's lockstep, but its accuracy is the operator's claim. External verification mechanisms — third-party auditors examining the operator's energy infrastructure, comparing reported attestations against utility-bill data, checking RAPL counter consistency across the operator's hardware — can validate or contest the attestations. The substrate provides the cryptographic substrate for the attestation; external verification provides the truth.

For the aggregation-spoofing variant, the substrate's chain verification (§10) is the structural defense. Aggregate joule-cost claims that span many bytes can be audited by examining each byte's chain individually. An adversary inflating aggregate costs by spoofing per-transition attestations leaves a per-transition trail that auditors can examine.

**Residual risk.** Three residual risks. First, the deterministic estimate's published per-operation energy costs are themselves an attestation that operators rely on; if the substrate's authors publish incorrect per-operation costs, the deterministic estimate is wrong substrate-wide. The treatise's discipline is to publish the per-operation costs with sourced derivations and to update them as hardware evolves; v0.4 specification work. Second, attested measurements are inherently operator-trusted. Applications that demand higher confidence than operator attestation provides must compose layer-2 verification (third-party audit, regulator-issued certifications) on top of the substrate. The substrate cannot eliminate the trust requirement; it can only make the attestation auditable. Third, joule-cost data composes with carbon-accounting frameworks and energy-efficiency standards that themselves have specifications, units, and methodologies; mismatches between the substrate's joule-cost reporting and these external standards may produce data that is internally consistent but externally unusable.

**Operational response.** Joule-cost discrepancies between the deterministic estimate and the attested measurement, or between attested measurements and external evidence, are flagged for operator investigation. Operators with persistently-anomalous attestations are subject to dissent-log accumulation (§14) and potential cluster expulsion (planned v0.4). Applications using joule-cost data for high-stakes purposes (compliance, regulatory reporting) should require third-party audit of attestation accuracy as part of their risk management.

### 24.2 Economic griefing

**Attack.** An adversary submits transitions that are valid at the protocol layer but economically wasteful — transitions designed to consume cluster resources without producing useful work, to drive up operational costs for cluster operators and users, or to degrade application-layer performance through volume-based denial-of-service.

Examples: an adversary submits millions of zero-value transfers (sending one penny of USD-byte to themselves repeatedly), inflating cluster transaction volume without producing economic activity. An adversary submits maximum-size mints up to the issuer's authorization limits, padding the cluster's state with worthless byte allocations. An adversary submits high-frequency transition patterns designed to maximize cluster CPU and disk I/O without producing user-visible value.

**Defense.** The substrate's defenses against economic griefing operate at the *cluster-operator layer* (rate limiting, abuse detection) rather than at the protocol layer.

The substrate's protocol does not distinguish "useful" transitions from "useless" transitions; every cryptographically-valid transition is processed identically. This is intentional: protocol-level filtering of transitions based on economic value would require the substrate to make value judgments about user behavior, which is a layer-2 concern, not a substrate concern.

Cluster operators can implement application-layer rate limiting: limiting the rate of transitions per submitter pubkey, per source IP, per cluster connection, per cargo type. The substrate's transport layer (§17) does not natively support rate limiting, but operators can add it via reverse-proxy infrastructure, application-firewall rules, or custom transport implementations. Rate-limited submitters who exceed their limits are throttled or rejected at the operator's discretion.

Anomaly detection at the operator layer can identify griefing patterns: an unusual concentration of zero-value transitions from a small set of pubkeys, sustained high-frequency activity from a single submitter, mint patterns that exceed reasonable issuer activity. Identified griefers can be subject to operator-level access controls.

For volume-based economic attacks (the adversary's transitions consume disproportionate cluster resources), the substrate's natural economic feedback loop applies: cluster operators charge for their services, and operators experiencing higher griefing volumes either raise their fees, refuse griefing submitters, or accept the operational cost. Honest users gravitate to operators with lower-fee, lower-griefing environments; griefers are economically isolated.

**Residual risk.** Three residual risks. First, the substrate's protocol-level neutrality means any defense against griefing operates above the protocol layer. An adversary determined to grief can find operators willing to accept their volume (perhaps by paying premium application-layer fees) and continue griefing despite other operators' restrictions. Second, the line between "griefing" and "legitimate high-frequency use" is application-defined, not protocol-defined; an operator's anti-griefing rules can be miscalibrated, blocking legitimate users along with griefers. Third, distributed griefing campaigns (many adversarial pubkeys each submitting modest volume, collectively producing griefing-scale traffic) are harder to detect and isolate than concentrated single-source griefing.

**Operational response.** Cluster operators monitor traffic patterns and adjust rate limits, fees, and access controls in response to observed griefing. The substrate's gossip overlay can carry operator-level reputation about persistent griefers, allowing other operators to apply similar restrictions. Application-layer anti-abuse services (CAPTCHAs, proof-of-work challenges for submission, KYC for high-volume users at compliant operators) provide additional layers of defense.

### 24.3 Fee manipulation

**Attack.** Cluster operators charge application-layer fees for substrate access. An adversary attempts to manipulate the fee market — coordinating with other operators to fix prices above competitive levels, predatory pricing to drive competitors out of the market and then raising prices once concentrated, or fee-discrimination that steers high-value users to higher-fee tiers without their awareness.

The attack profile: adversaries operate cluster nodes (or related infrastructure: gateway services, wallet integrations) and exploit the substrate's open-architecture status to extract economic rent through fee manipulation, recreating the institutional middle's rent layer at the cluster-operator tier.

**Defense.** The substrate's defenses against fee manipulation are *transparency and competition* rather than protocol-layer control.

The substrate's protocol does not specify or constrain fees. Cluster operators are free to charge whatever they want for their services; users are free to choose which operators to use. The substrate's pricing market is therefore competitive at the operator layer, not regulated at the protocol layer.

Transparency of fees: operators publish their fee schedules; users can compare operators before choosing where to submit transitions; price differences across operators drive competition. The substrate's protocol-level interoperability (any cluster's bytes can be transferred to any other cluster, subject to cross-cluster transfer protocols when v0.4 ships) means user switching costs are low, increasing competitive pressure on operators.

Substrate-level interoperability: a user dissatisfied with one operator's fees can connect to a different operator with lower fees, with no substrate-level lock-in. The substrate's architecture explicitly avoids the network-effect lock-in that traditional financial intermediaries enjoy — there is no proprietary protocol, no licensed integration, no exclusive access right that prevents users from switching.

For the cartel-and-coordination variant, the substrate's transparency helps: visible fee schedules make coordinated price-fixing easier to detect than opaque industry pricing does. Antitrust enforcement (where it exists, and where regulators care) can investigate detected coordination. The substrate provides the data; regulators provide enforcement.

**Residual risk.** Three residual risks. First, the substrate-level interoperability defense is partial in v0.3: cross-cluster transfer is design-only (§11.3.3, §21.3), so user switching between clusters has friction until v0.4. Second, network-effect dynamics may concentrate cluster-operator market share over time even with substrate-level interoperability — the largest operators benefit from scale economies, customer-acquisition advantages, and integration network effects, leading to natural oligopoly. Third, fee discrimination based on user attributes (large users get lower rates, small users subsidize them; users in less-regulated jurisdictions get different prices than users in heavily-regulated jurisdictions) may emerge naturally and is hard to prevent without protocol-level intervention the substrate explicitly avoids.

**Operational response.** Users monitor fee competition and switch operators when better options emerge. Substrate-monitoring services can publish comparative fee data, helping users make informed choices. Antitrust regulators can investigate suspected price-fixing using publicly-available fee data. The substrate's role is to enable competition, not to prevent specific operators from charging high fees if they can find willing buyers.

### 24.4 MEV-equivalent extraction

**Attack.** Maximum Extractable Value (MEV) attacks in blockchain systems exploit the ability to reorder, insert, or censor transactions to extract value — front-running, back-running, sandwich attacks, censorship-for-pay. The attacks depend on the network having a transaction-ordering decision point (the block producer's choice of ordering) where an adversary can pay to influence the ordering.

The substrate has no equivalent decision point at the protocol layer. The substrate's input ordering is canonical hash-sort (§9.4 step 4), which is deterministic and unmanipulable; no node chooses ordering, no auction determines ordering, no fee market produces priority ordering. The substrate's protocol-level MEV surface is empty.

**Attack adapted to substrate.** An adversary attacks at the *application layer* — the wallets, market-making services, and exchange-equivalent applications that operate on top of the substrate. Application-layer adversaries can front-run users' transitions if they observe the user's intent before submission and can submit competing transitions faster. Cluster operators with privileged visibility into pending submissions before broadcast can attempt to capture MEV-equivalent value at the operator layer.

The attack profile: not protocol-level MEV (which is structurally absent), but application-layer MEV operating through observation and submission timing.

**Defense.** The substrate's defenses against application-layer MEV operate through *protocol-level neutrality* and *layer-2 privacy mechanisms*.

The substrate's protocol-level neutrality means no operator has guaranteed first-look at user submissions. A user submitting a transition to multiple operators simultaneously (gossip-broadcast at the application layer) prevents any single operator from front-running the user — the user's transition reaches all operators effectively at once, and the canonical hash-sort produces the same ordering at every cluster regardless of which operator received it first.

Layer-2 privacy mechanisms (encrypted submission pools, threshold-encrypted commit-reveal flows, zero-knowledge transition proofs) can hide users' intentions from operators until the transitions are committed, eliminating the observation window that application-layer MEV depends on. Several blockchain ecosystems have explored these mechanisms (Flashbots SUAVE, encrypted mempools); the substrate's architecture supports composing them as layer-2 services without protocol changes.

For the operator-level MEV variant (cluster operators with privileged visibility into pending submissions extracting value before broadcast), the substrate's economic-feedback defense applies: users will not use operators with documented MEV-extraction practices. The substrate's transparency makes the practices documentable; user choice handles the rest.

**Residual risk.** Three residual risks. First, layer-2 privacy mechanisms are themselves complex and may have their own attack surfaces; threshold-encrypted commit-reveal protocols have known vulnerabilities (selective abort attacks, free-riding on threshold-decryption participants), and substrate-level deployment of such mechanisms must engage these. Second, application-layer MEV requires application-layer observability of user intentions — wallet operators, exchange platforms, market-making services all have this observability and can extract MEV-equivalent value even with substrate-level neutrality. Third, the substrate's deterministic ordering eliminates protocol-level MEV but does not eliminate the *information asymmetries* between users and operators that economic-attack literature has shown produce predictable patterns of operator-favored extraction.

**Operational response.** Users seeking MEV resistance use layer-2 privacy mechanisms (when available) or submit through anonymized channels (Tor, mix-network) that prevent operators from associating submissions with user identity. Substrate-monitoring services can audit operator behavior for MEV-extraction patterns and publish operator reputation data. Application-layer wallets and integrators can implement MEV-resistant submission strategies (private relayer networks, just-in-time submission to minimize observation windows) on behalf of their users.

### 24.5 What the substrate's economic-mechanism layer does not attempt

The discipline of naming what the substrate is *not* doing economically continues. Several architectural choices are made deliberately to avoid problems that other distributed systems have grappled with at length.

**The substrate has no native token.** No substrate coin exists for users to hold, stake, or speculate on. The cargo-bearing bytes (USD-bytes, joule-bytes, etc.) are the only economic assets in the substrate; they reference external value (dollars, energy, calories) rather than substrate-internal value. The absence eliminates token-related attack surface entirely (no governance-token capture, no validator-stake centralization, no liquidity-pool manipulation), but it also means the substrate cannot self-fund through token issuance — operational sustainability depends on application-layer fees and external funding (issuer fees, operator service charges, donations).

**The substrate has no fee market at the protocol layer.** Transitions cost nothing to submit at the protocol level. This eliminates fee-market attack surface (priority-auction manipulation, fee-spike crisis dynamics, transaction censorship for fee-payment refusal), but it also means that *operators* must choose their fee mechanisms entirely outside the protocol. The protocol provides no built-in fee-collection mechanism, no enforcement of operator pricing, no protocol-level reward for operators' work.

**The substrate has no leader election.** No protocol-level role exists for "the leader of frame N." The lockstep protocol's deterministic input ordering and BFT supermajority commit do not require any single node to be in charge. This eliminates leader-election attack surface (leader bribery, leader-selection manipulation, leader DoS), but it also means the protocol does not benefit from leader-based optimizations (single-leader proposal phases, leader-driven transaction ordering).

**The substrate has no economic incentive layer for operators.** The protocol does not pay operators for their work. Operators must extract value through application-layer fees, by their own design. This eliminates the protocol-incentive attack surface (selfish mining, validator-reward manipulation, MEV-with-protocol-incentive), but it also means operator economics must be solved at the application layer, not within the substrate.

These four absences are the substrate's deliberate architectural simplicity. They eliminate large categories of attack surface that have consumed decades of distributed-systems research. They also mean the substrate's economic mechanisms are *minimal* — operators and applications must build their own economic structures on top, with the substrate providing only the underlying transaction layer. The treatise's claim is that this division of labor is correct: the substrate provides cryptographically-grounded byte-denominated commerce, and economic mechanisms layer on top, not within.

### 24.6 Composition with other categories

Economic attacks compose with other categories' defenses in three ways.

**Composition one: economic attacks frequently bridge from issuer attacks (§22).** Run dynamics, regulatory capture, fee manipulation — all involve economic mechanisms operating through the issuer or operator layer. The substrate's defenses at §22 (transparency, plurality, exit) are the same defenses that handle most §24 attacks; the categorization separates them analytically but the defenses are integrated.

**Composition two: economic attacks compose with social attacks (§25).** Coordinated griefing campaigns, fee-cartel formation, MEV-extraction normalization — these operate at the social layer (community coordination, narrative shaping, contributor influence) as much as at the economic layer. Readers concerned about the substrate's economic-attack surface should read §24 and §25 together.

**Composition three: the substrate's economic absences are the substrate's biggest economic defense.** No token / no fees / no leaders / no protocol-incentive layer — the four absences in §24.5 eliminate categorical attack surface. The treatise's discipline is that the absences are intentional and load-bearing, not oversights to be corrected. Engineers reading §24 should understand that the substrate's economic simplicity is itself a defense, and that the costs of the simplicity (no protocol-level operator funding, no built-in fee collection) are the costs the substrate has chosen to bear.

The next section (§25) walks social attacks. Where §24 engaged attacks operating through economic incentives, §25 engages attacks operating through social, organizational, and reputational mechanisms — contributor takeover, foundation capture, hostile fork-and-rebrand, name-squatting, search-result poisoning. The composition is that §24 defenses are largely architectural (the substrate's protocol-level economic absences); §25 defenses are largely organizational (the substrate's contributor practices, fork-detection, naming-and-discovery hygiene), and the social attacks are the ones the substrate's authors have the least direct control over.
## §25. Social attacks on the substrate itself

The substrate is a protocol, a reference implementation, a treatise, and a community of authors, contributors, operators, and users. The first three are technical artifacts that can be specified, audited, and verified. The fourth is a social structure that cannot be specified or audited in the same way. Adversaries who cannot break the cryptographic primitives, exploit the protocol invariants, or capture the issuers may instead attack the substrate's social and organizational structures — taking over contributor pools, capturing whatever foundation-equivalent stewardship exists, forking the substrate hostilely under deceptive branding, squatting the substrate's names and domains, poisoning search results so users find imitations rather than the real thing.

Social attacks are the most under-specified attack class in most distributed-systems threat models because they are organizational rather than technical. The substrate's threat model engages them anyway, because adversaries with enough resources to attack the substrate at scale will deploy organizational attacks alongside technical ones, and a treatise that ignored the social layer would be incomplete.

This section walks five attack classes against the four-element rubric (§18.3). The attacks are §25.1 contributor takeover, §25.2 foundation capture, §25.3 hostile fork-and-rebrand, §25.4 name-squatting, §25.5 search-result poisoning. §25.6 closes by acknowledging the limits of the substrate's authors' control over the social layer and naming the substrate's structural choices that limit the leverage social attackers can apply.

By the close of §25, the reader should accept that the substrate's social-attack defenses are organizational and procedural rather than cryptographic, that several of these defenses are genuinely partial, and that the substrate's structural choices (open-source licensing, no token, no foundation as a captureable entity) are the load-bearing defenses against the largest class of social attacks.

### 25.1 Contributor takeover

**Attack.** An adversary infiltrates the substrate's contributor pool — the developers, reviewers, and maintainers with commit access to the reference implementation. With enough contributor positions, the adversary can introduce deliberate vulnerabilities into the codebase, weaken existing defenses through subtle modifications, or block defensive pull requests through review-process manipulation. The infiltration may be patient (a single adversary establishing a multi-year track record of legitimate contributions before the malicious commit) or aggressive (a coordinated team of adversaries flooding the contributor pool simultaneously).

The attack profile is well-documented in adjacent open-source ecosystems: the XZ Utils backdoor (CVE-2024-3094, March 2024) was a textbook contributor takeover, with a single adversary spending three years establishing maintainer trust before introducing a backdoor that nearly reached production Debian and Red Hat distributions. Multiple Linux kernel contribution attempts have been detected and rejected through review-process vigilance. The pattern is real, ongoing, and well-resourced.

**Defense.** The substrate's defenses against contributor takeover operate through *multi-maintainer review*, *reproducible builds*, and *implementation diversity*.

Multi-maintainer review: every change to the reference implementation requires review and signoff from at least two independent maintainers, with explicit cryptographic signatures (signed commits, signed tags) bound to the maintainer's known identity. Single-maintainer commits are blocked by repository-level branch protection. The XZ Utils backdoor succeeded in part because the original maintainer's solo workflow had no second-reviewer requirement; the substrate's reference implementation's workflow does.

Reproducible builds: the reference implementation's release artifacts are built from source by multiple independent build systems, producing byte-identical outputs. A maintainer attempting to slip a backdoor into a released binary that doesn't match the source code is detected by reproducible-build verification. The Tor Project, Bitcoin Core, and several Linux distributions have established reproducible-build infrastructure; the substrate adopts the same practices.

Implementation diversity: the substrate's protocol is specified at the conformance-vector level (§20.9, §23.5 invariants) such that multiple implementations in different languages can be developed and tested against the same specification. A backdoor in the Rust reference implementation does not propagate to a Python implementation or a Go implementation; users running a non-Rust implementation are immune to Rust-specific backdoors. The substrate's authors actively encourage alternative implementations and provide the conformance vectors that make them verifiable.

The substrate's repository hosting (GitHub at v1.0, with mirrors on alternative platforms) is itself a single-point-of-failure that must be hardened. The reference implementation's release process includes signing every release tag with maintainer GPG keys; any release artifact whose signature does not verify is rejected. Mirror repositories on Codeberg, Sourcehut, and self-hosted Git servers provide redundancy if the primary platform is compromised.

**Residual risk.** Three residual risks. First, multi-maintainer review depends on maintainers having genuinely independent judgment; an adversary who can influence multiple maintainers simultaneously (through bribery, social engineering, or coordinated infiltration) can defeat the requirement. Second, reproducible builds verify that release artifacts match source code, but they do not verify that the source code itself is benign — a subtle backdoor introduced through legitimate-looking PRs that pass review will be in both the source and the reproduced binary. Third, implementation diversity is currently aspirational rather than actual; v0.3 has only the Rust reference implementation, and second-implementation work is community-dependent. The substrate's claim is conditional on the second-implementation work happening; if it doesn't, the substrate has only one implementation and its security is monoculture-bounded.

**Operational response.** Contributor takeover detected after the fact (post-commit anomaly review, security audit findings, community-reported suspicions) triggers commit-history forensic analysis, code review of recent changes by the suspect maintainer, and possible reversion of affected commits. The repository's branch-protection rules can be tightened to require additional reviewers for commits from probationary contributors. Legal action against confirmed adversaries is jurisdiction-dependent and slow; the substrate's primary response is technical (revert, audit, patch) rather than legal.

### 25.2 Foundation capture

**Attack.** Open-source projects often have foundation-equivalent organizational entities — non-profits that hold trademarks, manage domain registrations, organize conferences, distribute grants, and represent the project in legal and policy contexts. Adversaries attempt to capture these entities by getting their representatives onto governing boards, redirecting grant flows to favored contributors, manipulating trademark policy to constrain alternative implementations, or using legal authority to suppress critics.

The attack profile: an adversary establishes legitimate-looking participation in the foundation, builds reputation over months or years, and uses positional authority to shape the substrate's direction in ways that benefit the adversary. The adversary may be a state actor, a corporate competitor, an institutional incumbent threatened by the substrate, or a wealthy individual with personal motives.

**Defense.** The substrate's defense against foundation capture is *not having a foundation to capture*.
