The substrate v1.0 has no foundation, no non-profit entity, no centralized governance organization. The reference implementation is hosted at a public Git repository (with mirrors). The treatise is available under open-content licensing. The substrate's name is not trademarked at the protocol level. There is no entity that can be captured because there is no entity at all.

This is not an oversight. The substrate's authors have considered foundation creation and rejected it for v1.0. The reasoning: a foundation creates a single point of failure for capture attacks. Bitcoin's lack of a controlling foundation is widely credited as a structural defense against capture; Ethereum's foundation has been the target of repeated influence campaigns. The substrate adopts the Bitcoin pattern: protocol coordination through informal community processes, decision-making through implementation reality (whatever the reference and conforming implementations actually do is what the substrate is), and governance through fork dynamics (if the community disagrees with the reference implementation's direction, anyone can fork).

The cost of having no foundation is real: no centralized funding for development, no legal entity to defend trademarks or copyrights, no formal mechanism for resolving disputes between contributors. The substrate accepts these costs because the alternative — a foundation that can be captured — produces worse failure modes.

For functions a foundation would normally perform (grants for development, trademark defense, legal representation), the substrate relies on *distributed organic mechanisms*: individual donations to specific contributors or working groups, community-led trademark vigilance, contributor self-organization for legal questions. These mechanisms are messier and less efficient than foundation-mediated alternatives, but they are also harder for any single adversary to capture.

**Residual risk.** Three residual risks. First, the absence of a foundation means the substrate cannot easily defend trademark or copyright claims against bad-faith actors. An adversary who registers "smart byte substrate" as a trademark in their jurisdiction can attempt legal action against the actual substrate's authors and operators. The defense is preemptive trademark filing by community members in major jurisdictions and the documentation of prior art (this treatise itself, with its publication date, serves as prior art). Second, organic distributed funding may produce uneven contributor support — popular contributors get well-funded, less-visible contributors get neglected — leading to development priorities that don't reflect the substrate's actual needs. Third, the substrate's no-foundation choice depends on the community being capable of self-organization; if the community fragments or fails to coordinate, the absence of formal coordination structure is a liability.

**Operational response.** When trademark or naming disputes arise, the community responds through public documentation of the substrate's history, prior art, and existing usage patterns. When funding gaps appear, individual contributors and donors fill them through direct support. When legal questions arise, contributors consult their own legal counsel; the substrate's authors do not provide centralized legal guidance because there is no centralized authority to provide it.

### 25.3 Hostile fork-and-rebrand

**Attack.** An adversary forks the substrate's reference implementation, modifies it to serve adversarial purposes (introducing backdoors, removing privacy features, adding mandatory KYC, redirecting issuer-registry control to adversarial entities), and rebrands the result to compete with the legitimate substrate. The hostile fork claims compatibility with or improvement over the original, attempts to capture market share through marketing or regulatory advantage, and may eventually attempt to displace the legitimate substrate as "the" smart byte substrate.

The attack profile is well-known in cryptocurrency: numerous Bitcoin forks have attempted to position themselves as the "real" Bitcoin (Bitcoin Cash, Bitcoin SV, etc.), often with substantial marketing budgets and confused user populations. The substrate, being open-source, can be forked freely; the question is whether the legitimate substrate can be distinguished from hostile forks.

**Defense.** The substrate's defenses against hostile fork-and-rebrand operate through *conformance verification* and *protocol-level interoperability boundaries*.

Conformance verification: any implementation claiming to be the substrate must pass the substrate's conformance test vectors (§20.9, §23.5 invariants). Implementations that fail conformance are not the substrate, regardless of their branding. Hostile forks that introduce protocol-incompatible modifications fail conformance and are detectable as non-substrate. Hostile forks that modify only the application layer (wallets, integrations, operational tools) but maintain protocol conformance are technically still substrate implementations, but their non-protocol modifications can be identified and disclosed by community review.

Protocol-level interoperability boundaries: the substrate's bytes are content-addressed and chain-verified. A hostile fork operating its own clusters cannot transfer its bytes into legitimate substrate clusters (cross-cluster transfer protocols verify that the source cluster is part of the legitimate substrate's federation, identified through the gossip overlay's reputation history). The hostile fork's bytes remain confined to the hostile fork's clusters; users who want to interact with the legitimate substrate must use the legitimate implementation.

Reputation continuity: the legitimate substrate's reference implementation has a continuous commit history, a public maintainer set, conference appearances and academic citations, and an established treatise (this document). A hostile fork attempting to displace the legitimate substrate must establish equivalent reputation — which is expensive and slow — or rely on user confusion. Community education and documentation (clear "the legitimate substrate is the one at this URL with this maintainer set") helps users distinguish.

**Residual risk.** Three residual risks. First, conformance verification is only as good as the conformance vectors; an adversary who exploits conformance-vector gaps (test scenarios the vectors don't cover) can produce a hostile fork that passes conformance while still being adversarial. The substrate's authors must maintain the conformance vectors as a security-critical artifact. Second, regulatory or business pressure can produce situations where users *prefer* a hostile fork because it complies with regulations the legitimate substrate refuses to comply with — recreating the institutional middle's compliance environment within the substrate's nominal architecture. Third, marketing and user-acquisition campaigns operate at scales that small contributor communities cannot match; a well-funded hostile fork can capture significant user attention before the community's response materializes.

**Operational response.** Hostile forks identified by the community are documented publicly (a "known forks" page maintained by the substrate's contributors, listing each fork's deviations from conformance and the operational risks of using it). Conformance vectors are updated to detect specific hostile-fork modifications when they are identified. The legitimate substrate's authors make no claim to legal authority over forks; the substrate is open-source and forks are permitted by license. The defense is informational, not legal.

### 25.4 Name-squatting

**Attack.** An adversary registers domain names, social-media handles, package-registry namespaces, and other naming resources associated with the substrate, with the goal of either monetizing the squatting (forcing the substrate's authors to buy back the names at high prices) or impersonating the substrate to mislead users. Squatted resources may serve malicious content (phishing pages, malware downloads, fake documentation that omits security features).

**Defense.** The substrate's defenses against name-squatting are *preemptive registration*, *cryptographic identity*, and *community vigilance*.

Preemptive registration: substrate-related domain names (smartbyte.substrate, smart-byte-substrate.org, similar variants), package-registry namespaces (npm "smart-byte-substrate", crates.io "smart-byte-substrate", PyPI "smart-byte-substrate"), and social-media handles (Twitter/X, Mastodon, Bluesky, GitHub organization names) should be registered by the substrate's contributors before public launch. The cost is modest (a few hundred dollars per year for domains, free for most package registries) and the defensive value is significant.

Cryptographic identity: the substrate's authoritative communication channels — release announcements, security advisories, maintainer signatures — use cryptographic signing. A user verifying a security advisory's signature against published maintainer pubkeys can confirm authenticity regardless of which domain or platform delivered the message. Squatted domains delivering unsigned or invalidly-signed content are identifiable.

Community vigilance: substrate contributors and users monitor naming resources for suspicious registrations and report findings to community channels. Common naming-attack patterns (typosquatting, homograph attacks using Unicode lookalikes, sub-domain mimicry) are documented for user awareness.

**Residual risk.** Three residual risks. First, preemptive registration has practical limits — the substrate cannot register every plausible domain, every package-name variant, every possible social-media handle in every jurisdiction. Some squatting will occur. Second, cryptographic identity defenses depend on users actually verifying signatures, which most users do not do. Tool-level integration (signed package managers that verify automatically, signed-release verification in deployment tooling) reduces the user burden but is not universal. Third, social-media platforms have inconsistent name-recovery policies; a squatted handle on one platform may be unrecoverable while another platform might honor a substrate-related claim.

**Operational response.** Identified squatting attempts are documented publicly. Cryptographic verification is standardized in the substrate's release tooling so that users can detect impersonation attempts automatically. Community communication channels are documented (with cryptographic verification) so users have a clear "this is the legitimate substrate's official channel" reference.

### 25.5 Search-result poisoning

**Attack.** An adversary manipulates search-engine results so that users searching for "smart byte substrate" or similar queries find adversarial content — hostile forks (§25.3), squatted domains (§25.4), misleading documentation, or outright disinformation about the substrate. Search-result manipulation can be SEO-based (creating large volumes of substrate-mentioning content that links to adversarial destinations), advertising-based (paying for top-of-results ad placement on substrate-related queries), or platform-based (exploiting search-engine policies that penalize the legitimate substrate while elevating alternatives).

The attack is particularly effective during the substrate's early adoption period, when authoritative resources are still establishing themselves and adversaries can preempt the legitimate substrate's search ranking.

**Defense.** The substrate's defenses against search-result poisoning operate through *authoritative link infrastructure*, *direct-discovery channels*, and *community SEO*.

Authoritative link infrastructure: the substrate's reference implementation, treatise, and authoritative documentation all live at well-known canonical URLs that other authoritative sources (conference papers, GitHub README links, related-project documentation) link to consistently. Inbound links from authoritative sources improve search ranking organically; the substrate's authors cultivate these links through normal academic and open-source-community participation.

Direct-discovery channels: users seeking the substrate are encouraged to find it through trusted sources (academic papers, well-known security mailing lists, established open-source aggregators) rather than through search-engine queries. The substrate's release announcements include canonical URLs that users should bookmark; subsequent updates use the same canonical URLs rather than introducing new ones.

Community SEO: the substrate's contributors maintain documentation, blog posts, and community forum content that rank organically for substrate-related queries. Cross-linking between contributor sites builds the link graph that search engines use to identify authoritative content. The substrate's authors do not engage in paid SEO or link-buying schemes (which are themselves potentially compromising) but they also do not abstain from organic SEO best practices.

**Residual risk.** Three residual risks. First, search-engine algorithms are opaque and can change without warning; the substrate's organic ranking depends on the engines' continued willingness to surface authoritative content over manipulated content. Second, paid advertising can outrank organic results, particularly for high-value queries; an adversary willing to spend on ads can ensure their content appears before the legitimate substrate's content. Third, search-engine providers are themselves entities that can be pressured (by states, by large advertisers, by their own policies) to demote the substrate's content; if Google decides to remove the substrate from search results, the substrate's discovery via Google fails entirely. This is a §27 (physical attacks) concern as well as a §25 concern.

**Operational response.** Substrate users who detect search-result poisoning report it through community channels. Documentation is updated to provide direct links and to warn about the specific impersonation patterns observed. Long-term, the substrate's reputation builds through organic citation (by legitimate publications, conferences, and adjacent open-source projects) that is harder to displace through paid manipulation.

### 25.6 The limits of the substrate's authors' control

A meta-observation worth naming explicitly. Social attacks on the substrate operate at the layer where the substrate's authors have the least direct control. Cryptographic primitives (§19) are mathematically constrained. Protocol invariants (§20) are auditable. Cluster topology (§21) is operationally bounded. Issuer behavior (§22) operates in legal and regulatory frameworks. State integrity (§23) is technically verifiable. Economic mechanisms (§24) operate in market conditions.

Social structures are different. They depend on community formation, individual participation, organizational coordination, and reputational dynamics — none of which can be specified in conformance vectors or enforced by protocol logic. The substrate's defenses at the social layer are organizational practices (multi-maintainer review, signed releases, conformance vectors), structural choices (no foundation, open licensing, no token), and community vigilance — all of which are necessary but none of which is sufficient on its own.

The substrate's structural choices that limit social-attack leverage:

**No token.** The substrate has no native asset to manipulate, capture, or use as a leverage point. Adversaries cannot acquire substrate-tokens to gain governance power because there are no substrate-tokens.

**No foundation.** The substrate has no centralized organizational entity to capture, infiltrate, or sue. Adversaries seeking to apply organizational pressure must do so against decentralized contributors, which is harder.

**Open licensing.** The substrate is dual-licensed under Apache-2.0 and MIT. Adversaries cannot leverage license restrictions against legitimate users; users cannot leverage license restrictions against adversaries either, but the symmetry favors free use over capture.

**Conformance verification.** The substrate's identity is the conformance vectors. Implementations that pass conformance are the substrate; implementations that fail are not. Adversaries cannot rebrand non-substrate implementations as the substrate without passing the same vectors that the legitimate implementation passes.

**Cryptographic continuity.** The substrate's authoritative communications use cryptographic signing. Adversaries cannot impersonate the substrate's authors without compromising their keys, and the multi-maintainer review structure makes single-key compromise insufficient for substrate-wide impersonation.

These five structural choices compose into a substrate that is harder to socially attack than alternative architectures with tokens, foundations, restrictive licenses, or centralized authority structures. The choices are not perfect defenses, but they raise the cost of social attacks substantially and limit the leverage adversaries can apply.

The honest disclosure: social attacks are the threat class the substrate's authors are most worried about over the long term. Cryptographic primitives are conservative for decades. Protocol invariants are auditable indefinitely. Social structures are fragile, depend on continued community engagement, and can be eroded slowly enough that defenders may not notice until significant damage is done. The substrate's structural choices help, but the substrate's social health requires ongoing community work that the substrate's authors cannot fully control.

### 25.7 Composition with other categories

Social attacks compose with other categories' defenses in three ways.

**Composition one: social attacks frequently bridge from issuer attacks (§22) and economic attacks (§24).** Foundation capture, contributor takeover, and search-result poisoning often operate alongside regulatory capture (§22.3), fee manipulation (§24.3), or governance social engineering (§22.2). Adversaries with resources for one category typically deploy across multiple categories simultaneously. The defenses must be similarly integrated.

**Composition two: social attacks compose with legal attacks (§26) and physical attacks (§27).** A state-level adversary attacking the substrate may use social attacks (foundation capture, contributor pressure) alongside legal attacks (operator obligation, securities classification) and physical attacks (operator coercion, infrastructure seizure). The substrate's defenses across §25, §26, §27 are interdependent.

**Composition three: social-layer defenses are organizational, not technical.** Unlike the cryptographic and protocol defenses of §19-§23, social-layer defenses depend on community practices, organizational hygiene, and reputational dynamics. The substrate's authors maintain the technical defenses; the substrate's community maintains the social defenses. The treatise's discipline is to name this division of labor honestly: the substrate is more than a protocol, and its security depends on more than protocol correctness.

The next section (§26) walks legal attacks. Where §25 engaged threats operating through social and organizational mechanisms, §26 engages threats operating through legal and regulatory mechanisms — securities classification, transaction taxation, operator obligations, contract-law claims, and intellectual-property attacks. The composition is that §25 defenses depend on community-level coordination; §26 defenses depend on jurisdictional plurality and protocol-level absence of regulatable features. Together, §25 and §26 cover the non-technical attack surface that adversaries use when they cannot break the substrate technically.
## §26. Legal attacks

The substrate operates in legal environments. Cluster operators are organized under specific jurisdictions' laws. Issuers are typically regulated entities (stablecoin providers under banking law, energy producers under utility regulation). Users transact across borders subject to taxation, anti-money-laundering, and securities regulation. The substrate's protocol-level neutrality cannot insulate the substrate's participants from the legal frameworks they operate within; legal attacks are real, jurisdictionally varied, and increasingly sophisticated.

This section walks five attack classes against the four-element rubric (§18.3). The attacks are §26.1 securities classification, §26.2 transaction taxation, §26.3 operator obligation, §26.4 contract-law claims, §26.5 intellectual-property attacks. §26.6 closes by acknowledging the substrate's structural choices that limit legal-attack leverage and naming the jurisdictional plurality the substrate depends on.

The treatise does not provide legal advice. The substrate's authors are not lawyers; the engagements below describe attack patterns and structural defenses, not legal opinions about specific jurisdictions or specific operators. Substrate adopters in any jurisdiction must consult their own counsel for compliance with applicable laws.

By the close of §26, the reader should accept that the substrate's legal-attack surface is real and partially defensible, that the substrate's "protocol not service" framing is load-bearing for limiting legal exposure, and that several of the substrate's most consequential defenses (jurisdictional plurality, multi-issuer architecture, layer-2 separation) operate at the legal layer as well as the technical layer.

### 26.1 Securities classification

**Attack.** A regulator classifies the substrate's bytes — or specific cargo classes within them — as securities under their jurisdiction's securities law. With securities classification, the substrate's bytes become subject to registration requirements, disclosure obligations, anti-fraud provisions, and intermediary-licensing rules. Issuers face securities-registration obligations they cannot meet without becoming regulated broker-dealers; cluster operators face exchange-registration obligations; users face investor-accreditation requirements.

The U.S. SEC has applied the Howey test (SEC v. W.J. Howey Co., 328 U.S. 293, 1946) to crypto-asset offerings since the 2017 DAO Report and has pursued enforcement against numerous projects on securities grounds. The EU's Markets in Crypto-Assets Regulation (MiCA, in force since 2024) provides a more structured framework but imposes significant licensing requirements on most crypto-asset service providers. Other jurisdictions (Japan, Singapore, UAE) have variously stricter or more permissive frameworks.

The attack profile: a regulator reaches the substrate's authors, issuers, or operators with enforcement actions claiming securities violations, with the goal of either forcing compliance (registration, licensing, restricted access) or shutting down substrate operations within the regulator's jurisdiction.

**Defense.** The substrate's structural defenses against securities classification operate through *cargo-class neutrality* and *non-investment-product framing*.

The substrate's bytes carry cargo. The cargo is what determines regulatory status. A USD-byte represents a claim on dollar reserves held by an issuer; whether this constitutes a security depends on the issuer's structure (a regulated stablecoin provider's USD-bytes face different regulatory treatment than an unregulated startup's USD-bytes). A joule-byte represents a claim on energy production; whether this is a security depends on whether the joule-byte's holder expects investment returns from the issuer's efforts (the Howey test's "expectation of profits derived from the efforts of others" prong) or simply represents a unit of energy purchase. A kcal-byte representing already-produced food is not a security under any reasonable reading; it's a food purchase.

The substrate's protocol layer does not produce securities. The protocol layer produces *bytes* — cryptographically-provenanced units of arbitrary cargo. Whether any specific cargo class is a security is an issuer-and-cargo-class question, not a substrate-layer question. The substrate's authors do not market, sell, or distribute investment products; they ship a protocol and a reference implementation under open-source licensing.

For cargo classes that *are* securities under applicable law (some kinds of governance tokens, some kinds of fractional asset claims), the appropriate response is for issuers to register with relevant regulators, comply with disclosure requirements, and limit distribution to qualified parties. The substrate's protocol does not prevent compliance; it provides the cryptographic transaction layer underneath whatever compliance regime the issuer adopts.

**Residual risk.** Three residual risks. First, regulatory definitions evolve. A regulator with sufficient creativity can argue that almost any cargo class is a security if it benefits from any "common enterprise" or "expectation of profits"; the substrate's cargo-class diversity does not by itself prevent expansive regulatory readings. Second, the substrate's authors and reference-implementation maintainers may be pursued under accomplice or aiding-and-abetting theories even if they do not directly issue or distribute regulated cargo; this has happened in adjacent crypto enforcement cases — Alexey Pertsev was convicted in Dutch court in May 2024 (sentenced to 64 months, appealing) on a theory that Tornado Cash itself executed money laundering, while Roman Storm was convicted in U.S. federal court in August 2025 on the unlicensed-money-transmission charge but received hung-jury outcomes on the more serious money laundering and sanctions charges. Third, unregistered offerings of substrate-issued cargo by issuers operating in regulated jurisdictions create securities violations regardless of the substrate's protocol-level neutrality; the issuers bear the legal exposure, but reputational damage to the substrate is real.

**Operational response.** Issuers facing securities classification typically respond by either registering and complying (becoming regulated entities with significant operational overhead) or restricting distribution to non-U.S./non-EU users (geographic exclusion through application-layer KYC and access controls). Substrate-level operators in regulated jurisdictions may face exchange-registration obligations if they facilitate trading; the substrate's protocol-level absence of trading mechanisms (no order books, no matching engines at the protocol layer) is helpful but not dispositive — application-layer trading services on top of the substrate may still trigger exchange registration. Users in regulated jurisdictions consult their own legal counsel about which substrate cargo classes they can hold or transact in compliantly.

### 26.2 Transaction taxation

**Attack.** Tax authorities classify substrate transitions as taxable events — every transfer of cargo-bearing bytes treated as a sale or exchange triggering capital-gains calculation, every cross-cluster transfer as a realization event, every micropayment as a separate taxable transaction. Compliance becomes operationally infeasible at substrate scale: a user making thousands of micropayments per year faces tax-reporting obligations that overwhelm the underlying transaction value.

The U.S. IRS treats cryptocurrency transactions as taxable events for federal income tax purposes (IRS Notice 2014-21, expanded by the 2024 Form 1099-DA reporting requirements). The U.K. HMRC has similar policies. Many jurisdictions are actively expanding their tax-reporting requirements for crypto-asset transactions. The reporting burden, even when each transaction's tax liability is small, can be prohibitive.

The attack profile is not adversarial in the conventional sense — tax authorities are not seeking to harm the substrate — but the cumulative effect of comprehensive transaction taxation can make substrate use operationally untenable for users in jurisdictions with aggressive enforcement.

**Defense.** The substrate's defenses against transaction-taxation overreach operate through *cargo-class differentiation* and *layer-2 reporting tools*.

Cargo-class differentiation: not every cargo class triggers the same tax treatment. USD-byte transfers between two users may be treated as currency transfers (potentially with no realized gain or loss if the USD-byte's value is constant) rather than as sales of property. Joule-byte transfers may be treated as energy purchases (deductible business expenses for businesses, not capital-gains events). Kcal-byte transfers may be treated as food purchases. The substrate's cargo-neutral architecture allows users to compose transactions that match the tax treatment they need; the substrate does not impose a single tax treatment across all cargo.

Layer-2 reporting tools: the substrate's content-addressed history (§10) provides complete, auditable records of every transaction a user has participated in. Tax-preparation tools operating on top of the substrate can produce comprehensive reports — categorizing transactions by cargo class, computing realized gains and losses where applicable, generating jurisdiction-specific tax forms. The substrate provides the data; layer-2 services provide the compliance interface.

For micropayment-scale transactions, several jurisdictions provide de minimis exemptions (the U.S. allows up to $200 in personal-use crypto transactions per transaction without reporting, though this varies). The substrate's transaction-volume capacity does not change the applicability of these exemptions; users transacting below the de minimis threshold remain unaffected.

**Residual risk.** Three residual risks. First, the U.S. 1099-DA reporting regime (effective 2025-2026) requires certain crypto-asset brokers to report user transactions to the IRS, with penalties for non-reporting. Substrate operators offering broker-like services in U.S. jurisdiction must comply or restrict access. Second, jurisdictions with more aggressive tax enforcement (some EU member states, India, China) treat all crypto-asset transactions with high scrutiny; users in these jurisdictions face high compliance costs. Third, the substrate's transparency (every transition visible on the chain) makes evasion harder than traditional cash transactions; users seeking transaction privacy must use layer-2 anonymizing tools, which themselves face increasing regulatory pressure.

**Operational response.** Users consult tax-preparation tools and tax professionals for compliance with their jurisdiction's reporting requirements. Layer-2 services may offer cargo-class-specific tax reporting (separate reports for stablecoin transfers, energy purchases, food purchases) that simplify compliance. Users in jurisdictions with high compliance burden may shift to cargo classes with lower tax friction (joule-bytes for energy purchases rather than USD-bytes for the equivalent dollar value).

### 26.3 Operator obligation

**Attack.** Regulators impose obligations on substrate cluster operators — anti-money-laundering reporting, know-your-customer requirements, sanctions compliance, transaction monitoring, suspicious-activity reporting. Operators face the choice of complying (becoming regulated financial institutions with significant overhead) or restricting their services to non-regulated jurisdictions or non-regulated user classes.

The FATF Travel Rule (Recommendation 16, expanded to crypto-assets in 2019) requires virtual-asset service providers to collect and transmit originator and beneficiary information for transactions above a threshold. The EU's Transfer of Funds Regulation (in force June 2023) imposes parallel requirements at lower thresholds. The U.S. Bank Secrecy Act and OFAC sanctions impose reporting and screening obligations on any operator deemed a "financial institution" or "money services business."

The attack profile: regulators classify substrate operators as virtual-asset service providers, money services businesses, or equivalent regulated entities, imposing operational costs that small operators cannot absorb and forcing consolidation around large, regulated, capture-friendly operators.

**Defense.** The substrate's defenses against operator-obligation overreach operate through *protocol-not-service framing* and *jurisdictional plurality*.

Protocol-not-service framing: the substrate is a protocol specification and a reference implementation, distributed under open-source licenses. The substrate's authors do not operate clusters, do not facilitate transactions, do not provide custody, do not execute trades. Cluster operators are independent entities; the substrate's authors do not control them, profit from them, or direct their compliance. This separation is structurally important: it limits the substrate's authors' regulatory exposure while allowing operators to make their own compliance decisions.

The protocol-not-service distinction has been litigated in adjacent crypto contexts with mixed but instructive results. Bitcoin's authors have largely escaped direct regulatory exposure because no single entity operates Bitcoin's network. Ethereum's foundation has had more regulatory exposure because of the foundation's coordinating role. The Tornado Cash precedent is particularly relevant and has evolved across jurisdictions: the U.S. Treasury sanctioned the Tornado Cash smart contracts in August 2022 (the first time OFAC sanctioned smart contract addresses directly); the U.S. Court of Appeals for the Fifth Circuit ruled on November 26, 2024 that immutable smart contracts do not qualify as "property" under federal law and that OFAC exceeded its authority; OFAC lifted the sanctions in March 2025; Pertsev was nonetheless convicted in Dutch court in May 2024 (appealing); Storm was convicted in U.S. federal court in August 2025 on unlicensed-money-transmission grounds but received hung-jury outcomes on the more serious charges, and the U.S. DOJ issued an "Ending Regulation by Prosecution" memo in April 2025 signaling reduced criminal-enforcement appetite for protocol developers. The substrate's authors aim for the Bitcoin pattern — code released under open license, no foundation, no centralized service offering — to maximize the protocol-not-service positioning, though the legal effectiveness of the positioning is jurisdiction-dependent and the precedent landscape continues to evolve.

Jurisdictional plurality: substrate operators distributed across multiple jurisdictions provide redundancy against single-jurisdiction enforcement. An operator in the U.S. complying with FATF Travel Rule requirements does not prevent an operator in Switzerland from operating with different compliance choices. Users seeking to avoid Travel Rule reporting can transact through operators in jurisdictions where the rule does not apply, with the legal risks of doing so depending on the user's own jurisdiction.

The substrate's protocol-level absence of operator-friendly compliance features (no built-in KYC integration, no native transaction monitoring, no protocol-mandated reporting) is honest about what the substrate does not provide. Operators wanting these features must implement them at the application layer, which both limits universality (compliance is operator-specific, not substrate-wide) and protects the substrate's protocol-level neutrality.

**Residual risk.** Three residual risks. First, the protocol-not-service distinction is legally tenuous in some jurisdictions and the precedent landscape is unsettled. The Pertsev conviction (Dutch court May 2024) shows that European jurisdictions may pursue protocol authors directly; the Storm verdict (August 2025) shows the U.S. theory has narrowed to the unlicensed-money-transmitter charge while excluding the broader money-laundering and sanctions theories; the Fifth Circuit's November 2024 ruling and the OFAC delisting in March 2025 represent partial wins for the protocol-not-service framing. The substrate's authors' jurisdictional choices and operational practices affect their direct exposure, and the legal landscape is changing fast enough that v1.0-era positioning may not match v2.0-era enforcement. Second, jurisdictional-plurality defense fails if regulators coordinate globally. The FATF's mutual evaluation processes increasingly pressure jurisdictions to harmonize crypto regulations; a U.S./EU/Japan/UK alignment on substrate operator obligations could dramatically shrink the available operator set. Third, well-meaning compliance choices by individual operators (offering reporting tools, implementing KYC, adopting Travel Rule wrappers) can normalize compliance expectations and pressure non-compliant operators by competitive comparison rather than by regulatory mandate.

**Operational response.** Operators in regulated jurisdictions typically register as appropriate (money services businesses, virtual-asset service providers, or equivalent), implement required compliance measures, and absorb the operational costs. Operators preferring to avoid regulation operate in less-regulated jurisdictions, restricting their user base to those willing to use offshore operators. Users navigate the operator landscape based on their own compliance needs and risk tolerances. The substrate's role is to make multiple operator choices available; the substrate does not prescribe specific compliance regimes.

### 26.4 Contract-law claims

**Attack.** Adversaries pursue contract-law claims against substrate operators, issuers, or even authors, arguing that substrate participation creates implicit contracts that have been breached. Examples: a user whose bytes are lost due to a software bug sues the reference implementation's authors for breach of implied warranty. An issuer whose business is harmed by substrate-level changes sues the authors for tortious interference. A regulator argues that the substrate's documentation creates promises that constitute consumer contracts subject to consumer-protection law.

The attack profile: contract-law claims convert technical and architectural choices into legal liabilities, with damages potentially exceeding any rational scaling of the substrate's authors' actual involvement. Open-source software has historically benefited from license disclaimers (the "AS IS" clause in MIT/Apache licenses) but these protections vary across jurisdictions and may not survive aggressive contract-law theories.

**Defense.** The substrate's defenses against contract-law claims operate through *open-source license disclaimers*, *no-promises framing*, and *contributor-individual liability containment*.

Open-source license disclaimers: the substrate is dual-licensed under Apache-2.0 and MIT. Both licenses include broad disclaimers of warranty ("AS IS"), explicit limitations of liability, and disclaimers of fitness for any particular purpose. The treatise and the reference implementation both bear these disclaimers prominently. Users accepting the substrate accept the licenses; the disclaimers' enforceability varies by jurisdiction but provides substantial legal protection in most.

No-promises framing: the treatise is careful to describe what the substrate *does* and what it *does not provide*, with consistent acknowledgment of partial freedoms (§6.2), unimplemented v0.4 work (§11.5, §15.5, §16.5, §21.6, §23.4), and structural limits. The substrate does not promise the institutional middle's services; it does not guarantee specific outcomes; it does not warrant specific cargo classes for specific use cases. The treatise's discipline of explicit non-promises limits the surface area for "you promised X and didn't deliver" claims.

Contributor-individual liability containment: the substrate's contributor model is open and distributed. Individual contributors take responsibility for their own contributions; there is no centralized entity (no foundation, §25.2) that bears collective liability for the project. This limits the targets for contract-law claims to individual contributors, who can be defended on individual-licensee grounds rather than as collective project sponsors.

**Residual risk.** Three residual risks. First, license disclaimers are not universally enforceable. Some jurisdictions (notably Germany under § 309 BGB, and parts of Brazilian consumer law) limit the enforceability of broad warranty disclaimers; users in these jurisdictions may have residual contract claims that the licenses cannot disclaim. Second, contributor-individual liability containment depends on contributors actually being individuals; a contributor working through an LLC or corporate entity may inherit institutional liability that broader claims can reach. Third, the substrate's reference implementation, treatise, and documentation are *content* that may bear obligations beyond software licensing — content that could be argued to constitute professional advice (financial, technical, legal), with corresponding professional-liability exposure for the authors.

**Operational response.** Substrate authors maintain prominent disclaimer notices in the treatise, in the reference implementation, and in published documentation. Authors who wish to limit their personal exposure may organize their substrate-related work through limited-liability entities that bear the work's risks rather than the individual authors. Users with potential contract claims are encouraged to pursue them through individual operator relationships (where commercial agreements actually exist) rather than through the substrate's authors (who have no commercial relationship with users).

### 26.5 Intellectual-property attacks

**Attack.** Adversaries pursue intellectual-property claims to constrain the substrate's distribution, modification, or use. Patent claims: an adversary holds a patent that allegedly reads on the substrate's protocol or implementation, demanding licensing fees or claiming infringement damages. Trademark claims: an adversary registers "smart byte substrate" as a trademark in their jurisdiction and pursues legal action against the actual substrate's authors and operators. Copyright claims: an adversary argues that the substrate's reference implementation contains copyrighted material from another project without proper attribution, triggering DMCA takedowns or copyright-infringement claims.

The attack profile is well-known in adjacent open-source contexts: SCO's lawsuits against Linux from 2003 onward (eventually defeated but consuming significant defense resources), patent trolls' assertions against open-source projects (typically settling or being dismissed but creating sustained legal pressure), trademark disputes over project names. The substrate is potentially exposed to all of these.

**Defense.** The substrate's defenses against IP attacks operate through *prior-art establishment*, *patent-non-aggression* via the Apache license, and *cryptographic-namespace* approaches.

Prior-art establishment: this treatise itself, with its publication date and detailed technical specifications, serves as prior art for any future patent claims that might attempt to read on the substrate's mechanisms. The substrate's reference implementation, published openly with timestamped commits, similarly establishes prior art. Any patent claim against the substrate must contend with detailed prior-art documentation that predates most plausible patent priority dates.

Patent-non-aggression via Apache license: the Apache 2.0 license includes an explicit patent grant from contributors to users — Apache 2.0 contributors implicitly license any patents they hold that read on their contributions. This protects substrate users from being sued by substrate contributors over patent claims arising from substrate contributions. The license does not protect against third-party patent assertions, but it limits the patent-attack surface from within the substrate's own contributor community.

Cryptographic-namespace: the substrate uses content-addressing throughout (byte identities, history hashes, state hashes). Names that matter at the protocol level are cryptographic, not human-readable. An adversary pursuing trademark claims against "smart byte substrate" cannot reach the protocol's content-addressed identifiers; they can only target the human-readable name. The substrate is robust to renaming if necessary; the protocol's identity is in its conformance vectors and content-addressing, not in its trademark.

**Residual risk.** Three residual risks. First, patent litigation is expensive to defend even when meritless. An adversary willing to bring frivolous patent claims can drain the substrate's contributors' resources before being defeated, potentially achieving the adversary's goal of hampering substrate development. Second, trademark claims in specific jurisdictions can succeed even when the trademark is bad-faith; the substrate's contributors may need to navigate name changes in specific markets without prior coordination. Third, copyright claims based on alleged code-similarity to closed-source proprietary systems can be hard to defend even when no actual infringement occurred; the substrate's reference implementation must be audited for clean-room provenance before release.

**Operational response.** Substrate contributors maintain documentation of their work's clean-room provenance (no contributor uses proprietary code from previous employers, all contributions are written from public specifications and prior-art only). Patent claims against the substrate are documented publicly and defended through prior-art arguments; the substrate's authors do not settle frivolous patent claims and do not pay licensing fees for invalid patents. Trademark issues in specific jurisdictions are handled by community-led trademark filings and (where necessary) name variants for specific markets.

### 26.6 The structural limits of legal-attack defenses

The substrate's structural choices that limit legal-attack leverage:

**Protocol not service.** The substrate's authors do not operate clusters, do not facilitate transactions, do not provide custody. The protocol-not-service framing is the foundation of legal-attack defense across §26.1 (no securities offering), §26.3 (no operator obligation on authors), and §26.4 (no contractual relationship with users). The framing's legal effectiveness is jurisdiction-dependent but provides substantial protection in jurisdictions that recognize the distinction.

**Jurisdictional plurality.** Substrate operators, issuers, and users distributed across multiple jurisdictions provide redundancy against single-jurisdiction enforcement. The substrate inherits this defense from its federated architecture; cluster topology that spans jurisdictions naturally produces legal-attack-resistance through jurisdictional diversity.

**Open-source licensing.** Apache 2.0 and MIT licenses provide warranty disclaimers, patent grants, and broad permissions for use and modification. The licenses' protections are not absolute but provide substantial legal cover for users, contributors, and operators in most jurisdictions.

**No tradable substrate-internal asset.** The substrate has no native token (§24.5); there is no substrate-issued asset that can be classified as a security or subject to securities regulation. Cargo-bearing bytes inherit their legal classification from the cargo, not from the substrate; users seeking to limit securities exposure can choose cargo classes that don't trigger securities classification in their jurisdiction.

**Cryptographic-namespace identity.** The substrate's protocol-level identity is in its conformance vectors and content-addressing, not in human-readable trademarks. The substrate is robust to renaming if necessary; trademark attacks on specific names cannot reach the protocol itself.

These five structural choices compose into a substrate that has *limited* legal-attack exposure compared to alternative architectures with foundations, native tokens, or proprietary licensing. The defenses are partial and jurisdiction-dependent, but they raise the cost of legal attacks substantially and limit the leverage adversaries can apply.

The honest disclosure: legal attacks are jurisdictionally varied in ways that generic threat-model engagement cannot fully capture. The substrate's defenses are designed to be robust across jurisdictions, but specific jurisdictions can mount attacks that the substrate's general-purpose defenses do not anticipate. Substrate adopters must consult their own legal counsel in their own jurisdiction; the treatise provides architectural context but cannot provide jurisdiction-specific legal guidance.

### 26.7 Composition with other categories

Legal attacks compose with other categories' defenses in three ways.

**Composition one: legal attacks frequently follow social attacks (§25) or accompany physical attacks (§27).** A state-level adversary may pursue legal claims against substrate operators while simultaneously attempting contributor takeover (§25.1) and infrastructure seizure (§27). The defenses across §25, §26, §27 must be integrated; isolating any one category produces incomplete defense.

**Composition two: legal-attack defenses depend on structural protocol choices that enable cross-jurisdiction operation.** The substrate's protocol-level absence of operator-friendly compliance features (no built-in KYC, no native AML reporting, no protocol-mandated transaction monitoring) is what allows operators in less-regulated jurisdictions to operate without the compliance overhead that operators in heavily-regulated jurisdictions face. Without this protocol-level neutrality, the substrate would be uniformly subject to the most-aggressive jurisdiction's regulations.

**Composition three: legal-attack defenses are inherently partial and jurisdiction-dependent.** Unlike cryptographic defenses (§19) which are mathematically constrained, or protocol invariants (§20) which are auditable, legal defenses depend on specific legal regimes and their interpretation. The substrate's discipline is to name the structural choices that maximize legal robustness while acknowledging that specific jurisdictions can mount attacks that no general-purpose architectural defense can fully prevent.

The next section (§27) walks physical attacks. Where §26 engaged threats operating through legal and regulatory mechanisms, §27 engages threats operating through physical-coercive mechanisms — operator coercion, hardware seizure, network egress blocking, BGP hijacking. The composition is that §26 defenses operate within legal frameworks; §27 defenses operate when legal frameworks are bypassed entirely or used as cover for coercive action. Together, §26 and §27 cover the state-level attack surface that the substrate's most resourceful adversaries can deploy.
## §27. Physical attacks

The substrate operates on physical infrastructure: servers in datacenters, network connections through ISPs and IXPs, undersea cables, BGP-routed packets, electrical power. Adversaries with physical access to any of these can attack the substrate by mechanisms that have no protocol-layer defense — seizing servers, coercing operators, blocking network traffic, hijacking BGP routes. The substrate's defenses against physical attacks operate at the *operational* layer (geographic distribution, infrastructure plurality, backup recovery) rather than at the cryptographic or protocol layer.

This section walks four attack classes against the four-element rubric (§18.3). The attacks are §27.1 operator coercion, §27.2 hardware seizure, §27.3 network egress blocking, §27.4 BGP hijacking. §27.5 closes by acknowledging the partial nature of the substrate's physical-attack defenses and naming the operational practices that limit damage even when individual operators are compromised.

The honest disclosure that this section makes upfront: the substrate's defenses against physical attacks are weaker than its defenses in any other category engaged so far in Part III. Adversaries with state-level coercive power can substantially harm specific substrate operators, specific clusters, and specific user populations. The substrate's claim is not that physical attacks fail; it is that physical attacks against any single point in the substrate's federated infrastructure produce bounded damage that the rest of the federation continues operating around. This is *operational resilience*, not *cryptographic resistance*, and the difference matters.

By the close of §27, the reader should accept that the substrate's federated architecture is the load-bearing physical-attack defense, that geographic and jurisdictional plurality are necessary conditions for substrate freedom claims, and that physical attacks are where the substrate's defenses cross from technical mechanisms into operational practices that the substrate's authors have less direct control over than at the protocol layer.

### 27.1 Operator coercion

**Attack.** A state actor — law enforcement, intelligence services, or an authoritarian government — coerces a substrate cluster operator into modifying their operations to serve the state's purposes. The coercion may be lawful (court orders, regulatory directives, formal legal compulsion) or unlawful (extralegal pressure, threats, detention, asset seizure outside legal process). The coerced operator may be required to: provide real-time access to the cluster's transitions for surveillance, refuse service to specific users or pubkeys, falsify state attestations, or shut down operations entirely.

The attack profile spans a wide range. At the legal end: a U.S. cluster operator served with a National Security Letter requiring data disclosure. In the middle: a Chinese operator subject to the Personal Information Protection Law's data-localization requirements. At the extreme: an operator in an authoritarian regime physically detained until they provide cluster credentials, with no legal recourse and no public visibility.

**Defense.** The substrate's defenses against operator coercion operate through *operator plurality*, *protocol-level neutrality*, and *coercion-resistant operational practices*.

Operator plurality: the substrate's federated architecture means no single operator's compromise affects the substrate as a whole. A user whose preferred operator is coerced can switch to a different operator in a different jurisdiction. Cluster operations continue around the coerced operator; that operator's specific contributions to the cluster's lockstep can be replaced by other cluster members' lockstep activity. The substrate's operational tooling supports rapid operator switching and cluster-membership reconfiguration.

Protocol-level neutrality: the substrate's protocol provides no operator-controlled mechanisms for selective service refusal, surveillance integration, or transaction censorship. An operator coerced to refuse service to specific users can do so only at the application layer (their own connection-acceptance logic), not at the substrate's protocol layer. The user can connect to a different operator and have their transactions processed identically. The protocol's neutrality means that coerced operators cannot be effectively turned into substrate-wide enforcement mechanisms; their coercion affects only their direct users.

Coercion-resistant operational practices: substrate operators can implement *threshold-key-management* practices where multiple operators must collaborate to authorize sensitive operations. An operator under coercion who is asked to produce signing keys can honestly report that the keys are split across multiple operators in multiple jurisdictions, and that no single operator has the ability to comply with the coercion alone. This shifts the coercion to require multi-jurisdictional cooperation, which is dramatically harder to arrange than single-jurisdiction coercion.

Tor and equivalent transports (§17.4) provide an additional layer: substrate clusters operating over Tor can be reached by users without revealing the user's identity to the operator, limiting the user-identification value of any compelled-disclosure order against the operator. The operator's compelled disclosure reveals the existence of substrate traffic but not the identities of users behind it.

**Residual risk.** Three residual risks. First, operator plurality assumes alternative operators exist. In jurisdictions where regulators have driven all substrate operators offshore, users in that jurisdiction must use offshore operators with the legal risk that entails. The substrate's strategic case (§28) depends on offshore operators remaining available — i.e., on no global regulatory consensus shutting down substrate operations entirely. Second, protocol-level neutrality is meaningful only if users actually have operator alternatives accessible. A coerced ISP that blocks access to alternative operators (§27.3) eliminates the user's practical ability to switch even when alternatives technically exist. Third, threshold key management is operational practice, not protocol mandate. Operators who have not implemented threshold practices have single keys subject to single-coercion compromise; the substrate cannot enforce that operators implement these practices.

**Operational response.** A coerced operator may be unable to publicly disclose the coercion (gag orders, NSLs in the U.S., similar mechanisms elsewhere). The substrate's operational community develops *canary statements* — periodic public statements from operators affirming that they have not been coerced. The disappearance of an operator's canary is an indirect signal of coercion that other community members can observe. Coerced operators who are able to publicly disclose coercion may do so, allowing users to switch to alternatives. The substrate's gossip overlay can carry operator-status information so users can respond to coercion signals.

### 27.2 Hardware seizure

**Attack.** Law enforcement or intelligence services physically seize a substrate cluster operator's hardware — servers, persistent storage, hardware security modules, key-management appliances. With the hardware, the adversary obtains: the operator's signing keys (potentially), the cluster's persisted state at the moment of seizure, the operator's logs and operational records, and any user-identification data the operator's application layer collected.

The attack is straightforward to execute against operators in jurisdictions with cooperative law enforcement and inadequate legal protection for operators' infrastructure. Recent precedents include Tornado Cash-related arrests and prosecutions: Alexey Pertsev was detained in the Netherlands in August 2022 and convicted in May 2024 (sentenced to 64 months, currently appealing); Roman Storm was arrested in Washington State in August 2023 and convicted in U.S. federal court in August 2025 on the unlicensed-money-transmission charge with hung-jury outcomes on the more serious charges; Roman Semenov remains at large. Various crypto-exchange seizures and broader cybercrime hardware seizures targeting operators of any contested service round out the precedent landscape.

**Defense.** The substrate's defenses against hardware seizure operate through *operational hygiene practices* and *cryptographic key isolation*.

Operational hygiene: substrate operators are advised to maintain hardware in jurisdictions with stronger legal protections than their primary operational base (Switzerland, Liechtenstein, Singapore for many use cases), to use hardware security modules (HSMs) that resist key extraction even with physical access, to implement hard-disk encryption that requires authentication separate from the hardware itself, and to maintain operational practices (regular key rotation, distributed backups, encrypted logs) that limit the value of seized hardware.

Cryptographic key isolation: an operator's signing keys should be protected by HSMs or hardware tokens that prevent key extraction even with full physical access to the surrounding system. Yubikeys, Trezor devices, and dedicated HSMs (Thales, Utimaco, AWS CloudHSM) provide this protection at varying levels of assurance. An operator whose hardware is seized but whose keys are HSM-protected has lost their operational ability to participate in the cluster but has not surrendered the keys themselves.

Distributed backups: persisted state (§16) replicated across multiple operators in multiple jurisdictions means that hardware seizure at any single operator does not destroy the cluster's data. The substrate's content-addressed history (§10) is verifiable from any honest peer; an operator whose persistence is seized can rejoin the cluster from honest peers' state-sync (planned v0.4) once they recover operational capability.

Threshold-key practices (§27.1) further limit the value of hardware seizure: an operator whose key shares are distributed across multiple jurisdictions cannot sign anything from any single seized HSM. Hardware seizure becomes useless without simultaneous seizure across jurisdictions.

**Residual risk.** Three residual risks. First, HSM protection has limits. Sufficiently sophisticated state-level adversaries (NSA, GCHQ, PLA) may have key-extraction capabilities that commercial HSMs cannot resist; the substrate's operators should not assume HSM protection is absolute against state-level adversaries. Second, distributed-backup operational practices add operational complexity that small operators may not implement. The substrate's defenses depend on operators implementing the practices, not just having them available. Third, hardware seizure may include user-data seizure (application-layer logs, user pubkey-to-identity mappings) that compromises user privacy independent of substrate-protocol defenses.

**Operational response.** Operators whose hardware is seized announce the event publicly (where legally permitted), rotate their keys, and re-establish operations from preserved key shares and distributed backups. The cluster continues operating through other members; the seized operator's loss is bounded by the federation's plurality. Users whose data was on the seized operator's application layer may face privacy compromise; the substrate's operational guidance is that high-value users should diversify their operator relationships and use anonymizing intermediaries to limit single-operator exposure.

### 27.3 Network egress blocking

**Attack.** A state actor or ISP blocks network traffic to substrate cluster nodes from users in their jurisdiction. The blocking may target specific IP addresses (clusters' known addresses), specific protocols (the substrate's TCP framing), or specific traffic patterns (recognized substrate handshake signatures). Users in the blocked jurisdiction cannot reach substrate operators; their substrate use is suppressed without any direct attack on the substrate's protocol or operators.

The attack is well-precedented: China's Great Firewall, Iran's intermittent VPN blocks, Russia's blocks against specific cryptocurrency services. Network egress blocking is a standard tool for jurisdictions seeking to suppress specific online activities without legal action against the activities' providers.

**Defense.** The substrate's defenses against network egress blocking operate through *transport diversity*, *circumvention infrastructure*, and *protocol-flexibility*.

Transport diversity: the substrate's transport layer (§17) is intentionally pluggable. v0.3 supports TCP and in-process mpsc; v0.4 will add QUIC and WebSocket; v1.x targets Tor and other anonymizing transports. Users in jurisdictions blocking specific transports can fall back to alternative transports — TCP-blocked users can use QUIC, port-restricted users can use WebSocket on standard web ports, deeply-restricted users can use Tor. The substrate's protocol is unchanged across transports; only the wire format differs.

Circumvention infrastructure: substrate users can connect through circumvention tools (Tor, Snowflake, Lantern, Outline VPN, mainstream commercial VPNs). The substrate does not require these tools but is designed to work alongside them. Circumvention tools' own resilience to blocking is well-developed; the substrate inherits whatever circumvention robustness the chosen tool provides.

Protocol-flexibility: substrate handshakes and traffic patterns can be designed to resist deep-packet-inspection (DPI) recognition. Pluggable transport designs (Tor's obfs4, meek, Snowflake) demonstrate that protocol traffic can be made hard to distinguish from common web traffic, frustrating signature-based blocking. v1.x substrate work should include obfuscated transport options for high-censorship environments.

The gossip overlay (§11.4) provides additional resilience: a user who can reach any substrate node can verify byte history and submit transactions; the gossip overlay propagates the user's submissions to clusters that may be unreachable directly. Even partial connectivity can support substrate use.

**Residual risk.** Three residual risks. First, the substrate's pluggable-transport infrastructure depends on operators actually deploying alternative transports. If all substrate operators use only TCP, transport diversity is theoretical rather than practical. v0.4 work on QUIC, WebSocket, and Tor transports is required for transport diversity to be meaningful. Second, deep-packet-inspection capabilities continue to improve, with state-level DPI systems increasingly capable of detecting circumvention tools through statistical traffic analysis. The arms race between censorship and circumvention is ongoing; the substrate inherits the current state of the art but cannot guarantee future censorship resistance. Third, complete network isolation (a jurisdiction that blocks all internet access to non-state-approved infrastructure) defeats any substrate-level defense; users in such jurisdictions must use out-of-band channels (satellite internet, manual transfers across borders) that are themselves under attack.

**Operational response.** Substrate operators monitor for blocking patterns affecting their user base and deploy alternative transports to maintain reachability. Users in heavily-restricted jurisdictions develop practices for using circumvention tools effectively (regularly changing entry points, using multiple parallel circumvention paths, maintaining offline backups of substrate-relevant data). The substrate's community shares circumvention guidance through documentation and mutual support.

### 27.4 BGP hijacking

**Attack.** An adversary with access to BGP route announcements (a state-controlled telecom, a compromised ISP, an attacker with route-hijacking capability) announces routes that direct substrate-cluster traffic through adversarial infrastructure. The adversary intercepts substrate traffic, observes communication patterns, potentially modifies traffic in flight (though the substrate's cryptographic signatures detect modification), or simply blackholes traffic to prevent it from reaching its destination.

BGP hijacking has been used at scale: the 2008 YouTube hijack by Pakistan Telecom, multiple Chinese ISPs' BGP attacks, the 2018 Amazon Route 53 hijack to steal cryptocurrency. The attack is technically sophisticated but well-documented; state-level adversaries and well-resourced criminals can execute it.

**Defense.** The substrate's defenses against BGP hijacking operate through *cryptographic verification* (which already protects against in-flight tampering), *transport diversity* (which limits single-route dependence), and *operational monitoring*.

Cryptographic verification: substrate messages are cryptographically signed (§19). An adversary intercepting and modifying messages produces invalid signatures that honest nodes reject. BGP hijacking that aims to inject forged messages fails at the substrate's verification layer; the only effective BGP attack against the substrate is observation (revealing communication patterns) or denial-of-service (blackholing traffic). The substrate's transport-layer encryption (TLS over TCP, native TLS in QUIC) further limits observation value, encrypting message contents even if metadata (source/destination IPs, traffic timing) remains visible.

Transport diversity: a user reaching a substrate cluster through one transport can fall back to another if BGP hijacking affects the first. A cluster operating across multiple data centers in multiple ASNs is harder to hijack comprehensively than a single-data-center cluster. Operational practice should distribute cluster nodes across multiple network providers to limit single-provider hijacking exposure.

Operational monitoring: BGP route changes affecting substrate clusters can be detected by route-monitoring services (BGPmon, Cisco's BGP monitoring, RIPE's RIS service). Substrate operators monitor for unexpected route changes affecting their clusters' IP ranges and respond by switching to backup providers, alerting the community, or temporarily routing through alternative paths until the hijack is addressed.

RPKI (Resource Public Key Infrastructure, RFC 6480 and related specifications) provides cryptographic authorization for BGP route announcements; ASNs implementing RPKI can reject route announcements from unauthorized origins. Substrate operators should encourage their network providers to implement RPKI; this is an internet-wide hardening effort that the substrate benefits from but does not control.

**Residual risk.** Three residual risks. First, RPKI deployment is incomplete globally; many ASNs still accept unsigned route announcements, and BGP hijacking remains effective in regions with low RPKI coverage. The substrate inherits the broader internet's BGP-security state. Second, traffic-pattern observation (visible source/destination IPs, traffic timing) reveals operator and cluster identities even when message contents are encrypted. Adversaries with traffic-analysis capabilities can identify substrate users by their connection patterns to known substrate clusters. Tor and equivalent traffic-anonymization tools mitigate but do not eliminate this exposure. Third, BGP hijacking that simply denies service (blackholing) does not require message modification or observation; the substrate's response to denial-of-service is operational (switch transports, switch providers) rather than cryptographic.

**Operational response.** Substrate operators implement BGP route-monitoring and respond to detected hijacks by working with network providers to filter the bad routes and re-establish correct routing. Persistent BGP attacks against a specific operator may require switching infrastructure providers entirely. The substrate's community shares BGP-monitoring infrastructure and incident-response practices.

### 27.5 The partial nature of physical-attack defense

The substrate's defenses against physical attacks are *partial*. This is the section's most important honest disclosure, and it deserves explicit treatment rather than being scattered across the residual-risk subsections.

Physical attacks bypass the substrate's cryptographic and protocol-level defenses entirely. An adversary who can seize hardware does not need to break ed25519. An adversary who can coerce operators does not need to clear the BFT bound. An adversary who can hijack BGP routes does not need to manipulate the canonical hash sort. The substrate's response to physical attacks is operational resilience, not cryptographic resistance — limiting damage from any individual attack while continuing operations through the unaffected federation.

The substrate's structural choices that limit physical-attack leverage:

**Federated architecture.** No single point of physical compromise affects the substrate as a whole. An operator seized in one jurisdiction is one of many operators; the cluster continues operating through others. A network blocked in one jurisdiction is one of many networks; users connect through alternatives. This is the foundation of substrate physical-attack resilience.

**Geographic distribution.** Operators in multiple jurisdictions provide redundancy against single-jurisdiction enforcement. The substrate's strategic case depends on operators *actually* being distributed — if all operators end up in U.S. jurisdiction (by regulatory pressure, by market consolidation, by network effect), the geographic-distribution defense fails. The substrate's authors actively encourage operator distribution across jurisdictions and specifically across the major jurisdictional clusters (North America, EU, Asia-Pacific, jurisdictions with strong digital-rights protection like Switzerland and Iceland).

**Content-addressed recovery.** A cluster recovering from physical compromise of any subset of its members can rebuild from honest peers' content-addressed state attestations (§16, §23.3 — though state-sync from honest peers is v0.4 work). Hardware seizure does not destroy data because the data is verifiable from any honest peer.

**Protocol-level absence of single-point-of-failure features.** No leader, no foundation, no token, no centralized authority — the substrate's protocol-level absences (named in §24.5 and §25.6) limit the leverage physical attacks can apply. There is no leader to seize, no foundation to raid, no token to confiscate. Physical attacks must target distributed infrastructure to have substrate-wide effect, which is operationally much harder than targeting centralized infrastructure.

**Operational resilience practices.** Threshold key management, distributed backups, transport diversity, BGP monitoring — these operational practices are not protocol-mandated but are widely adopted by substrate operators. The substrate's documentation includes operational guidance for these practices; the substrate's community supports their adoption.

These five structural choices compose into a substrate that is *resilient* against physical attacks rather than *immune* to them. The treatise's discipline is to name the difference. A substrate operator can be coerced, seized, blocked, or hijacked — and the substrate continues operating while that operator recovers (or while users switch to other operators).

The honest disclosure: physical attacks against state-level adversaries are the threat class the substrate's authors expect to escalate fastest. As substrate adoption grows, state-level interest in the substrate grows, and physical-attack capabilities are deployed. The substrate's defenses are designed to make these attacks expensive and operationally bounded, not to make them impossible. Substrate adopters operating in adversarial jurisdictions must understand that they are operating in adversarial jurisdictions, with all the operational risk that entails.

### 27.6 Composition with other categories

Physical attacks compose with other categories' defenses in three ways.

**Composition one: physical attacks frequently accompany legal attacks (§26).** A state-level adversary using legal compulsion to obtain operator data may simultaneously deploy physical-attack capability if legal mechanisms are insufficient. The defenses across §26 and §27 are interdependent; isolated treatment of either category produces incomplete defense. The substrate's jurisdictional-plurality defense operates across both categories simultaneously.

**Composition two: physical attacks frequently complete state-level attacks (§28-class adversaries).** The state-level adversary class (§18.1 class four) uses legal pressure first, social attacks (§25) second, physical attacks third — escalating from less-coercive to more-coercive mechanisms as needed. A treatise that engaged each category in isolation would miss the integrated nature of state-level adversarial capability.

**Composition three: physical-attack defense is the substrate's most operational layer.** Unlike cryptographic defenses (§19) which are mathematically constrained, or protocol invariants (§20) which are auditable, physical-attack defenses depend on operator practices, infrastructure choices, and community vigilance. The substrate's authors maintain the protocol; the substrate's operators maintain the operational defense surface. The treatise's discipline is to name this division of responsibility honestly.

The next section (§28) walks AI-layer attacks. Where §27 engaged threats from state-level adversaries deploying physical-coercive capability, §28 engages threats from AI-mediated incumbents — the strategic adversary class that the treatise has named since §1 as the substrate's primary competition. §28 is the load-bearing strategic engagement that Part III has been building toward; it integrates the technical-threat-model discipline of §19-§27 with the strategic-positioning discipline of §1, §3.5, §6.4, §7, §22.6, and §25.6. By the close of §28, Part III is complete and the substrate's threat model is fully specified.
## §28. AI-layer attacks

The treatise has been honest from §1 onward that the substrate competes against an AI-mediated trajectory. AI companies — Anthropic most concretely, given the treatise's authorship context, but the pattern generalizes to OpenAI, Google DeepMind, and the broader AI-as-financial-infrastructure cohort — are racing to capture the institutional middle's transformation as it consolidates around AI-augmented services. The race is not between AI and the substrate; both use AI capabilities. The race is between AI deployed *as the new institutional middle* and AI deployed *as the substrate's tooling for substrate operations*. The two trajectories are mechanically distinct, lead to different power structures, and produce different futures. §28 engages the attack vectors AI-mediated incumbents can deploy against the substrate to win the first trajectory.

This section walks four attack classes against the four-element rubric (§18.3). The attacks are §28.1 AI-as-issuer capture (AI companies become the dominant cargo issuers, capturing the substrate's freedom-bounded layer), §28.2 AI-as-host capture (AI companies become the dominant cluster operators, controlling substrate access), §28.3 AI-as-validator capture (AI companies' models become the substrate's de-facto verification authority through user trust), §28.4 AI-as-narrative capture (AI-mediated information channels shape user understanding to favor incumbent products over substrate adoption). §28.5 closes by engaging the substrate's most strategically important question — whether the substrate's authors believe the substrate will ship and be adopted before the AI-mediated rent layer consolidates — and naming the conditions under which each trajectory wins.

§28 is the load-bearing strategic engagement that Part III has been building toward. The previous categories — cryptographic, lockstep protocol, cluster topology, issuer, state, economic, social, legal, physical — each engaged a class of adversary the substrate must survive. §28 engages the *integrated* adversary: AI-mediated incumbents who can deploy attacks across multiple categories simultaneously, with structural advantages no other adversary class possesses (AI compute capacity, information-channel control, regulatory access, vendor relationship density, marketing scale). The defenses against each AI-layer attack are weaker than the defenses against any single non-AI attack in its respective category, because AI-mediated incumbents combine multiple attack capabilities that the substrate's single-category defenses were not designed to handle simultaneously.
