By the close of §28, the reader should accept that the substrate's strategic competition is real, that the substrate's defenses against AI-layer attacks are partial, and that the substrate's most important defense is *winning the timing race* — shipping, getting adopted, and acquiring independent constituency before the AI-mediated rent layer consolidates beyond the substrate's ability to displace it.

### 28.1 AI-as-issuer capture

**Attack.** AI companies become the dominant issuers of cargo-bearing bytes in the substrate, leveraging their AI-augmented financial infrastructure to issue USD-bytes, joule-bytes, compute-hour-bytes, and any other cargo class with compelling AI-mediated pricing, customer service, and integration. Once dominant, AI-company issuers can: charge spreads that recreate the institutional middle's rent extraction, condition issuance on AI-company-specific compliance regimes, refuse issuance to users or applications that compete with AI-company services, and use issuance authority as leverage to constrain the substrate's broader development.

The attack profile: AI companies enter the substrate's issuer landscape with structural advantages — superior pricing through AI-augmented operations, superior customer experience through AI-mediated interfaces, superior integration through AI-tooling that other issuers cannot match. The advantage compounds: better experience attracts more users; more users justifies more AI investment; more AI investment widens the experience gap. The substrate's multi-issuer architecture (§22) becomes nominal; in practice, one or two AI-company issuers per cargo class capture most of the volume.

The attack is not adversarial in the conventional sense — AI companies offering better service is not malice. The structural threat is that "better service" leverages AI-company structural advantages to reproduce the institutional middle's monopoly within the substrate's nominal multi-issuer framework, with AI companies becoming the new institutional middle.

**Defense.** The substrate's defenses against AI-as-issuer capture operate through *structural plurality requirements*, *protocol-level absence of issuer-friendly features*, and *competing issuer enablement*.

Structural plurality: the substrate's multi-issuer architecture (§15.1) is protocol-mandated rather than voluntary. Users can choose any issuer's bytes; cluster operators must accept transitions for all registered issuers; the substrate's protocol does not privilege any issuer over any other. AI-company issuers operating with structural advantages compete on those advantages, but they cannot use the substrate's protocol to lock users into their services. Users can switch issuers at any time, with bytes from one issuer functionally equivalent to bytes from another for cargo-class-equivalent purposes.

Protocol-level absence of issuer-friendly features: the substrate has no built-in mechanism for issuer-level user lockup, mandatory KYC integration, compliance-attestation requirements, or any of the features AI-company issuers might use to differentiate themselves from other issuers and attempt to lock users in. AI-company issuers can implement such features at the application layer, but users can route around them by using less-restricted issuers — and the substrate's protocol-level neutrality means routing-around is technically straightforward.

Competing issuer enablement: the substrate's reference implementation, conformance vectors, and treatise are open-source. Anyone can become an issuer for any cargo class; the substrate's authors actively encourage diverse issuance and provide tooling that lowers the operational cost of being an issuer. Cooperative issuers (community solar cooperatives for joule-bytes, agricultural cooperatives for kcal-bytes, credit unions for USD-bytes) can issue bytes alongside AI-company issuers, with users choosing based on values, pricing, or practical considerations.

The substrate's joule-cost transparency (§8.6, §24.1) provides additional defense: AI-company issuers' operations have substantial energy costs that are transparently reportable. Users comparing issuers' joule-cost-per-transition data can identify AI-company issuers as energy-intensive and choose lower-energy alternatives based on their preferences.

**Residual risk.** Three residual risks. First, structural plurality is meaningful only if alternative issuers actually exist and operate competitively. AI-company structural advantages may make non-AI issuers economically unsustainable, with cooperatives and credit unions exiting cargo classes where AI companies dominate. The substrate's protocol allows alternatives; it cannot guarantee alternatives' economic viability. Second, AI-company issuers operating across many cargo classes simultaneously can offer integrated services (a single AI company issuing USD-bytes, joule-bytes, and compute-hour-bytes) that single-cargo competitors cannot match operationally. Cross-cargo integration becomes a soft lock-in even without explicit lock-in mechanisms. Third, regulatory frameworks favoring large institutional issuers (capital requirements, licensing burdens) may exclude smaller competitors from issuance entirely, with regulation effectively privileging AI-company issuers against the cooperative alternatives the substrate would otherwise enable.

**Operational response.** Substrate community development efforts focus on supporting non-AI issuers — providing operational infrastructure, regulatory navigation, and user-acquisition support. Substrate-monitoring services publish issuer-quality and issuer-concentration data, allowing users to make informed choices about issuer diversity. Where regulatory frameworks favor AI-company issuers, substrate community advocacy engages with regulators to argue for proportionate requirements that smaller issuers can meet.

### 28.2 AI-as-host capture

**Attack.** AI companies become the dominant cluster operators in the substrate, leveraging their compute infrastructure, AI-augmented operational capabilities, and customer-acquisition channels to host substrate clusters at scale. Once dominant, AI-company hosts can: charge fees that recreate the institutional middle's rent extraction at the operator layer, condition cluster access on AI-company-specific terms, integrate AI-company services as default substrate access points, and use hosting authority to influence substrate development direction.

The attack profile parallels §28.1: AI companies enter cluster operation with structural advantages (superior infrastructure, superior reliability, superior integration with AI-mediated tooling). Users gravitate to AI-company-hosted clusters because they work better; smaller hosts cannot match the experience; the substrate's federated architecture becomes nominal; in practice, one or two AI-company hosts capture most of the substrate's cluster operations.

**Defense.** The substrate's defenses against AI-as-host capture operate through *protocol-level interoperability*, *operator plurality enablement*, and *user-side switching infrastructure*.

Protocol-level interoperability: the substrate's protocol (Parts II) makes any conforming cluster fully interoperable with any other conforming cluster. A user using an AI-company-hosted cluster can transfer bytes to a user on a smaller community cluster without protocol-level friction. Cross-cluster transfer protocols (§11.3.3, v0.4 work) make this operationally smooth. AI-company hosts cannot leverage their dominance to fragment the substrate; the protocol's interoperability is enforced cryptographically rather than through commercial agreements.

Operator plurality enablement: the substrate's reference implementation is open-source and well-documented. The operational requirements for running a cluster are modest (modest CPU, modest RAM, persistent storage, reliable network — see §11.1's empirical scaling table). The substrate's authors actively support smaller hosts through documentation, deployment tooling, and community infrastructure. Cooperative hosts (university clusters, community-organization clusters, individual-operator clusters) can compete with AI-company hosts on values and trust even when they cannot compete on operational scale.

User-side switching infrastructure: substrate-using applications (wallets, integration tools, bridge services) should support multi-host operation by default, distributing user submissions across multiple hosts simultaneously rather than concentrating on any single host. The substrate's gossip overlay (§11.4) provides the routing infrastructure; user-side tools should be designed to leverage it. A user submitting to multiple hosts simultaneously cannot be locked into any one host's terms.

**Residual risk.** Three residual risks. First, protocol-level interoperability is technical; operational interoperability requires user-side tooling that makes cross-host operation transparent. If user-side tooling is dominated by AI-company-built wallets and integrations, the technical interoperability is theoretical and AI-company hosts maintain practical lock-in. Second, AI companies' compute scale and operational capability may make their hosts' performance dramatically better than smaller hosts'. Users facing latency or reliability differences may not value substrate values enough to accept the operational degradation. Third, AI-company hosts may offer integration with AI-company services (financial AI agents, customer service agents) that smaller hosts cannot match. Bundled services become a soft lock-in even without explicit lock-in mechanisms.

**Operational response.** Substrate community efforts include supporting non-AI hosts (cooperative hosting, community infrastructure, user-friendly deployment tooling) and developing user-side tooling that defaults to multi-host operation. Substrate-monitoring services publish host-quality and host-concentration data. Where AI-company hosts attempt to leverage dominance for lock-in, the substrate's community develops alternative tooling that preserves user choice.

### 28.3 AI-as-validator capture

**Attack.** Users delegate verification of substrate transactions to AI assistants. An AI assistant becomes the user's interface to the substrate — interpreting transitions, validating cargo claims, identifying suspicious activity, recommending transactions. The user's actual verification capability degrades; they trust the AI's judgment because AI-mediated interfaces are more convenient than manual chain verification.

Once user verification is AI-mediated at scale, AI companies' models become the de-facto authority on substrate transaction legitimacy. AI companies can: bias their models' verification toward AI-company-approved issuers and hosts, flag user-favored alternatives as suspicious, refuse to recommend non-AI-company services, and use verification authority as leverage to shape substrate behavior.

The attack profile is subtle: the substrate's protocol-level verification (§10.2) remains correct; the user's chain verification produces the right answer; but the user delegates verification to an AI assistant whose judgment is influenced by the AI company's commercial interests. The substrate's cryptographic guarantees become irrelevant if users do not exercise them directly.

**Defense.** The substrate's defenses against AI-as-validator capture operate through *user-empowerment tooling*, *competing AI verification*, and *protocol-level verifiability transparency*.

User-empowerment tooling: substrate verification is computationally cheap (§10.2 walks the procedure in five steps; a typical byte's full chain verification takes milliseconds). Substrate-using applications should default to performing verification client-side rather than delegating to remote services. Users see verification results directly; they understand what verification means and what it doesn't mean. The substrate's community develops verification tooling that prioritizes user understanding over AI-mediated convenience.

Competing AI verification: open-source AI models (Llama, Mistral, others) can perform substrate verification independently of AI-company-controlled models. Substrate-using applications can offer verification through user-chosen AI assistants, with transparent reporting of which AI made each verification decision. Users concerned about AI bias can choose verification through AI models they trust or perform verification manually.

Protocol-level verifiability transparency: every substrate verification reduces to a deterministic computation over the byte's content-addressed chain. There is no judgment involved; the verification either succeeds or fails based on cryptographic correctness alone. AI assistants performing verification are not making judgments; they are running the verification procedure. The substrate's transparency means that AI-mediated verification is auditable: an AI claiming a transaction is valid can be checked against the underlying cryptographic verification, and disagreement is detectable.

**Residual risk.** Three residual risks. First, user-empowerment tooling depends on users actually using it. If AI-mediated interfaces are dramatically more convenient, users will use them and accept whatever verification their chosen AI provides. The substrate cannot force users to verify directly; it can only make direct verification possible. Second, competing AI verification depends on alternative AI models being competent for substrate-specific verification tasks. If AI companies invest heavily in substrate-verification capabilities and open-source alternatives lag, AI-company verification dominates by capability rather than by user choice. Third, AI-mediated decisions about non-protocol-level issues (which cargo to accept, which issuers to trust, which hosts to use) involve judgment that the substrate's transparency does not constrain. AI assistants advising users on these decisions can shape user behavior in ways that protocol-level verification correctness does not address.

**Operational response.** Substrate community efforts include developing verification tooling that prioritizes user understanding, supporting open-source AI verification capabilities, and educating users about the difference between protocol-level verification (cryptographic, judgment-free) and judgment-laden decisions (cargo selection, issuer choice, host choice). Substrate-using applications transparently report what verification was performed and by which model.

### 28.4 AI-as-narrative capture

**Attack.** AI-mediated information channels shape user understanding of the substrate. Search results, AI-assistant responses to substrate-related questions, AI-summarized news about the substrate's adoption — all are influenced by the AI providers' editorial choices. AI providers can: emphasize substrate alternatives in their responses, frame substrate adoption as risky or fringe, prioritize AI-company-friendly substrate variants, and shape long-term user perception in ways that cumulatively favor incumbent products over substrate adoption.

The attack is not necessarily deliberate; AI companies' models reflect their training data, their RLHF preferences, and their commercial context. An AI assistant trained primarily on content reflecting the institutional middle's perspective will naturally produce responses framed by that perspective, even without explicit anti-substrate intent. The cumulative effect over millions of user interactions is substantial narrative shaping.

The attack profile: a user asks an AI assistant about smart byte substrate adoption. The assistant's response reflects its training: substrate adoption is "complex" and "controversial"; AI-company alternatives are "established" and "trusted"; risks are emphasized; benefits are downplayed. The user concludes that substrate adoption is not worth the effort. Multiplied across millions of users, this is decisive market influence with no overt manipulation.

**Defense.** The substrate's defenses against AI-as-narrative capture operate through *clear authoritative documentation*, *open-source AI alternatives*, and *substrate-quality evidence accumulation*.

Clear authoritative documentation: this treatise itself is the substrate's primary defense against narrative capture. AI assistants trained on the treatise will encounter the substrate's actual claims, defenses, and tradeoffs in the substrate's own voice. The treatise's discipline of explicit acknowledgment (partial freedoms, v0.4 work, structural costs) makes it harder for AI assistants to characterize the substrate as overpromising; the substrate's own honesty about its limits is a defense against caricatures of those limits.

Open-source AI alternatives: AI assistants based on open-source models (Llama, Mistral, Qwen, others) can be trained or fine-tuned on substrate-favorable data without the commercial constraints AI-company models face. Substrate-using applications can offer AI assistance through user-chosen models; users concerned about narrative bias can use models they trust.

Substrate-quality evidence accumulation: as the substrate is adopted, real-world evidence accumulates — successful issuers, healthy clusters, satisfied users, audited operations. The evidence is publicly verifiable through the substrate's transparency; AI assistants summarizing the evidence cannot easily distort it without producing summaries that contradict the underlying data. Substrate's transparency creates an information environment that resists narrative capture: claims about substrate behavior can be checked against substrate state directly.

**Residual risk.** Three residual risks. First, clear documentation depends on AI training data reaching the documentation. If AI providers selectively exclude substrate documentation from their training corpora (or deprioritize it in fine-tuning), the substrate's documentation does not influence AI-mediated narratives. The substrate cannot force AI providers to include its documentation. Second, open-source AI alternatives depend on the broader open-source AI ecosystem maintaining capability competitive with AI-company models. If AI-company models substantially outperform open-source alternatives, users gravitate to AI-company models for capability reasons, exposing themselves to whatever narrative bias those models contain. Third, narrative capture operates over long time scales — months and years rather than minutes. By the time substrate adopters notice that AI-mediated narratives are systematically biased against the substrate, significant adoption damage may have already occurred.

**Operational response.** Substrate community efforts include maintaining authoritative documentation that is unambiguously the substrate's own voice, supporting open-source AI ecosystems, and educating users about the difference between AI-mediated information and substrate-direct information. Substrate-using applications offer direct access to substrate state and documentation, allowing users to bypass AI-mediated framing when they choose.

### 28.5 The strategic timing question

Part III has engaged the substrate's threat model across ten attack categories. Cryptographic, lockstep protocol, cluster topology, issuer, state, economic, social, legal, physical, AI-layer. Each category has been engaged against the four-element rubric (§18.3); each has named defenses, residual risks, and operational responses; each has been honest about the partial nature of those defenses.

The substrate's most strategically important question — engaged here at the close of Part III rather than scattered across earlier sections — is whether the substrate's defenses, taken together, are *sufficient given the timing*. The substrate's defenses against any single attack class are reasonably robust. The substrate's defenses against the *integrated state-level + AI-mediated* adversary that combines attacks across multiple categories simultaneously are weaker. The substrate's defenses against an integrated adversary deploying their attacks *before the substrate has acquired independent constituency* are weakest of all.

The treatise's strategic claim, named honestly: the substrate must ship and acquire adoption before the AI-mediated rent layer consolidates beyond the substrate's ability to displace it. This is the timing race. The race is winnable but not won. The race's outcome depends on conditions the substrate's authors only partially control.

The pace of consolidation is rapid. By 2026, AI-agent financial infrastructure is moving from pilots into production: stablecoin transaction volume reached approximately $33 trillion in 2025 (up roughly 72% year over year), with stablecoin supply surpassing $300 billion and projected to reach approximately $420 billion in 2026 with agentic payments named as a primary growth driver; Coinbase's x402 protocol (open-sourced 2025) enables instant programmable payments over HTTP for AI agents; the ERC-8004 standard for verifiable on-chain AI agent identity deployed on BNB Chain in February 2026; market analysts (MarketsandMarkets) project the AI-agents market growing from approximately $7.84 billion in 2025 to approximately $52.62 billion by 2030 at 46.3% CAGR. The infrastructure for AI-mediated financial transactions is being built now, on the institutional middle's terms, with stablecoins as the cargo and AI companies as the issuance and routing layer. The substrate's window for offering an alternative is bounded by how fast that infrastructure consolidates — measured in months and quarters, not years and decades.

**Conditions under which the substrate wins:**

- The substrate ships v1.0 with a working reference implementation and publishes the treatise (this document) before AI-company financial-services products reach broad adoption.
- Multiple competing substrate implementations emerge in the year following v1.0, demonstrating implementation diversity and preventing single-implementation monoculture.
- Multiple jurisdictions develop favorable regulatory frameworks for the substrate's protocol-not-service framing, creating jurisdictions where substrate operation is unambiguously legal.
- Cooperative issuers (credit unions for USD-bytes, community solar cooperatives for joule-bytes, agricultural cooperatives for kcal-bytes) launch substrate-issuance offerings that compete with AI-company issuers on values and trust.
- User-side tooling (wallets, integrations, browsers with substrate support) reaches usability parity with AI-company alternatives.
- Substrate adoption acquires enough scale that the substrate has its own constituency — users, operators, issuers, developers — independent of any single jurisdiction's regulatory permission or any single AI company's tolerance.

**Conditions under which the substrate loses:**

- AI companies achieve regulatory and market dominance in financial AI services before the substrate ships, with AI-mediated financial agents becoming the default rather than substrate-mediated direct transactions.
- Regulatory frameworks consolidate globally to require AI-mediated compliance for any financial transaction above trivial thresholds, effectively forbidding substrate operation outside AI-company-mediated channels.
- The substrate's reference implementation has a critical security failure that damages reputation before alternative implementations emerge.
- The substrate's community fragments before achieving independent constituency, with multiple substrate variants competing for the same niche and none achieving critical mass.
- AI-mediated narratives shape user understanding so thoroughly that substrate adoption never reaches the threshold where independent constituency is possible.
- Physical and legal attacks on substrate operators succeed in jurisdictionally consolidating substrate operation into AI-company-friendly hands before independent infrastructure is established.

The substrate's authors' honest assessment, named explicitly: the conditions under which the substrate wins are achievable but require substantial work that has not yet been done. The conditions under which the substrate loses are paths the substrate is currently on if no countervailing work is done. Neither outcome is determined; both are achievable.

### 28.6 The substrate's most important defense

The substrate's most important defense, across all categories engaged in Part III, is *winning the timing race*. Cryptographic primitives are conservative for decades. Protocol invariants are auditable indefinitely. Cluster topology defenses are sufficient for sophisticated single-category adversaries. Issuer-layer defenses limit institutional-middle re-entry. Economic, social, legal, physical defenses are layered and partial. AI-layer defenses are weakest. But the substrate's *strategic survival* does not depend on any single category's defenses; it depends on the substrate acquiring independent constituency before the AI-mediated rent layer consolidates.

Independent constituency means: enough users to make substrate adoption matter politically; enough issuers to make multi-issuer architecture practically meaningful; enough operators to make federation distribution real; enough developers to maintain the substrate's reference implementation and conformance vectors; enough public visibility to make narrative capture costly; enough regulatory engagement to establish substrate-favorable frameworks in major jurisdictions. Independent constituency is what protects the substrate from being absorbed, captured, or marginalized by AI-mediated incumbents.

Building independent constituency is work the substrate's authors do partially and the substrate's community does mostly. The treatise commits the substrate's authors to: maintaining the reference implementation, maintaining the conformance vectors, publishing the treatise and its updates, supporting community development efforts, and engaging publicly with substrate adopters and critics. The community's commitment is to: developing alternative implementations, operating independent clusters, launching cooperative issuance, building user-side tooling, advocating with regulators, and educating users.

The honest disclosure that closes Part III: the substrate's threat model has been engaged comprehensively, but the substrate's strategic survival is not primarily a threat-model question. It is an adoption-and-timing question, and the answer is to be determined by work done after the treatise's publication. The treatise specifies what the substrate is and what it must survive; the work of making the substrate actually win is ongoing.

### 28.7 Composition with all other categories: Part III closes

The four AI-layer attacks engaged in §28 compose with all other Part III categories simultaneously. AI-as-issuer capture (§28.1) is an extreme of issuer attacks (§22) operating with structural advantages. AI-as-host capture (§28.2) is an extreme of cluster topology attacks (§21) deployed by adversaries with operational scale. AI-as-validator capture (§28.3) is a social attack (§25) operating through delegated verification rather than direct compromise. AI-as-narrative capture (§28.4) is a social attack (§25) operating through information-channel control rather than identity capture. The cryptographic, protocol, state, economic, legal, and physical categories all provide layered defenses that AI-mediated incumbents must overcome, but the integration of those defenses across categories is what determines whether AI-layer attacks succeed.

Part III is now complete. Eleven sections. Ten attack categories engaged with the four-element rubric. Specific defenses named, specific residual risks acknowledged, specific operational responses specified. The substrate's threat model is the most comprehensive treatment of distributed-systems threat models that the treatise's authors have produced; it is also necessarily incomplete because real adversaries discover attacks the treatise does not anticipate.

Part IV walks the workarounds — the alternative architectures critics propose instead of the substrate. Each is treated seriously; each is shown to fail at scale or against the adversaries Part III engaged. Part IV's discipline is to engage the workarounds as they are actually proposed rather than as straw versions of those proposals. The result is the substrate's case as the *correct* architecture rather than just *one* architecture among many — the case that completes Part I's establishment of the substrate's necessity with the demonstration that no proposed alternative survives the engagement Part III specified.

By the close of Part IV, the substrate's case as both *necessary* (Part I) and *correct* (Parts II-III + Part IV's failure of alternatives) will be established. Parts V-VII engage the retrofits, distribution implications, and closing argument that complete the treatise.
